First Argument: Multiplicity of Directions Implies Division
Author introduces the principle of direction multiplicity and presents the first argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the existence of an indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'), which shows that the multiplicity of directions implies division, leading to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If something has multiple sides, it must be divisible.

The author begins by stating that all proofs against the indivisible part reduce to a few principles. He then presents the first argument: if an indivisible atom existed, it would have multiple directions (right, left, etc.), which would necessitate its division—contradicting its indivisibility. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's style: he first introduces the general principle, then gives the specific argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the structure
The passage has two parts: (1) a general statement about the proofs reducing to principles, and (2) the first argument based on direction multiplicity. Pay attention to the conditional statement 'if the part existed...' and its consequences.
3
Understand the contradiction
The argument assumes an indivisible part, then deduces that it has multiple sides (right, left, etc.). Since these sides are distinct, the part would be divisible. This contradicts the original assumption, making it absurd (muhal).
This is a proof by contradiction (burhan al-tamanu').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد أن أدلة نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ على كثرتها ترجع إلى عدة أصول، يتفرع على كل منها وجوه من الاستدلال فجعلت بمنزلة الطرق، وأشير في عنوان كل منها إلى وجه الضعف، ومورد المنع ، فمنها ما يبتنى على أن تعدد جهات الشيء ونهاياته تستلزم الانقسام في ذاته، وهي وجوه : الأول : أنه لو وجد الجزء أي الجوهر المتميز الذي لا انقسام فيه أصلاً لتعددت جهاته ضرورة فتتعدد جوانبه وأطرافه، لأن ما منه إلى اليمين غير ما منه إلى اليسار وكذا الفوق والتحت والقدام والخلف فيلزم انقسامه على تقدير عدم انقسامه محال .
He intends that the proofs of negating the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'), despite their abundance, reduce to several principles (usul), from each of which branch out aspects (wujuh) of inference; so they are made like the ways, and in the title of each is indicated the point of weakness and the place of rejection. Among them are those based on the multiplicity of directions (jihaat) and extremities (nihayaat) of a thing entailing division in its essence, and they are aspects: The first: that if the part—i.e., the distinguished substance (al-jawhar al-mutamayyiz) which has no division at all—existed, its directions would necessarily multiply, so its sides (jawanib) and extremities (atraaf) would multiply, because what is from it to the right is other than what is from it to the left, and likewise the above and below, front and back; so its division follows, while assuming its non-division is absurd (muhal).
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of proofs for the negation of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'). The author states that these proofs, despite their abundance, reduce to several principles (usul), from each of which various arguments (wujuh) branch out. He indicates in the title of each the point of weakness and point of rejection. Among them are those based on the idea that multiplicity of directions (jihaat) and extremities (nihayaat) of a thing entail division in its essence. Then he presents the first argument: If the part (i.e., the indivisible substance) existed, it would necessarily have multiple directions, thus its sides (jawanib) and ends (atraaf) would be multiple, because that which is to its right is not that which is to its left, and likewise up and down, front and back. So its division follows, while assuming its indivisibility is absurd (muhal). So the argument shows a contradiction from the assumption of an indivisible part.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
الأصول
الوجوه
الجهات
الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes division to prove division.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it assumes an indivisible part, then deduces that it must have distinct sides (which are not assumed to be parts initially), and from those distinct sides it infers division. The distinct sides are necessary consequences of the assumption of existence in space, not presupposed division.

Checkpoint
What does the first argument conclude from the assumption of an indivisible part?
A) It proves the part's existence.
B) It shows the part must have no directions.
C) It leads to a contradiction because the part would have multiple directions but cannot divide.
D) It shows the part is divisible into smaller parts.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against the indivisible atom shows that the assumption of an indivisible substance leads to a logical contradiction because it would have multiple sides, implying division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Second Argument: Adjoining Parts Implies Division
Second argument: two-part conjunction disjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational argument against the indivisible part based on how parts adjoin.

Core Idea
Adjoining parts must either meet entirely or partially; both lead to contradictions.

The second argument presents a simple yet powerful disjunction. When two supposedly indivisible parts adjoin, either they make contact entirely (so no volume increase) or they meet only partially (which implies division). Both outcomes contradict the notion of an indivisible atom. This lesson unpacks the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the two branches.
2
Understand the first branch
Consider the possibility of entirely meeting parts. Ask: does this allow a body to form? The conclusion says no.
3
Understand the second branch
Partial meeting yields two ends, which means the part is divisible.
4
Reflect on the implication
The argument shows the indivisible atom leads to absurdity, so it cannot exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه إذا انضم جزء إلى جزء، فإما أن يلاقيه بالكلية بحيث لا يزيد حيز الجزئين على حيز الواحد فيلزم أن لا يحصل من انضمام الأجزاء حجم ومقدار، فلا يحصل جسم أولاً بالكلية بل بشيء دون شيء فيكون له طرفان وهو معنى الانقسام.
The second: that when a part adjoins to a part, either it meets it entirely so that the space of the two parts does not exceed the space of one, resulting in no volume and magnitude from the joining of parts, so no body is produced; or it meets it not entirely but with something without something, so it has two ends, and that is the meaning of division.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the indivisible part. It considers two parts joining together. Either they meet entirely, such that the combined space is no larger than a single part, which would mean no increase in size and thus no body (jism) can be formed from multiple parts; or they do not meet entirely, meeting only partially, so that the assembly has two ends (tarafan), which is the very meaning of division. So the assumption of an indivisible part leads either to the impossibility of forming bodies or to the part itself being divisible.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument only applies to physical atoms.
Correction: It is a logical argument about any indivisible unit, not just physical atoms.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what happens if two parts meet entirely?
A) They produce a larger body.
B) Their combined space equals that of one part, so no body is produced.
C) They must have two ends.
D) They become divisible.

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that adjoining indivisible parts leads either to no body formation or to division, thus disproving the indivisible atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument reveals that assumptions can lead to absurd contradictions. Have you ever held a belief that you never fully examined? Take a moment to identify one such belief and trace its logical consequences—are you willing to follow the reasoning wherever it leads, even if it forces you to abandon the belief?
?
The mutakallimun used logic to break down the nature of matter. When it comes to your own character, what is an 'indivisible part' you have assumed is unchangeable? What would happen if you allowed that part to be examined and, if needed, transformed?

Third Argument: Three Parts in Contact Implies Division
Third argument shows that when three parts touch in order, the middle either divides or no volume forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational argument against atomism: how the contact of three parts forces the divisibility of the middle part or the impossibility of volume.

Core Idea
Three parts in order force the middle to divide or volume disappears.

This lesson presents the third argument from the classical kalam tradition against indivisible atoms. The argument is a logical demonstration: if three parts are arranged in order, the middle part must either prevent the outer two from touching (implying it has distinct faces and thus divides) or not prevent them (leading to no volume at all). Either way, the assumption of indivisible parts is contradicted. We will walk through each scenario and understand its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the two alternatives introduced by 'إما أن يمنع' and 'وإما أن لا يمنعهما'.
The Arabic uses the dual form 'الوسطاني' for the middle part.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Focus on the conditional: the middle either prevents the outer parts from touching or it does not. Understand each branch.
In the first branch, the middle must have two different faces; in the second, it contributes nothing to volume.
3
Visualize the reasoning
Draw three circles in a line. In the first scenario, label the left and right faces of the middle circle as distinct. In the second, show the outer circles touching each other past the middle.
This makes the logical force tangible.
4
Connect to the overall argument
Consider how this argument directly refutes the existence of indivisible atoms (juz' la yatajazzā). Either the atom must divide (contradicting indivisibility) or no volume can be built from them.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إذا تماست ثلاثة أجزاء على الترتيب بأن يكون واحد منها بين اثنين، فالوسطاني إما أن يمنع الآخرين عن التلاقي والتماس، فيكون وجهه الذي يلاقي أحدهما غير الذي يلاقي الآخر، فينقسم . وإما أن لا يمنعهما فلا يحصل من اجتماع الجزئين حجم ومقدار وهكذا في الثالث والرابع ، فلا يحصل الحجم.
The third: if three parts touch in order, such that one of them is between two, then the middle one either prevents the other two from meeting and touching, so its face that meets one is other than that which meets the other, so it divides; or it does not prevent them, so no volume and magnitude result from the meeting of the two parts, and likewise in the third and fourth, so no volume results.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument. Consider three parts arranged in order with one between two others. The middle part either prevents the outer two from contacting each other, in which case the face of the middle part that touches one is different from the face that touches the other, implying that the middle part has at least two distinct parts and thus is divisible. Or the middle part does not prevent them from meeting, meaning they contact directly, so the middle part does not contribute to volume, and then the same reasoning applies to subsequent parts, leading to no volume at all. Either way, the assumption of indivisible parts fails.

Key Terms

الوسطاني
منع
تلاقي
وجه
انقسام
حجم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument depends on actual physical contact between atoms.
Correction: The argument is a conceptual/logical demonstration using contact as a thought experiment; it does not require atoms to actually touch in reality.

Checkpoint
In the third argument, what does it mean if the middle part prevents the two outer parts from touching?
A) The middle part must have two different faces and thus divides.
B) The outer parts touch each other through the middle.
C) No volume is produced.
D) The middle part is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that indivisible parts cannot compose a volume because any middle part either divides or becomes redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that if a part is between two others, it either divides or becomes irrelevant. In your own life, when you stand between two conflicting truths or demands, do you try to have two faces to please both, or do you risk becoming useless by avoiding the conflict? Which path leads to a real, integrated self?
?
If indivisible atoms cannot form a volume, what does that say about the supposedly indivisible 'self' you cling to? Are you willing to face the possibility that your soul is not a simple, indivisible essence but something that can be divided by sin, repentance, and growth?
?
The argument forces you to choose: either the middle part divides or volume disappears. Likewise, when you are in the middle of two contradictory impulses—like faith and doubt—do you allow yourself to be split, or do you let one side fully occupy you so that the other has no place? Which choice actually builds your character?

Sunlight on an Atom: The Fourth Argument
Fourth argument uses sunlight on a surface of indivisible atoms to show different illumination implies division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth argument uses the scenario of sunlight on a surface of indivisible atoms to demonstrate that the atoms must be divisible.

Core Idea
Sunlight on an atom reveals its sides, forcing division.

This lesson presents the fourth argument against indivisible atoms. It uses a simple thought experiment: imagine a surface made of atoms that cannot be split. When sunlight falls on it, one side becomes illuminated while the other remains dark. This contrast shows that the atom has distinct sides, meaning it is divisible after all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise logical structure: a hypothesis, a condition, an observation, and a conclusion.
The passage is a single sentence; break it into clauses.
2
Visualize the thought experiment
Imagine a flat surface (safha) made of extremely small parts that are assumed to be indivisible. It has length and width. Sunlight shines on it. What do you observe?
Focus on the face that receives light versus the opposite face.
3
Identify the implication
The illuminated face is different from the non-illuminated one. For this difference to exist, the atom must have two distinct sides, meaning it is divisible. This contradicts the assumption of indivisibility.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Connect to the larger debate
Understand that this argument is part of a series of proofs against atomism. It shows that even a simple physical scenario requires parts and division.
Atomists claimed atoms have no parts, but this thought experiment shows they must.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أنا نفرض صفحة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ بحيث يكون له الطول والعرض فإذا أشرقت عليها الشمس فبالضرورة يكون وجهها المقابل للشمس المضيء بها غير الوجه الآخر فينقسم.
The fourth: that we suppose a surface (safha) of parts that do not divide, such that it has length and width; if the sun shines upon it, then necessarily its face facing the sun, illuminated by it, is other than the other face, so it divides.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument. It asks us to imagine a surface (safha) composed of indivisible parts, having length and width. When sunlight shines on this surface, one side of each part facing the sun is illuminated, while the opposite side remains unlit. So the face that receives light is distinct from the other face. For this to be possible, the atom must have two distinct sides, and thus must be divisible. Therefore, the assumption that the atoms are indivisible is contradicted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this argument depends on the physical existence of sunlight or assumes actual division of light.
Correction: It is a geometrical thought experiment. The key point is that even conceptually, the atom must have two distinct faces, which implies it has parts and is therefore divisible.

Checkpoint

A) The entire surface becomes equally illuminated.
B) The part facing the sun is different from the other side, implying division.
C) The parts become invisible.
D) The surface has no length or width.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses sunlight to show that even indivisible atoms must have distinct sides, proving they are divisible.

Fifth Argument: The Indivisible Part at a Meeting Point
Geometrical proof that a part at a meeting point forces division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth rational argument that if an indivisible part is placed at the meeting point of two other parts, it must divide, and you will grasp the four geometrical demonstrations used to prove this entailment.

Core Idea
An indivisible part at a meeting point must divide, else contact would be impossible.

Having established earlier arguments against atomism, the fifth argument tackles a specific geometrical scenario: placing an indivisible part at the junction of two others. The author explains why contact forces partial contact, and then provides four 'ways' (wujuh) to illustrate the logical necessity of division. These are not physical experiments but rational geometrical proofs that expose the contradiction in atomism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the entailment statement
Read the first part of the passage that states the claim and why contact must be partial. Recognise that if the part touched one base entirely, it would sit on that base, not on the junction.
The key word is 'ملتقى' (meeting point) and 'تماس' (contact).
2
Examine the four ways (wujuh)
Go through each of the four numbered demonstrations. They are: (1) a simple supposition with an objection, (2) motion of a part across the junction, (3) a line of four parts with upper/lower moving equally, (4) a line of five parts with two moving to meet. Notice how each shows the junction as a distinct locus.
Way (1) is noted to have a hidden objection, but the text doesn't elaborate.
3
Reflect on the rational nature of these arguments
These are not empirical experiments but rational geometrical proofs. They show that an indivisible part cannot occupy a meeting point without thereby having distinct sides, implying division.
This connects to the broader kalam method of using pure reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: أنه إذا وقع جزء لا يتجزأ على ملتقى جزءين آخرين لزم انقسام الثلاثة . أما الملازمة فلأن التماس بينه وبين كل منهما إنما يكون بالبعض أي يكون شيء منه مماساً لشيء من هذا، وشيء آخر مماساً لشيء من ذاك، إذ لو ماس أحدهما بالكلية لكان عليه لا على الملتقى، وأما بيان حقيقة اللزوم فبوجوه : (1) أن نفرض الجزء على الملتقى وفيه مناقشة لا تخفى . (2) أن يتحرك من جزء إلى جزء فاتصافه بالحركة إنما يكون عند كونه على الملتقى لا على الأول، إذ لم تبتدأ الحركة ولا على الثاني إذ قد انقطعت . (3) أن نفرض خطاً من أربعة أجزاء فوق الأول جزء وتحت الرابع جزء ثم نفرض مرور الفوقاني والتحتاني على الخط بحركة على السواء مع اتفاق في الابتداء ، أي تكون الحركتان على حد واحد من السرعة والبطء ويكون ابتداؤهما معاً، فبالضرورة تتحاذيان على ملتقى الثاني والثالث. أي حيث يكون الفوقاني فوق الملتقى والتحتاني تحته . (4) أن نفرض خطاً من خمسة أجزاء فوق الأول جزء، وفوق الخامس جزء، ثم أخذا معاً في حركة على السواء إلى حد الالتقاء، فبالضرورة يكون ذلك في وسط الخط أعني الجزء الثالث، فيكون هو على ملتقاهما من تحت، ولا يخفى أن هذه
The fifth: that if an indivisible part falls on the meeting point (multaqa) of two other parts, then the division of all three follows. As for the entailment, because the contact between it and each of them is only by a part—i.e., something of it is tangent to something of this, and another thing tangent to something of that—since if it touched one of them entirely, it would be upon it, not upon the meeting point. And as for the demonstration of the reality of the entailment, it is through aspects: (1) that we suppose the part on the meeting point, and there is in it an objection not hidden. (2) that it moves from part to part, so its description with motion is only when it is on the meeting point, not on the first, since motion did not begin, nor on the second, since it has ceased. (3) that we suppose a line of four parts, above the first part a part and below the fourth a part, then we suppose the passage of the upper and lower along the line with equal motion with agreement in the beginning, i.e., the two motions are on one measure of speed and slowness and their beginnings are together, so necessarily they align on the meeting point of the second and third, i.e., where the upper is above the junction and the lower is below it. (4) that we suppose a line of five parts, above the first part a part, and above the fifth a part, then they both take together in equal motion to the point of meeting, so necessarily that is in the middle of the line, i.e., the third part, so it is upon their meeting point from below, and it is not hidden that these...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, which is the most elaborate. It states that if an indivisible part falls on the meeting point (multaqa) of two other parts, then all three parts must divide. First, it explains the entailment: the contact between the falling part and each of the two base parts must be partial; otherwise, the falling part would lie entirely on one base part, not on the junction. Then it provides four ways (wujuh) to demonstrate the necessity of division: (1) Simply supposing the part on the junction, but notes there is an objection. (2) If the part moves from one base part to another, its motion occurs only when it is at the junction, not when it is on either base part, implying it has a distinct state at the junction. (3) Imagine a line of four parts with an upper part above the first and a lower part below the fourth; if both upper and lower move equally towards each other, they will align at the junction of the second and third parts, showing that the part at the junction has a specific position. (4) Imagine a line of five parts with a part above the first and a part above the fifth; both move equally towards each other, they meet at the third part, confirming the junction as a locus. The argument cuts off, but the gist is that these scenarios show the indivisible part must have distinct parts to be at the junction, thus it divides.

Key Terms
ملتقى
تماس
بالبعض
حركة
خط
فوقاني
تحتاني
التقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these are actual physical experiments that could be performed.
Correction: They are rational geometrical arguments meant to show logical contradiction in the concept of indivisible parts, not empirical tests.

Checkpoint
In the fifth argument, what does the second way (wajh) demonstrate about a part moving from one part to another?
A) The part moves continuously without stopping.
B) Motion is only attributed to the part when it is at the junction.
C) The part cannot move at all.
D) The part divides during motion.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument uses geometrical scenarios to prove that an indivisible part at a meeting point must have distinct sides, contradicting its indivisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recurring conflict in your life—a 'meeting point' of two opposite concerns (e.g., career and family). You often treat it as a single, indivisible problem. In light of this lesson, what are the distinct 'parts' of that conflict that you must address separately? This week, will you take the risk of addressing one part without solving it entirely, trusting that partial contact is necessary?
?
The argument shows that what appears indivisible actually has distinguishable sides. Consider a deeply ingrained habit you believe you cannot change because it feels like one 'unit' of behavior. Pause and identify: what are the smaller components (triggers, actions, consequences) that make up this habit? Which one will you intentionally modify tomorrow, demonstrating that the 'indivisible' can be divided?

The First Geometric Proof: Bisection of a Line
Taftazani presents the first geometric proof: bisection of a line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of bisecting any line leads to a contradiction if matter were composed of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Every line can be bisected, but atoms cannot be divided.

In his refutation of atomism, al-Taftazani begins with a simple geometric observation: any line can be split into two equal halves. This seems trivial, but if the line were made of indivisible atoms, bisection would require cutting an atom—an impossibility. This lesson unpacks the first geometric argument against the indivisible atom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the logical structure: a premise, a conditional statement, and a conclusion.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the premise
The premise is that every line can be bisected. Why is this considered self-evident? It is a geometric axiom.
3
Grasp the contradiction
If matter is atomic, a line composed of atoms cannot be bisected without splitting an atom. But geometry demands that every line is bisectable, leading to a contradiction.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider what this says about the nature of physical extension: it must be continuous, not discrete.
This proof is foundational for later arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة هندسية على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
قال (ومنها)
ما يتعلق بأصول هندسية مبنية على انتفاء الجزء وهي وجوه : الأول : كل خط يمكن تنصيفه ففي المركب من الأجزاء الوتر يلزم تجزيء الوسطاني.
الثاني : كل خط يمكن أن يعمل عليه مثلث متساوي الأضلاع ولا يتصور في المركب من جزئين إلا بوقوع جزء على ملتقى الجزئين.
الثالث : كل زاوية مستقيمة الخطين تنقسم لا إلى نهاية .
الرابع : إذا ثبت أحد طرفي الخط المستقيم وأدير حتى عاد إلى وضعه الأول حصلت الدائرة، ثم إذا أدير نصفها على قطرها الثابت حصلت الكرة، ووجود الجزء ينفيهما لأنا لو فرضنا محيط الدائرة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فإما أن يكون ظواهر الأجزاء كبواطنها، فيلزم تساوي ظاهر المحيط وباطنه أو أكثر فيلزم الانقسام، أو بين الظواهر فرج خلاء لا يسع كلها منها جزءاً فيلزم الانقسام، أو يسع فيكون الظاهر ضعف الباطن، ولأن المدار الذي بلاصق المنطقة، إما أن يكون بإزاء كل جزء منها جزء منه فيتساويان، أو أقل فينقسم .
Geometric proofs for negating the indivisible part. He said: And among them are what pertain to geometric principles based on the negation of the part. They are aspects: First: Every line can be bisected, so in that composed of parts, the bisection of the middle [line] is necessary. Second: Every line can have an equilateral triangle constructed on it, and it is inconceivable in that composed of two parts except by a part falling at the junction of the two parts. Third: Every angle with straight lines can be divided infinitely. Fourth: If one end of a straight line is fixed and it is rotated until it returns to its original position, a circle is obtained; then if its half is rotated on its fixed diameter, a sphere is obtained. The existence of the part negates both, because if we suppose the circumference of the circle to be composed of indivisible parts, then either the outer parts are like the inner parts, so the outer part of the circumference and the inner part would be equal — or more, so division follows; or there is between the outer parts a void space that does not contain from them a part — so division follows; or it contains [a part], so the outer part would be double the inner part. And because the circle that is tangent to the zone, either it is opposite every part of it a part of it, so they are equal, or less, so it divides.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a set of four geometric proofs (adilla handasiyya) against the existence of the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazza'). The author states that these are based on geometric principles that presuppose the negation of the atom. The first proof: every line can be bisected; if the line were composed of atoms, then at the point of bisection, the line would have to be divided, implying that the atom itself is divisible, which is contradictory. The second proof: an equilateral triangle can be constructed on any line; if the line is composed of two atoms, the apex of the triangle would have to fall on the junction of the two atoms, implying that the apex is an atom that meets the two atoms, again requiring division. The third proof: an angle with straight lines can be divided infinitely; if the sides of the angle were composed of atoms, the infinite division would be impossible, so atoms cannot exist. The fourth proof is more elaborate: if a line is rotated about one endpoint, it forms a circle; if the circle's circumference were composed of atoms, then either the outer and inner parts of the circumference are equal (which is false) or there is a void between them, leading to division; or if the void contains a part, then the outer is double the inner — both absurd. Similarly, for the sphere. Thus geometry necessitates infinite divisibility, refuting the atom.

Key Terms
تنصيف
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the first geometric proof demonstrate?
A) Lines can be infinite.
B) Atomic composition leads to contradiction in bisection.
C) Atoms are divisible.
D) Geometry is irrelevant to atomism.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of bisecting any line proves that matter cannot be composed of indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has your conception of Allah's creation included the idea that matter is continuous and divisible without end? How does this proof challenge any complacency you might have in thinking you fully understand the nature of the physical world? Will you commit to studying more proofs to deepen your certainty?
?
If every line can be bisected, then every aspect of your life—your character, your deeds, your intentions—can be examined and refined infinitely. What is one part of yourself you have considered 'indivisible' and unchangeable, and how will you begin to 'bisect' it today through self-accountability?
?
This proof uses a simple geometric truth to refute a false belief. Are you willing to let such clear rational arguments reshape your worldview, even if it means abandoning comfortable assumptions? What assumption about the world or about your faith are you holding onto that might be contradicted by clear evidence?

The Second Geometric Proof: Equilateral Triangle
Taftazani presents the second proof using equilateral triangles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how constructing an equilateral triangle on a line composed of atoms leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
An equilateral triangle forces an atom to sit at the junction.

The second argument uses a basic geometric construction: on any line, you can build an equilateral triangle. But if the base line is made of two atoms, the apex must fall exactly at the point where the two atoms meet, which would require the apex to be an atom occupying the junction—an impossibility. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof. Note the key phrase 'بوقوع جزء على ملتقى الجزئين'.
2
Visualize the triangle
Draw a line composed of two adjacent squares (atoms). Try to construct an equilateral triangle with that as base.
The apex must be directly above the midpoint of the base.
3
Identify the contradiction
If the base is two atoms, the midpoint is the junction. The apex must lie on that point, implying an atom spans the junction, which cannot happen.
4
Consider the assumption
The proof assumes that the line is composed of exactly two parts. But the general principle holds for any number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة هندسية على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
قال (ومنها)
ما يتعلق بأصول هندسية مبنية على انتفاء الجزء وهي وجوه : الأول : كل خط يمكن تنصيفه ففي المركب من الأجزاء الوتر يلزم تجزيء الوسطاني.
الثاني : كل خط يمكن أن يعمل عليه مثلث متساوي الأضلاع ولا يتصور في المركب من جزئين إلا بوقوع جزء على ملتقى الجزئين.
الثالث : كل زاوية مستقيمة الخطين تنقسم لا إلى نهاية .
الرابع : إذا ثبت أحد طرفي الخط المستقيم وأدير حتى عاد إلى وضعه الأول حصلت الدائرة، ثم إذا أدير نصفها على قطرها الثابت حصلت الكرة، ووجود الجزء ينفيهما لأنا لو فرضنا محيط الدائرة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فإما أن يكون ظواهر الأجزاء كبواطنها، فيلزم تساوي ظاهر المحيط وباطنه أو أكثر فيلزم الانقسام، أو بين الظواهر فرج خلاء لا يسع كلها منها جزءاً فيلزم الانقسام، أو يسع فيكون الظاهر ضعف الباطن، ولأن المدار الذي بلاصق المنطقة، إما أن يكون بإزاء كل جزء منها جزء منه فيتساويان، أو أقل فينقسم .
Geometric proofs for negating the indivisible part. He said: And among them are what pertain to geometric principles based on the negation of the part. They are aspects: First: Every line can be bisected, so in that composed of parts, the bisection of the middle [line] is necessary. Second: Every line can have an equilateral triangle constructed on it, and it is inconceivable in that composed of two parts except by a part falling at the junction of the two parts. Third: Every angle with straight lines can be divided infinitely. Fourth: If one end of a straight line is fixed and it is rotated until it returns to its original position, a circle is obtained; then if its half is rotated on its fixed diameter, a sphere is obtained. The existence of the part negates both, because if we suppose the circumference of the circle to be composed of indivisible parts, then either the outer parts are like the inner parts, so the outer part of the circumference and the inner part would be equal — or more, so division follows; or there is between the outer parts a void space that does not contain from them a part — so division follows; or it contains [a part], so the outer part would be double the inner part. And because the circle that is tangent to the zone, either it is opposite every part of it a part of it, so they are equal, or less, so it divides.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a set of four geometric proofs (adilla handasiyya) against the existence of the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazza'). The author states that these are based on geometric principles that presuppose the negation of the atom. The first proof: every line can be bisected; if the line were composed of atoms, then at the point of bisection, the line would have to be divided, implying that the atom itself is divisible, which is contradictory. The second proof: an equilateral triangle can be constructed on any line; if the line is composed of two atoms, the apex of the triangle would have to fall on the junction of the two atoms, implying that the apex is an atom that meets the two atoms, again requiring division. The third proof: an angle with straight lines can be divided infinitely; if the sides of the angle were composed of atoms, the infinite division would be impossible, so atoms cannot exist. The fourth proof is more elaborate: if a line is rotated about one endpoint, it forms a circle; if the circle's circumference were composed of atoms, then either the outer and inner parts of the circumference are equal (which is false) or there is a void between them, leading to division; or if the void contains a part, then the outer is double the inner — both absurd. Similarly, for the sphere. Thus geometry necessitates infinite divisibility, refuting the atom.

Key Terms
مثلث متساوي الأضلاع
ملتقى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the second proof show about a line composed of two atoms?
A) It can form an equilateral triangle without issue.
B) The apex falls between the atoms, creating a gap.
C) The apex must be at the junction, which is impossible.
D) The triangle cannot be constructed at all.

Key Takeaway
Geometric construction of an equilateral triangle on an atomic base leads to an impossible situation at the junction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that a seemingly plausible belief (atomism) leads to logical contradiction. Are there any beliefs you hold that you have never subjected to rigorous logical examination? Will you commit today to questioning one such belief using clear reasoning?
?
The kalam scholars used geometry to correct false ideas. When you discover that you have been wrong about something important, do you humbly accept the truth, or do you resist? What is one area in your life where you are most resistant to changing your mind?
?
This argument forces you to accept a conclusion even if it feels counterintuitive. Are you willing to follow reason where it leads, even if it challenges your initial intuitions or comfort? Write down one specific situation where you need to apply this discipline.

The Third Geometric Proof: Infinite Divisibility of Angles
Taftazani presents the third proof: infinite divisibility of angles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the infinite divisibility of angles contradicts the existence of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Angles can be divided forever, but atoms cannot.

The third proof shifts from lines to angles: any straight-lined angle can be divided infinitely. If the sides of the angle were composed of atoms, infinite division would be impossible because you would eventually hit an atom that cannot be split. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third proof. Note the phrase 'تنقسم لا إلى نهاية'.
2
Understand infinite divisibility
In Euclidean geometry, an angle can be bisected repeatedly without end. This is a fundamental property.
3
Apply atomic assumption
If the sides are made of atoms, at some point the line of the angle's side would be only a few atoms wide, and further bisection would require splitting an atom.
4
Draw the contradiction
Thus, atomism contradicts the geometric fact of infinite divisibility. Therefore, atoms cannot exist.

Source Text

The Fourth Geometric Proof: Circle and Sphere
Taftazani presents the fourth proof using circles and spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the properties of circles and spheres prove that matter cannot be composed of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
A circle's circumference cannot be atomic without absurdity.

The fourth and most elaborate argument uses the generation of a circle (by rotating a line about a fixed endpoint) and a sphere (by rotating a semicircle). If the circumference were made of atoms, several absurd consequences follow regarding the outer and inner parts. This lesson dissects the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth proof. It is longer, so read carefully.
2
Understand the construction
A circle is formed by rotating a line about a fixed endpoint. A sphere is formed by rotating a semicircle about its diameter.
3
Analyze the circumference assumption
Suppose the circle's circumference is composed of atoms. Consider the outer and inner parts of these atoms.
The key is: either the outer part is equal to the inner part, or there is a gap, etc.
4
Work through the cases
The text enumerates possibilities: equal outer and inner (impossible because circumference is longer than inner circle), or a gap appears leading to division, or the gap contains an atom so outer is double inner.
Each case leads to a contradiction.
5
Extend to sphere
The same reasoning applies to the sphere, using circles of latitude (zones).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definition and Possibility of the Circle and Sphere
The author defines the circle and sphere and demonstrates their possibility and actuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defines the circle and sphere and why their actuality is used as an argument against atomism.

Core Idea
Geometric shapes like circles and spheres are real and possible, serving as a basis for rational arguments.

In his fourth argument, the author turns to geometry to demonstrate that certain shapes are not only conceivable but actually exist. This passage defines the circle and sphere in a constructive manner, showing that they are not merely abstract ideas. Understanding these definitions is crucial for following the subsequent refutation of atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get a clear sense of the author's definitions.
Notice the author starts by claiming possibility and actuality before defining.
2
Identify the definition of the circle
The author describes how a circle is generated: a fixed point, a rotating line, and the resulting surface. Note the equality of radii.
The definition is constructive—it shows how the shape is made.
3
Identify the definition of the sphere
Similarly, the sphere is defined by rotating a semicircle around its diameter. Note the reference to the center and equal lines to the surface.
Compare the two definitions: one is 2D, the other 3D.
4
Understand the claim of actuality
The author says both shapes are 'possible, rather actual.' Reflect on what this means in the context of debating atomism.
This passage sets the stage for using geometry as a counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن كلاً من الدائرة والكرة ممكن بل متحقق . أما الدائرة فلأنا نتخيل على السطح المستوي خطاً مستقيماً متناهياً نثبت أحد طرفيه، ونديره حول طرفه الثابت إلى أن يعود إلى موضعه الأول، فيحصل سطح يحيط به مستدير حاصل من حركة الطرف المتحرك وفي باطنه نقطة الطرف الثابت ، جميع الخطوط الخارجة من تلك النقطة إلى ذلك المحيط متساوية ، لكون كل منها بقدر ذلك الخط الذي أدرناه، ولا نعني بالدائرة إلا ذلك السطح أو الخط المحيط به، وأما الكرة فلأنا إذا أثبتنا قطر الدائرة، أعني الخط الخارج من المركز إلى المحيط في الجهتين، وأدرنا نصف الدائرة على ذلك الخط إلى أن يعود إلى وضعه الأول حصل سطح مستدير محيط بجسم في باطنه نقطة جميع الخطوط الخارجة منها إلى ذلك السطح متساوية، ولا نعني بالكرة إلا ذلك الجسم المحاط أو السطح المحيط
The fourth: Indeed each of the circle and the sphere is possible, rather actual. As for the circle, we imagine on a plane surface a finite straight line, fix one of its ends, and rotate it around its fixed end until it returns to its first position, then a surface is obtained surrounded by a circumference resulting from the motion of the moving end, and inside it is the point of the fixed end; all lines emerging from that point to that circumference are equal, because each of them is equal to that line which we rotated. And we mean by the circle nothing other than that surface or the line surrounding it. As for the sphere, if we fix the diameter of the circle—that is, the line from the center to the circumference in both directions—and rotate the semicircle on that line until it returns to its first position, a rounded surface is obtained surrounding a body inside which is a point from which all lines emerging to that surface are equal. And we mean by the sphere nothing other than that enclosed body or the surrounding surface.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a definition and demonstration. The author begins his fourth argument against atomism by establishing the reality of the circle and sphere. He defines the circle as the surface generated by rotating a line around one fixed endpoint, resulting in all radii being equal. He then defines the sphere as the solid generated by rotating a semicircle about its diameter. These definitions are important because they provide the basis for the subsequent refutation. The author asserts that both the circle and sphere are not only possible but actually exist, implying that geometry is real and not mere illusion.

كرة
مركز
محيط
قطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Geometry is irrelevant to theology.
Correction: The author uses geometric definitions and the actuality of circles and spheres as a rational argument to refute atomism, showing that geometry is foundational to theological reasoning.

D) An infinite line

Key Takeaway
The author establishes the reality of geometric shapes like circles and spheres as a foundation for rational argument against atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author shows that the circle and sphere are possible because they can be constructed step by step. Reflect on your own life: what steps are you taking to actualize the good that is possible for you? What 'construction' have you neglected?
?
If the precise equality of radii is a sign of order in creation, what does that mean for the order Allah expects in your character? Are you 'centered' on Him such that all your actions are equally directed to His pleasure?
?
The sphere is formed by rotating a semicircle—a partial shape—to completeness. Where in your life are you content with a 'semicircle' of obedience, stopping short of the full rotation that would make you a complete servant of Allah?

Refutation of Indivisible Parts Using the Circle
Sanusi refutes atomism using the circle's contradictory implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometric properties of the circle logically refute the theory of indivisible parts (atoms).

Core Idea
The circle's geometry forces us to reject indivisible parts.

The atomist theory claims that all bodies are composed of tiny indivisible particles. But the circle—a simple geometric figure—shows why this cannot be true. The author presents a logical argument using the properties of concentric circles to demonstrate the absurd consequences of such a view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once aloud, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the two cases the author considers.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main cases
Notice that the author splits the argument based on whether the exteriors of the parts are adjacent or not. Mark these two branches.
3
Work through the implications
Follow each branch to its conclusion. The first branch leads to absurd equality of all circles; the second leads to division of parts or sensory contradiction.
Visualizing concentric circles may help.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن كلاً من الدائرة والكرة ينافي كون الأجسام والخطوط والسطوح من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، أما الدائرة فلأنها لو كانت من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ، فإما أن تكون ظواهر الأجزاء متلاقية كبواطنها أولا، فعلى الأول إما أن تكون بواطنها أصغر من الظواهر فينقسم الجزء ، أو لا، فيساوي في المساحة باطن الدائرة، أعني المقعر ظاهرها أعني المحدب وهو باطل بالضرورة، وإن شئت فالبرهان، وذلك أنه يستلزم تساوي جميع الدوائر المحاطة بها حتى التي بقرب المركز، وكذا جميع الدوائر المحيطة بها حتى المحيط بجميع الأجسام وبطلانه ضروري ، واللزوم بين لأن التقدير تساوي الظاهر والباطن من كل دائرة ، وباطن المحيط يساوي ظاهر المحاط بحكم الضرورة ، وبحكم أن بإزاء كل جزء من المحيط جزءاً من المحاط، لأنه لا أصغر من الجزء، ولا فرج بين ظواهر الأجزاء. وعلى الثاني وهو أن تكون ظواهر الأجزاء غير متلاقية، يلزم انقسام الجزء لأن غير الملاقي غير الملاقي، وأيضاً فما بينها من الفرج إن لم يسع كل جزء منها جزءاً لزم انقسام الجزء وإن وسعه لزم كون الظاهر ضعف الباطن، والحس يكذبه
Then indeed each of the circle and the sphere contradicts the notion that bodies, lines, and surfaces are composed of indivisible parts. As for the circle, if it were composed of indivisible parts, then either the exteriors of the parts are adjacent like their interiors or not. In the first case, either their interiors are smaller than their exteriors, so the part divides, or not, then in area the interior of the circle—that is, the concave—equals its exterior—that is, the convex—and this is false by necessity. And if you wish, the proof: that it necessitates the equality of all circles enclosed within it, even those near the center, and likewise all circles surrounding it, even the one encompassing all bodies. Its falsity is necessary. The entailment is clear because the supposition is equality of the exterior and interior of every circle, and the interior of the surrounding circle equals the exterior of the surrounded circle by necessity, and because opposite every part of the surrounding circle is a part of the surrounded, since there is nothing smaller than the part and no gap between the exteriors of the parts. In the second case—that the exteriors of the parts are not adjacent—the division of the part follows because that which does not meet another does not meet. Also, as for the gap between them, if it does not contain a part of each part, then division of the part follows; if it does contain, then the exterior is double the interior, and sense perception denies this.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage refutes the theory of indivisible parts (atoms) using the properties of the circle. The author argues that if a circle were composed of indivisible parts, two contradictory cases arise concerning whether the parts touch or not. If they touch, either the interior of the circle is smaller than its exterior (which would require dividing the part) or they are equal. But if they are equal, it entails that all concentric circles are equal, which is absurd because the innermost circle would be the same size as the outermost. If the parts do not touch, then the gaps between them necessitate either division of the part or that the exterior is double the interior, which contradicts sense perception. Thus, the circle cannot be composed of indivisible parts.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
ظواهر
بواطن
مقعر
محدب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A circle can be composed of indivisible atoms.
Correction: The author demonstrates that this leads to logical contradictions, such as all concentric circles being equal or the exterior being double the interior.

Checkpoint
According to the author, if the exteriors of the parts of a circle are adjacent and the interior is not smaller, what must be true?
A) The interior is larger
B) The interior equals the exterior
C) The circle is possible
D) The parts divide

Key Takeaway
The circle argument shows that atomism leads to contradictions, so the theory of indivisible parts must be rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof rests on a simple 'either/or' that leads to contradiction. When you hold a belief that conflicts with clear evidence, do you honestly examine the alternatives, or do you cling to the familiar? Will you today identify one belief you have that you have never subjected to such rigorous examination and actually test it?
?
Sanusi writes that the equality of all circles is 'false by necessity.' Have you ever accepted a claim in your life that, once you followed its implications, led to something you knew was false? Did you have the courage to reject the original claim, or did you stop the chain of reasoning? What will you do the next time you sense such a contradiction?
?
The circle argument forces you to accept that indivisible parts cannot exist. If a simple geometric figure can dismantle a physical theory, what does that say about the power of reason? Are you willing to let rational demonstration—even when it is uncomfortable—shape your most fundamental beliefs, including those about God, the world, and yourself? Commit to one area where you will submit to the truth, no matter the cost.

Refutation of Atomism Using the Sphere
The author refutes atomism using the sphere and concludes that geometry disproves atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometric properties of a sphere disprove the existence of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
The sphere's geometry shows that atomism leads to impossible contradictions.

This passage extends the refutation of atomism from the circle to the sphere. The author argues that if a sphere were made of indivisible parts, then parallel circles on the sphere would either be equal (which is false) or would require dividing an atom. This proves that atomism cannot hold for spheres, which are real. He also dismisses the atomists' attempted counterargument about motion as mere imagination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Follow the logical argument
Identify the two possibilities the author presents and how both lead to contradiction.
The two possibilities are: either the touching circle is equal to the zone part by part, or it is less than a part.
3
Note the dismissal of the motion argument
The author concludes that the atomists' motion argument is 'pure imagination' and does not save their position.
This reinforces the conclusion that atomism is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكرة فلأنها لو كانت من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فالمدار الذي يلاصق المنطقة التي هي أعظم الدوائر المتوازية على الكرة إما أن يكون بإزاء كل جزء من المنطقة منه، فيلزم تساويهما ، وهكذا جميع ما يوازيهما حتى التي حول القطب وبطلانه ظاهر أو أقل من جزء فيلزم انقسام الجزء، إذا تقرر هذا فقد انتظم أنه كلما صح القول بالدائرة أو الكرة لم يصح القول بالجزء، لكن المقدم حق، أو كلما صح القول بالجزء لم يصح القول بهما، لكن التالي باطل ولا خفاء في أن ما ذكروا من حركة الخط ونصف الدائرة محض توهم لا يفيد إمكان المفروض فضلا عن تحققه ولو سلم فإنما يصح لو لم يكن الخط والسطح من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، إذ مع ذلك تمتنع الحركة على الوجه الموصوف لتأديها إلى المحال
As for the sphere, if it were composed of indivisible parts, then the circle of latitude that touches the zone—which is the greatest of the parallel circles on the sphere—either is opposite every part of the zone from it, so their equality follows, and likewise all that is parallel to them even those around the pole—and its falsity is obvious—or less than a part, then division of the part follows. When this is established, it becomes coherent that whenever the circle or sphere is affirmed, the statement of the part is not affirmed, but the premise is true; or whenever the statement of the part is affirmed, they are not affirmed, but the consequent is false. And it is not hidden that what they mentioned about the motion of the line and the semicircle is pure imagination that does not prove the possibility of the supposed, let alone its actuality. And even if granted, it would only be valid if the line and surface were not composed of indivisible parts, for with that, motion in the described manner is impossible because it leads to impossibility.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation using the sphere. The author considers the largest parallel circle on a sphere (the equator) and the circle of latitude touching it. If the sphere were made of atoms, this touching circle would either be exactly equal to the equator part by part (leading to equality of all parallels, including those near the poles, which is false) or smaller than a part (which would divide the part). This proves the sphere also cannot be atomic. The author then concludes that if circle and sphere exist, atomism is false; if atomism is true, then circle and sphere cannot exist. Since the former is established (circle and sphere are real), atomism is false. He additionally criticizes the atomists' attempt to explain motion of lines as mere imagination, insufficient to prove possibility.

Misconception: The sphere argument is separate from the circle argument and addresses a different issue.
Correction: The sphere argument actually reinforces the same conclusion as the circle argument: atomism is geometrically impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the motion of the line and semicircle mentioned by the atomists?
A) It proves their point
B) It is a valid argument
C) It is pure imagination
D) It proves the reality of atoms

Key Takeaway
The sphere argument proves that atomism contradicts geometric reality, and the atomists' motion argument is merely imaginary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author dismisses the atomists' motion argument as 'pure imagination'. Are there areas in your faith or life where you rely on imaginative justifications instead of sound reasoning from revelation and logic? What is one specific rational proof you need to study more deeply to ground your conviction?
?
A single sphere dismantled an entire worldview. How stable is your own worldview? What is one foundation of your faith that you know to be absolutely certain, and what is one area where you are less sure? What concrete action will you take this week to strengthen that weak area?

Euclid's Geometric Argument Against Atomism
The author presents Euclid's proof of the Pythagorean theorem as a fifth argument against atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's Pythagorean theorem (the Bride's Shape) is used to argue against the atomistic view of space.

Core Idea
Euclid's geometry reveals irrational lengths, disproving atomism.

The author presents a fifth argument against atomism drawn from Euclidean geometry. By invoking the Pythagorean theorem—known in Arabic as 'شكل العروس' (the Bride's Shape)—he shows that the hypotenuse of a right triangle is incommensurable with the legs. This mathematical fact challenges any theory that treats space as composed of indivisible atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms for triangle, hypotenuse, and square.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the theorem and the example
The author states Euclid's theorem: in a right triangle, the square of the hypotenuse equals the sum of squares of the legs. He then gives an example with legs of length 10.
The example attempts to show that the hypotenuse is irrational.
3
Reflect on the anti-atomistic implication
Consider why the existence of irrational lengths contradicts atomism: if space were made of atoms, all lengths would be integer multiples of an atom size, but √200 is not an integer.
This argument challenges atomistic physics.
4
Spot the numerical error
The text states that 14 squared is 106, which is incorrect (14²=196). This is likely a scribal error. Does the error affect the argument?
The argument's logic remains valid despite the slip.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس برهن اقليدس في شكل العروس على أن كل مثلث قائم الزاوية، مربع وتر زاويته القائمة مساوٍ لمربعي ضلعيها بمعنى أن الحاصل من ضربه نفسه مثل مجموع الحاصل من ضرب كل من الضلعين في نفسه، فإذا فرضنا كلا من الضلعين عشرة مثلاً كان مجموع مربعيهما مائتين، فيكون الضلع الآخر أعني وتر القائمة جذراً لمائتين وهو أكثر من أربعة عشر، لأن مجذورها مائة وستة
The fifth: Euclid proved in the 'Bride's Shape' that for every right triangle, the square of the hypotenuse—the side opposite the right angle—is equal to the squares of the two sides, meaning that the product of multiplying it by itself equals the sum of the products of multiplying each of the two sides by itself. So if we suppose each of the two sides to be, for example, ten, then the sum of their squares is two hundred. Then the other side—i.e., the hypotenuse—is the square root of two hundred, which is more than fourteen, because its square is one hundred and six.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth argument from Euclid's geometry. The author invokes the Pythagorean theorem (called the 'Bride's Shape' in Euclid) to show that the hypotenuse of a right triangle is incommensurable with the legs when the legs are equal. For example, if each leg is 10, the hypotenuse is √200, which is not an integer but an irrational number. This incommensurability is problematic for atomism because if lines were composed of discrete atoms, all lengths would be rational multiples. The existence of irrationals contradicts atomism. The author's example shows that the hypotenuse cannot be expressed as an integer multiple of the leg, thus undermining atomistic geometry. Note: The final phrase 'because its square is one hundred and six' is mathematically erroneous (14^2=196, not 106), likely a scribal error.

Key Terms
مثلث قائم الزاوية
وتر
مربع
جذر
شكل العروس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is just a geometric curiosity with no theological relevance.
Correction: The author uses it to show that atomism cannot accommodate irrational ratios, thus undermining a materialistic view of space.

Checkpoint
What is the name of the Euclidean proof mentioned by the author?
A) The Bride's Shape (شكل العروس)
B) The Pythagorean Theorem
C) The Theorem of the Hypotenuse
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Pythagorean theorem's proof of irrationality serves as a powerful argument against atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that a simple mathematical fact—the irrationality of √200—can overturn a whole worldview like atomism. What comfortable belief in your life might be contradicted by a clear truth you have been ignoring? Will you examine it today?
?
The passage shows that even basic geometry demands that we accept realities that do not fit our limited categories (like whole numbers). In your relationship with Allah, do you ever try to reduce His attributes (mercy, justice) to human measurements? What would it mean to let go of that attempt?
?
Euclid's argument uses a concrete example—legs of length 10—to make a universal point. What small, overlooked verse of the Quran or hadith have you dismissed as irrelevant, though it might challenge the very foundations of your current understanding? What would it cost you to take it seriously?

The Circle Argument against Atomism
Argues that circles are incompatible with indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometric properties of a circle demonstrate the impossibility of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
A circle's continuous nature refutes the possibility of atomic parts.

This passage engages directly with the theory of atomism, which claims that all bodies are made of indivisible units. The author uses the geometry of circles to show a contradiction: if circles exist, atomism cannot hold. You are about to see a dense argument that relies on the relationship between a circle's circumference and its interior.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Get a feel for the two hypotheses presented.
Notice the technical terms like 'جزء لا يتجزأ' and 'دائرة'.
2
Understand the first hypothesis
The first hypothesis assumes the surfaces of the circumference parts are contiguous. The author claims this forces equality between inner and outer. Ask yourself: why does equality follow?
The key is that each part of the circumference corresponds to exactly one part of the interior.
3
Analyze the second hypothesis
The second hypothesis assumes the surfaces are not contiguous, creating gaps. The author then considers two subcases: gaps too small to hold a part (leading to division) or gaps large enough (making outer double inner, contradicting sense).
This shows both subcases lead to absurdity.
4
Connect to atomism
Reflect on why this argument refutes atomism. Since circles exist (premise), the only way for atomism to be true is if one of the hypotheses holds, but both lead to impossibility.
Thus, atomism is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين لأن التقدير تساوي الظاهر والباطن من كل دائرة، وباطن المحيط يساوي ظاهر المحاط بحكم الضرورة، وبحكم أن بإزاء كل جزء من المحيط جزءاً من المحاط، لأنه لا أصغر من الجزء، ولا فرج بين ظواهر الأجزاء. وعلى الثاني وهو أن تكون ظواهر الأجزاء غير متلاقية، يلزم انقسام الجزء لأن غير الملاقي غير الملاقي، وأيضاً فما بينها من الفرج إن لم يسع كل جزء منها جزءاً لزم انقسام الجزء وإن وسعه لزم كون الظاهر ضعف الباطن، والحس يكذبه. وأما الكرة فلأنها لو كانت من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فالمدار الذي يلاصق المنطقة التي هي أعظم الدوائر المتوازية على الكرة إما أن يكون بإزاء كل جزء من المنطقة منه، فيلزم تساويهما، وهكذا جميع ما يوازيهما حتى التي حول القطب وبطلانه ظاهر أو أقل من جزء فيلزم انقسام الجزء، إذا تقرر هذا فقد انتظم أنه كلما صح القول بالدائرة أو الكرة لم يصح القول بالجزء، لكن المقدم حق، أو كلما صح القول بالجزء لم يصح القول بهما، لكن التالي باطل ولا خفاء في أن ما ذكروا من حركة الخط ونصف الدائرة محض توهم لا يفيد إمكان المفروض فضلا ولو سلم فإنما يصح لو لم يكن الخط والسطح من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، إذ مع تمتنع الحركة على الوجه الموصوف لتأديها إلى المحال
Because the estimation [requires] equality of the outer and inner of every circle, and the inner of the circumference equals the outer of that which is encompassed by necessity, and by the rule that for every part of the circumference there is a part of the encompassed, because there is nothing smaller than the part, and there are no gaps between the surfaces of the parts. On the second [hypothesis], which is that the surfaces of the parts are not contiguous, the division of the part follows because the non-contiguous is not contiguous. Also, what is between them of gaps—if a part does not encompass a part of them, the division of the part follows; if it encompasses it, then the outer is double the inner, and sense perception denies it. As for the sphere, if it were composed of indivisible parts, then the circle of latitude that touches the equator (which is the largest parallel circle on the sphere) would either have each part of the equator corresponding to a part of it, thus they would be equal, and likewise all parallel circles even those near the pole—its falsity is obvious—or less than a part, thus the part would divide. When this is established, it becomes clear that whenever the statement about the circle or sphere is correct, the statement about the part is not correct, but the premise is true; or whenever the statement about the part is correct, the statement about them is not correct, but the consequent is false. There is no hidden fact that what they mentioned about the motion of the line and the semicircle is pure illusion, not establishing the possibility of the assumed, let alone [being valid]; and even if it were granted, it would only be valid if the line and surface were not composed of indivisible parts, since motion in the described manner is impossible because it leads to an absurdity.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against atomism (the theory that bodies are composed of indivisible parts) based on the geometry of circles and spheres. The author argues that if a circle or sphere exists, it cannot be composed of indivisible parts. The argument proceeds in two parts: first for circles, then for spheres. For circles: consider a circle and its interior region. Either the circumference parts align exactly with the interior parts (requiring equality) or they do not, leading to division of the supposed indivisible part. For spheres: similarly, consider parallel circles on a sphere. The circle of latitude touching the equator must either correspond exactly to parts of the equator (requiring equality) or be less than a part (which divides the part). The conclusion is that circles and spheres are incompatible with indivisible parts; since circles and spheres are real, atomism is false. The author also dismisses a counterargument involving motion of a line and semicircle as mere illusion.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ (indivisible part)
دائرة (circle)
ظاهر (outer surface)
باطن (inner surface)
محاط (encompassed)
فرج (gap)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is purely mathematical and has no metaphysical implications.
Correction: The argument demonstrates a metaphysical contradiction: atomism leads to geometric impossibilities, proving the impossibility of indivisible parts existing in a world with circles.

Checkpoint
What does the first hypothesis about the circle's parts entail?
A) The inner and outer are unequal.
B) The inner and outer are equal.
C) The parts are divisible.
D) The gap between parts is real.

Key Takeaway
A circle cannot be composed of indivisible parts because either the parts align perfectly (forcing equality) or they do not (forcing division or contradiction with sense).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The kalam scholars used precise geometric arguments to defend their creed. Is your own faith built on such rigorous reasoning, or on comfortable feelings that have never been tested?
?
If the physical world is created with such mathematical consistency that a circle cannot exist without continuous parts, what does that tell you about the Creator's knowledge and wisdom? Are you willing to accept the implications for your life?
?
Consider a belief you hold about God or existence that you have never subjected to logical scrutiny. Are you prepared to examine it with the same honesty as this argument examines atomism?

The Sphere Argument against Atomism
Argues that spheres are incompatible with indivisible parts and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometry of a sphere reinforces the impossibility of indivisible parts and see the refutation of a counterargument.

Core Idea
The sphere's parallel circles force the same contradiction as the circle.

This lesson continues the anti-atomist argument, now applying the same reasoning to spheres. The author uses the largest parallel circle (the equator) and smaller circles toward the pole to show that atomism fails here as well. The passage ends with a dismissal of a possible objection involving motion of a line.

Guided Path
1
Read the sphere passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the sphere argument. Notice how it mirrors the circle argument but with parallel circles.
Key term: 'منطقة' (equator) and 'المتوازية' (parallel circles).
2
Understand the sphere argument
Consider the circle of latitude touching the equator. The author argues that either each part of the equator corresponds to a part of that circle (making them equal, which is false) or the circle of latitude is smaller than a part (forcing division).
This parallels the two hypotheses from the circle argument.
3
Grasp the general conclusion
The author concludes that whenever circles or spheres exist, atomism fails. Since we know circles and spheres exist, atomism is false.
This is presented as a categorical claim.
4
Analyze the refutation of the counterargument
The author dismisses a counterargument about motion of a line and semicircle as pure illusion. The refutation states that such motion is impossible if the line is made of indivisible parts.
This shows the argument is robust against objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين لأن التقدير تساوي الظاهر والباطن من كل دائرة، وباطن المحيط يساوي ظاهر المحاط بحكم الضرورة، وبحكم أن بإزاء كل جزء من المحيط جزءاً من المحاط، لأنه لا أصغر من الجزء، ولا فرج بين ظواهر الأجزاء. وعلى الثاني وهو أن تكون ظواهر الأجزاء غير متلاقية، يلزم انقسام الجزء لأن غير الملاقي غير الملاقي، وأيضاً فما بينها من الفرج إن لم يسع كل جزء منها جزءاً لزم انقسام الجزء وإن وسعه لزم كون الظاهر ضعف الباطن، والحس يكذبه. وأما الكرة فلأنها لو كانت من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فالمدار الذي يلاصق المنطقة التي هي أعظم الدوائر المتوازية على الكرة إما أن يكون بإزاء كل جزء من المنطقة منه، فيلزم تساويهما، وهكذا جميع ما يوازيهما حتى التي حول القطب وبطلانه ظاهر أو أقل من جزء فيلزم انقسام الجزء، إذا تقرر هذا فقد انتظم أنه كلما صح القول بالدائرة أو الكرة لم يصح القول بالجزء، لكن المقدم حق، أو كلما صح القول بالجزء لم يصح القول بهما، لكن التالي باطل ولا خفاء في أن ما ذكروا من حركة الخط ونصف الدائرة محض توهم لا يفيد إمكان المفروض فضلا ولو سلم فإنما يصح لو لم يكن الخط والسطح من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، إذ مع تمتنع الحركة على الوجه الموصوف لتأديها إلى المحال
Because the estimation [requires] equality of the outer and inner of every circle, and the inner of the circumference equals the outer of that which is encompassed by necessity, and by the rule that for every part of the circumference there is a part of the encompassed, because there is nothing smaller than the part, and there are no gaps between the surfaces of the parts. On the second [hypothesis], which is that the surfaces of the parts are not contiguous, the division of the part follows because the non-contiguous is not contiguous. Also, what is between them of gaps—if a part does not encompass a part of them, the division of the part follows; if it encompasses it, then the outer is double the inner, and sense perception denies it. This passage presents an argument against atomism (the theory that bodies are composed of indivisible parts) based on the geometry of circles and spheres. The author argues that if a circle or sphere exists, it cannot be composed of indivisible parts. The argument proceeds in two parts: first for circles, then for spheres. For circles: consider a circle and its interior region. Either the circumference parts align exactly with the interior parts (requiring equality) or they do not, leading to division of the supposed indivisible part. For spheres: similarly, consider parallel circles on a sphere. The circle of latitude touching the equator must either correspond exactly to parts of the equator (requiring equality) or be less than a part (which divides the part). The conclusion is that circles and spheres are incompatible with indivisible parts; since circles and spheres are real, atomism is false. The author also dismisses a counterargument involving motion of a line and semicircle as mere illusion.

Key Terms
كرة (sphere)
منطقة (equator)
دوائر متوازية (parallel circles)
جزء لا يتجزأ (indivisible part)
حركة الخط (motion of the line)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sphere argument is just a repeat of the circle argument and adds nothing new.
Correction: The sphere argument extends the reasoning to three dimensions and refutes a specific counterargument, solidifying the case against atomism.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the relationship between circles/spheres and indivisible parts?
A) They can coexist if the parts are large enough.
B) If circles or spheres exist, indivisible parts cannot exist.
C) Indivisible parts can exist only in spheres, not circles.
D) The motion of a line proves atomism is possible.

Key Takeaway
The sphere argument proves that if a sphere exists, indivisible parts cannot exist, and a potential objection is dismissed as illusory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument reveals that holding contradictory premises leads to absurdity. Are there areas in your life where you cling to two conflicting truths—perhaps in your understanding of God's attributes or on your duties to others? How will you seek clarity and resolve that tension?
?
The author dismisses a counterargument as 'pure illusion'. Is there an excuse or rationalization you have been using to avoid a hard truth about your faith or character? What would it take for you to see through that illusion and submit to the truth?
?
If a sphere cannot be composed of separate, indivisible parts, what does that suggest about the unity and coherence of creation? How does the recognition of an ordered, seamless universe call you to a more integrated and sincere worship of its Creator?
