The Fifth Argument: Euclid's Theorem vs. Atomism
Uses Euclid's Pythagorean theorem to argue against atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Euclid's Pythagorean theorem is used to refute the atomist claim that all lengths are composed of finite indivisible parts.

Core Idea
Geometry proves that some lengths are irrational, undermining atomism.

This lesson presents the fifth argument against atomism, drawn from Euclid's geometry. The author applies the Pythagorean theorem to a right triangle, showing that if the legs are composed of integer numbers of atoms, the hypotenuse must be an irrational length—something atomism cannot accommodate. This argument leverages a universally accepted geometric proof to challenge the coherence of atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the mathematical logic.
Notice that the passage assumes the truth of Euclid's theorem.
2
Understand the geometric claim
The passage states Euclid's theorem: in a right triangle, the square of the hypotenuse equals the sum of the squares of the other two sides.
This is the Pythagorean theorem, known as the 'Bride's Figure' in Islamic tradition.
3
Follow the numerical example
Assume each leg is 10 'parts'. Then the sum of squares is 200. The hypotenuse is √200, which lies between 14 and 15 and is not an integer.
This shows the hypotenuse cannot be a whole number of parts.
4
Grasp the implication for atomism
Atomism holds that all lengths are multiples of a smallest indivisible part. An irrational hypotenuse contradicts this, so atomism is false.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this geometric proof is considered decisive: it uses a universally accepted theorem to expose an inconsistency in atomism.
The argument does not require empirical measurement; it is purely rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس برهن اقليدس في شكل العروس على أن كل مثلث قائم الزاوية مربع وتر زاويته القائمة مساوٍ لمربعي ضلعيها بمعنى أن الحاصل من ضربه نفسه مثل مجموع الحاصل من ضرب كل من الضلعين في نفسه، فإذا فرضنا كلا من الضلعين عشرة مثلاً كان مجموع مربعيهما مائتين، فيكون الضلع الآخر أعني وتر القائمة جذراً لمائتين وهو أكثر من أربعة عشر، لأن مجذورها مائة وستة وتسعون، وأقل من خمسة عشر لأن مجذورها مائتان وخمسة وعشرون، وكذا كل مالا يكون لمجموع مربعي الضلعين جذر منطق
The fifth [argument]: Euclid proved in the 'Bride's Figure' that for every right triangle, the square of the hypotenuse is equal to the sum of the squares of the two legs; meaning that the product of its multiplication by itself equals the sum of the products of each of the two legs multiplied by itself. So if we assume each of the two legs is ten, for example, then the sum of their squares is two hundred. Thus the other side, i.e., the hypotenuse, is the square root of two hundred, which is greater than fourteen because its square is one hundred ninety-six, and less than fifteen because its square is two hundred twenty-five. Likewise, everything for which the sum of the squares of the two legs does not have a rational square root [is irrational].
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against atomism, based on Euclid's proof of the Pythagorean theorem (the 'Bride's Figure'). The argument shows that if the two legs of a right triangle are composed of integer numbers of indivisible parts (e.g., 10 each), then the hypotenuse must be irrational (√200), which cannot be expressed as a whole number of indivisible parts. Since the geometric theorem is true, atomism—which requires all lengths to be integer multiples of a smallest part—contradicts geometry. The conclusion is that atomism is false.

Key Terms
مربع وتر (square of the hypotenuse)
جذر (root)
منطق (rational)
شكل العروس (Bride's Figure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is a proof of the Pythagorean theorem itself.
Correction: The passage assumes the theorem as already proven; it uses the theorem to argue against atomism, not to prove geometry.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the example of the right triangle with legs of 10 parts?
A) The hypotenuse is exactly 14 parts.
B) The hypotenuse is irrational, contradicting atomism.
C) The Pythagorean theorem is false.
D) Atomism allows irrational lengths.

Key Takeaway
The Pythagorean theorem shows that even simple right triangles require irrational lengths, which atomism cannot explain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The irrationality of sqrt(200) is an objective truth that contradicts a cherished assumption (atomism). What cherished belief about yourself or the world would you be willing to abandon if logic and evidence proved it false?
?
This argument uses a simple geometric fact to lead to a profound conclusion about reality. When you observe the natural world, do you actively look for evidence of a Creator, or do you take things at face value?
?
Euclid's theorem is undeniable once understood. Are there truths in your faith that you know are certain, yet you live as if they are optional? How would your daily life change if you treated them as unshakeable realities?

The Right Triangle Argument Against Atomism
Sanusi presents the sixth argument against atomism using a right triangle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the right triangle argument uses Euclidean geometry to refute the atomist claim that all lengths are composed of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
A right triangle's hypotenuse cannot be an integer number of parts, disproving atomism.

After presenting arguments using squares and circles, Sanusi now turns to a right triangle to show that atomism leads to a geometric contradiction. By constructing a simple triangle from a line of two parts and a perpendicular of one part, we can see that the hypotenuse must be between two and three parts, which is impossible if all lengths are multiples of a single part. This argument relies on a basic theorem from Euclid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the construction.
Notice how Sanusi uses the phrase 'نفرض خطاً من جزءين'.
2
Understand the geometric construction
We assume a line of two parts (the base). Then we place a part (one unit) perpendicularly above one end of the base, forming a right angle. The triangle has legs of lengths 2 and 1.
The right angle is crucial for applying Euclid's theorem.
3
Apply Euclid's theorem
Euclid proved that in a right triangle, the hypotenuse is less than the sum of the legs (2+1=3) and greater than each leg (2 and 1). Therefore, the hypotenuse must be between 2 and 3 parts.
This is a classic result from the Elements.
4
Conclude the refutation
Since the hypotenuse is between 2 and 3, it cannot be an integer number of parts. If atomism were true, all lengths would be multiples of the part, but here we have a non-integer length. Thus, atomism is false.
The argument does not require computing the exact length, only the inequality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : نفرض خطاً من جزءين، فنضع فوق أحدهما جزءاً، فتحصل زاوية قائمة، فوترها يجب أن يكون أقل من الثلاثة وأكثر من الاثنين، لما بين إقليدس أن وتر القائمة أقل من مجموع ضلعيها، وأكثر من كل منهما
The sixth: We assume a line of two parts, then we place a part above one of them, resulting in a right angle. Its hypotenuse must be less than three and greater than two, because Euclid has shown that the hypotenuse is less than the sum of the two legs and greater than each of them.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage gives the sixth argument against atomism. It constructs a right triangle from two lines: one of two parts (the base) and one of one part (the perpendicular). By Euclid's theorem, the hypotenuse is less than the sum of the legs (3) but greater than either leg (2). Since the hypotenuse cannot be an integer number of parts (it must be between 2 and 3), it contradicts the atomist assumption that all lengths are multiples of a single indivisible part.

Key Terms
وتر القائمة (hypotenuse)
ضلعان (legs)
جزء (part)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument requires calculating the exact length of the hypotenuse to disprove atomism.
Correction: The argument only needs the inequality (between 2 and 3) to show that the hypotenuse is not an integer number of parts. The exact length is irrelevant.

Checkpoint
Why does the right triangle argument refute atomism?
A) The hypotenuse is exactly 2.5 parts, which is not a whole number.
B) The hypotenuse is less than 2 parts, contradicting the legs.
C) The hypotenuse is between 2 and 3 parts, so it is not an integer number of parts.
D) Euclid's theorem is false for this triangle.

Key Takeaway
Euclid's theorem shows that a right triangle's hypotenuse cannot be a whole number of parts, undermining the atomist assumption that all lengths are multiples of a single part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument uses a simple geometric proof to demolish a whole worldview. Are there beliefs you hold that you have never exposed to a similar honest test? What would it cost you to test them?
?
Sanusi trusts that a truth from Euclid is enough to overturn a theological position. Do you trust that reason and revelation are so aligned that a correct proof can refine your understanding of the divine? Or do you fear that rational arguments might weaken your faith?
?
This argument shows that even the smallest assumption (atomism) can lead to contradiction. What small assumption in your own thinking about God or the world might you be holding uncritically? How can you examine it today?

The Diagonal Refutes Atomism
Seventh argument: square diagonal refutes atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh argument against atomism, which shows that the diagonal of a square cannot be composed of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
The diagonal of a square refutes atomism: it can't be made of indivisible parts.

This argument uses a geometric figure—a 4x4 square—to show that atomism leads to contradictions. By considering the diagonal, the author demonstrates that the assumption of indivisible parts fails. This is one of several rational arguments against atomism in the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the precise geometric setup.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage considers two possibilities for the diagonal: either its parts are contiguous (touching) or there are gaps between them. Find these two cases in the text.
3
Analyze the contradictions
For each possibility, see why it leads to a contradiction: contiguous makes diagonal equal to side (false by Bride's Figure); gaps lead to either a diagonal of 7 parts or divisibility of parts. Both contradict atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : نفرض مربعاً من أربعة خطوط مستقيمة، مضمومة بعضها إلى البعض، على غاية ما يمكن كل منها من أربعة أجزاء، فقطره خط يحصل من الجزء الأول من الخط الأول والثاني من الثاني والثالث من الثالث والرابع من الرابع . فإن كانت متلاقية كان القطر مساوياً للضلع ، ويبطله شكل العروس، وإن كان بينها فرج ولا تكون إلا ثلاثاً، فإما أن يسع كل جزء منها جزءاً فيكون القطر كالضلعين سبعة أجزاء، وهو باطل بالشكل الجاري، أو أقل فينقسم الجزء، وبما ذكرنا من استقامة الخطوط وتضامها على غاية ما يمكن يظهر امتناع أن تقع الفرج فيما بين بعض الأجزاء دون البعض
The seventh: We assume a square of four straight lines, joined to one another, each as much as possible of four parts. Its diagonal is a line consisting of the first part from the first line, the second from the second, the third from the third, and the fourth from the fourth. If they are contiguous, the diagonal equals the side, which the 'Bride's Figure' refutes. If there are gaps between them—and they cannot be but three—then either each gap encompasses a part, making the diagonal like the two legs, seven parts, which is false by the ongoing figure; or less, thus the part divides. And by what we have mentioned about the straightness of the lines and their maximal alignment, it is impossible for gaps to occur between some parts and not others.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument against atomism, focusing on the diagonal of a square. The square is assumed to have sides of 4 indivisible parts each. The diagonal, according to the Pythagorean theorem (the Bride's Figure), should be irrational (√32 ≈ 5.66). The argument considers two possibilities for how the diagonal could be composed of parts: either the parts are contiguous (making the diagonal equal to the side, which is false), or there are gaps. If there are gaps, they must be either large enough to hold a part (making the diagonal 7, which is also false) or smaller, which would require the indivisible part to be further divided. Thus, atomism is impossible.

Key Terms
قطر (diagonal)
ضلع (side)
فرج (gap)
شكل العروس (Bride's Figure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Atomism can account for the diagonal of a square because the diagonal can be composed of integer parts.
Correction: The argument shows that no integer composition of parts can match the actual diagonal length (irrational), so atomism fails.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the seventh argument?
A) The diagonal of a square equals its side.
B) The diagonal of a square is irrational, so atomism is false.
C) The diagonal must be composed of exactly 7 parts.
D) Gaps between parts are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The diagonal of a square cannot be composed of integer parts, thus atomism is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The diagonal argument begins with a simple assumption and follows it to contradiction. When in your own life have you held onto an assumption that led to inconsistency? Are you ready to follow the truth wherever it leads, even if it breaks your worldview?
?
If even the diagonal of a square can testify to the unity and order of creation, what excuse do we have for ignoring the signs around us? What one sign will you meditate on today?
?
The argument forces us to admit that parts must divide. What does that say about your own assumptions about your 'indivisible' identity? Are you willing to let God divide your ego?

Two Objections to Atomism and Their Replies
Raises two objections against atomism arguments and gives replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends atomism by responding to two objections with scope-limiting replies.

Core Idea
Objections are answered by clarifying their limited applicability.

In this passage, the author raises two possible objections that could be leveled against geometric proofs for atomism. He then provides concise replies that restrict the objections to specific cases—intellectual parts and spherical bodies—thereby preserving the original arguments. Understanding this dialectic reveals how kalam arguments are refined through objection and response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
Read each objection and note its reasoning. The first concerns essentiality of parts; the second concerns shape and conjunction gaps.
The phrase '(ومنها)' indicates these are objections from opponents, not the author's own view.
2
Identify each reply
For each objection, locate the reply beginning with 'ورد بأن'. Observe how the reply qualifies the objection—limiting it to intellectual parts or spherical bodies.
3
Reflect on the dialectical method
Consider why the author includes objections he will refute. This is standard in kalam to strengthen arguments by addressing counterpoints.
The author is not conceding; he is defending.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها) ما يبتنى على مقدمات لا سبيل إلى إثباتها وهي وجوه : الأول: لو كان الجسم من الجزء لكان ذاتياً له، فيكون بين الثبوت. ورد بأن ذلك في الأجزاء العقلية وبعد تعقل الماهية. الثاني : الجزء متناه فيكون متشكلاً، فإن كان ضلعاً انقسم، وإن كان كرة فعند الانضمام يبقى فرج أقل من الجزء. ورد بأن ذلك في الأجسام الكرية
He said: 'Among them is what is based on premises that cannot be proven, and they are aspects: First: If the body were from the part, it would be essential to it, thus it would be between existence [and non-existence?]. Reply: That applies to intellectual parts and after the conceptualization of the essence. Second: The part is finite, thus it is shaped. If it is a side, it divides; if it is a sphere, then upon conjunction a gap smaller than the part remains. Reply: That applies to spherical bodies.'
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents two objections that might be raised against the geometric arguments refuting atomism, along with their replies. The first objection: If the body were composed of parts, then the part would be essential to the body, implying a contradictory status ('between existence'). The reply: This objection only holds for conceptual/intellectual parts, not for actual physical parts. The second objection: The indivisible part is finite, so it has a shape. If it is a polyhedron (like a cube), it divides when joined; if it is a sphere, conjunction leaves a gap smaller than the part. The reply: This applies only to spherical bodies, not to all bodies. The author thus defends the original arguments by limiting the scope of the objections.

Key Terms
جزء عقلي (intellectual part)
متناه (finite)
متشكل (shaped)
كرة (sphere)
ضلع (side)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objections presented are the author's own arguments against atomism.
Correction: The author raises these objections only to refute them, defending the original arguments.

Checkpoint

B) He says the objection only applies to spherical bodies.

D) He claims the gap is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The author defends atomism by limiting objections to intellectual parts or spherical bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Core Refutation of Atomism
Refutation argument of atomism using similarity premise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that refutes atomism by showing that if parts are similar, what is impossible for the whole must be possible for each part.

Core Idea
If all parts are alike, impossibility of division for the whole implies possibility for each part.

The passage opens by presenting the atomist doctrine, then formulates a refutation based on the premise that the supposed simples are equal in nature. This argument is the foundation for the entire critique, and it sets up the logical move that will be defended against objections later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the atomist view
Read the passage to understand the atomist claim: bodies are composed of minute, hard, similar parts that are indivisible in actuality.
This is attributed to Democritus and some ancients.
2
Grasp the key premise of similarity
Notice that the argument relies on the claim that these parts are 'equal in nature' (mutasāwiyat al-ṭab‘).
This premise is admitted by the atomists themselves.
3
Follow the logical inference
The argument states: if the whole aggregate cannot undergo separative division, then each part must be capable of it in essence; otherwise the impossibility would transfer to the whole.
This is the core deductive step.
4
Note the caveat about accidental impediments
It adds that possibility in essence does not contradict actual impossibility due to accidental factors like extreme smallness or hardness.
This fends off the first objection by al-Razi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم إنهم أبطلوا) كون الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ وهماً لا فعلاً بأنها لما كانت متساوية في الطبع بزعمهم جاز على كل ما جاز على الكل بحسب الذات، وإن امتنع بعارض تشخص أو غيره. يشير إلى إبطال ما ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس وجمع من القدماء، من أن ما يشاهد من الأجسام المفردة كالماء مثلاً ليست ببسائط على الإطلاق، بل إنما هي حاصلة من تماس بسائط صغار متشابهة الطبع، في غاية الصلابة، غير قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية، بل الوهمية فقط وبهذا وبتسميتها أجساماً يمتاز هذا المذهب عن مذهب القائلين بالجزء، وتقريره أن تلك الأجزاء لما كانت متشابهة الطبع باعترافهم جاز على كل منها ما جاز على الآخرين، وعلى المجموع الحاصل من اجتماعها، والقسمة الانفكاكية مما لا يجوز على المجموع، فيجوز على كل جزء، إذ لو امتنعت على الجزء نظراً إلى ذاته لامتنعت على المجموع، ثم إمكان الانفكاك نظراً إلى الذات لا ينافي امتناعه لعارض تشخص أو غيره، من صور نوعية أو غاية صغر أو صلابة، أو عدم آلة قطاعة، أو نحو ذلك فلا يرد اعتراض الإمام بأن الامتدادات الجسمية غير باقية عند الانفصال، ومتجردة عند الاتصال، فهي أمور متشخصة، ولعلها تمنع الماهية المشتركة عن فعلها. وأما اعتراضه بمنع تساوي الأجسام في الماهية، فلا يندفع بأن مبنى الكلام على اعترافهم يكون تلك البسائط متساوية في الطبع، لأن مراده على ما صرح به في المباحث المشرقية هو أنه لو ادعى مدع أنها متخالفة بالماهية، وأنه لا يوجد جزآن متحدان في الماهية لم يثبت أن كل جسم قابل للقسمة والانفكاكية، فلم يتم دليل على إثبات الهيولي. لكن لا خفاء في أنه احتمال بعيد لأن الكلام في الجسم المفرد الذي لا يعقل فيه اختلاف طبيعة وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل قول من قال إن القسمة بأنواعها تحدث في المقسوم أثنينية تساوي طباع كل واحد طباع المجموع على القسمة الواردة على الجسم المفرد، وإلا ففساده واضح، وفسر الطباع بمصدر الصفة الذاتية الأولية للشيء حركة أو سكوناً، كان أو غيرهما، فيكون أعم من الطبيعة، وفسر أنواع القسمة بما يكون بحسب الفتك والقطع، أو بحسب الوهم. والفرض، أو بحسب اختلاف عرضين، قارين، أي ما هو للموضوع في نفسه كالسواد والبياض، أو غير قارين أي ما هو له بالقياس إلى الغير كالتماس والتحاذي، وذلك لأن الانقسام إن تأدى إلى الافتراق فالأول، وإلا فإن كان في مجرد الوهم، فالثاني وإلا فالثالث وبما ذكرنا من اعتبار مجرد الوهم صار هذا قسماً ثالثاً، وإلا فهو من قبيل الانقسام الوهمي والفرضي بدليل قولهم: إن الجزء ما لا ينقسم لا كسراً ولا قطعاً، ولا وهماً ولا فرضاً، من غير تعرض لما يكون باختلاف عرضين، وذلك للقطع بأن الجسم الذي يتسخن بعضه، أو وقع الضوء على بعضه، أو لاقى ببعضه جسماً آخر، لم يحصل فيه الانفصال بالفعل، وبحسب الخارج ولم يصر جسمين، ثم إذا زال التسخن أو الضوء أو الملاقاة عاد جسماً واحداً. ولو كان كذلك لكانت المسافة تصير أقساماً غير متناهية في الخارج بحسب موافاة المتحرك حدوداً، ثم تعود متصلة في نفسها واحدة في ذاتها عند انقطاع الحركة، وما يقال إنا قاطعون بأن محل البياض من الجسم غير محل السواد منه مسلم، لكن باعتبار اختلاف العرضين لا بالنظر إلى ذات الجسم بحيث يعرض له
He said: 'Then they refuted that the body [consists] of parts that are not divisible, in imagination not in actuality, because when they [the parts] were equal in nature according to their claim, it is permissible for each what is permissible for the whole according to essence, even if it is impossible due to an accidental individuation or other.' He indicates the refutation of what Democritus and a group of the ancients held, that what is observed of individual bodies like water, for example, are not absolutely simple, but rather they arise from the contact of small simples similar in nature, extremely hard, not susceptible to separative division, but only imaginary, and by this and by naming them bodies this doctrine is distinguished from the doctrine of those who affirm the atom. Its exposition is that when those parts were similar in nature according to their admission, it is permissible for each what is permissible for the others and for the aggregate resulting from their combination, and separative division is not permissible for the aggregate, so it is permissible for each part, for if it were impossible for the part with respect to its essence, it would be impossible for the aggregate. Then the possibility of separation with respect to essence does not contradict its impossibility due to an accidental individuation or other, such as specific forms or extreme smallness or hardness or lack of a cutting tool or the like, so the objection of the Imam does not apply: that bodily extensions are not remaining at separation and are abstracted at conjunction, so they are individuated things, and perhaps they prevent the common essence from its act. As for his objection by denying the equality of bodies in essence, it is not repelled by saying that the basis of the discourse is on their admission that those simples are equal in nature, because his meaning — as he stated in al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya — is that if someone claimed that they are different in essence and that there exist no two parts united in essence, it would not be established that every body is susceptible to division and separation, so the proof for establishing matter would not be complete. But it is not hidden that this is a remote possibility, because the discourse concerns the single body in which difference of nature is not conceivable. And on this should be carried the statement of whoever said that division by its kinds produces in the divided a duality equal in nature of each one to the nature of the aggregate — [this applies] to the division occurring on the single body, otherwise its corruption is clear. And he interpreted 'nature' (ṭabā‘i‘) as the source of the primary essential attribute of a thing, whether motion or rest or other, so it is more general than nature (ṭabī‘a). And he interpreted the kinds of division as being according to tearing and cutting, or according to imagination and supposition, or according to difference of two accidents, stable — i.e. what belongs to the subject in itself like blackness and whiteness — or non-stable — i.e. what belongs to it in relation to another like contact and juxtaposition. That is because if division leads to separation, then the first [kind]; otherwise if it is in mere imagination, then the second; otherwise then the third. And by what we mentioned of considering mere imagination, this became a third kind; otherwise it is of the type of imaginary and suppositional division, as evidenced by their saying: 'The part is that which does not divide, not by breaking, not by cutting, not by imagination, not by supposition,' without addressing what is due to difference of two accidents. That is because it is certain that the body which is heated in some part, or light falls on some part, or it touches with some part another body, does not undergo actual separation externally and does not become two bodies; then when the heat or light or contact ceases, it returns as one body. And if it were like that, the distance would become infinite parts externally according to the coincidence of the moving body with limits, then it would return continuous in itself, one in its essence, when motion ceases. And what is said: 'We are certain that the locus of whiteness in the body is other than the locus of blackness' is granted, but by consideration of the difference of the accidents, not by looking at the essence of the body such that it occurs to it.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the atomist doctrine (that bodies are composed of indivisible parts) with responses to objections. It begins by quoting the author's statement that the refutation targets the view that bodies consist of parts indivisible in actuality but only imaginarible. The commentator (al-Taftazani) then identifies the target as Democritus and some ancients who held that observable bodies like water are not absolutely simple but arise from minute, hard, similar simples that cannot be actually divided—only imaginarily—distinguishing this from the view of those who affirm the atom (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazzā). The argument (taqrīr) proceeds: since these simples are admitted to be similar in nature, whatever is permissible for the aggregate is permissible for each part. Separative division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya) is not permissible for the aggregate (since the aggregate endures as one body), so it must be permissible for each part—otherwise the impossibility would apply to the aggregate as well. However, the possibility of separation by essence does not contradict its impossibility due to accidental factors like specific forms, extreme smallness, hardness, or lack of a cutting tool. This fends off the first objection by Imam al-Razi that bodily extensions are not persistent during separation and are abstract during connection, thus they are individuated and might prevent the common essence from causing division. The second objection denies the premise of equality of bodies in essence. The commentator responds that even if one denies equality, the argument still works for a single body where variation of nature is inconceivable; furthermore, the statement that division produces a duality equal in nature of each part to the whole should be understood as applying to the single body, not to composites. The passage then clarifies key terms: 'nature' (ṭabā‘i‘) is the source of the primary essential attribute (broader than nature/ṭabī‘a), and 'kinds of division' are three: 1) by tearing/cutting (leading to separation), 2) by imagination/supposition (no separation, merely conceptual), 3) by difference of two accidents (stable like color or non-stable like contact). The third was included as a distinct kind due to considering mere imagination. The passage ends by noting that when we say we are certain that the locus of whiteness differs from that of blackness, that is granted but only with respect to the accidents, not the essence of the body itself.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
قسمة انفكاكية
طبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation denies any possibility of division of atoms.
Correction: The refutation actually argues that division is possible in essence but may be prevented by accidental factors like smallness or hardness.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the impossibility of division for the aggregate imply the possibility of division for each part?
A) Because the parts are different in nature.
B) Because the parts are similar in nature, so what holds for the aggregate holds for each part.
C) Because the aggregate is not real.
D) Because division is only imaginary.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of atomism hinges on the premise of equal nature: if all parts are alike, what applies to the whole applies to each part, so indivisibility of the whole would be contradicted unless accidental impediments exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that something can be impossible in actuality but possible in essence due to accidental factors. What is one thing in your spiritual life that you have treated as essentially impossible (e.g., giving up a sin, establishing a habit of prayer, forgiving someone) that may only be accidentally difficult? What accidental impediment (e.g., laziness, pride, fear) can you identify and begin to remove this week?
?
If all parts are alike in nature, then what applies to the whole applies to each part. This logic refutes the atomist's central claim. Is there a belief about yourself you hold that seems indivisible—something you think you can never change—that, if examined by the same logic, might be false? What would it mean to treat that belief as potentially divisible (changeable) by addressing the accidental factors that seem to cement it?
?
The refutation uses the atomist's own admission against them. Are you willing to let the truth—even when it comes from an unexpected source—expose a flaw in your worldview? What ‘admission’ from your own life (an experience, a fact, a criticism) have you been resisting that could overturn a cherished but false assumption?

Responding to al-Razi's Objections
Responses to al-Razi's objections about extensions and essence equality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani answers al-Razi's two objections: the first concerning bodily extensions, and the second about the equality of bodies in essence.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's objections are deflected by clarifying scope and the role of accidental factors.

After presenting the core refutation, al-Taftazani addresses two specific objections raised by Imam al-Razi. The first objection challenges the persistence of extensions during separation, and the second denies the premise that all bodies are equal in essence. The responses show the resilience of the refutation by clarifying its assumptions and narrowing its application to single bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and response
The first objection claims that bodily extensions are not remaining at separation and are abstract at conjunction, making them individuated things that might prevent the common essence from acting.
Al-Taftazani responds by restating that possibility by essence is not hindered by accidental individuation.
2
Read the second objection and response
The second objection denies the equality of bodies in essence, arguing that if essences differ, the argument fails.
The response states that even if true, the argument holds for a single body where nature is constant.
3
Understand the remote possibility
Al-Taftazani acknowledges that the objection of differing essences is a remote possibility but not absurd, and notes that the discourse concerns the single body.
This shows the depth of the scholastic debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم إنهم أبطلوا) كون الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ وهماً لا فعلاً بأنها لما كانت متساوية في الطبع بزعمهم جاز على كل ما جاز على الكل بحسب الذات، وإن امتنع بعارض تشخص أو غيره. يشير إلى إبطال ما ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس وجمع من القدماء، من أن ما يشاهد من الأجسام المفردة كالماء مثلاً ليست ببسائط على الإطلاق، بل إنما هي حاصلة من تماس بسائط صغار متشابهة الطبع، في غاية الصلابة، غير قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية، بل الوهمية فقط وبهذا وبتسميتها أجساماً يمتاز هذا المذهب عن مذهب القائلين بالجزء، وتقريره أن تلك الأجزاء لما كانت متشابهة الطبع باعترافهم جاز على كل منها ما جاز على الآخرين، وعلى المجموع الحاصل من اجتماعها، والقسمة الانفكاكية مما لا يجوز على المجموع، فيجوز على كل جزء، إذ لو امتنعت على الجزء نظراً إلى ذاته لامتنعت على المجموع، ثم إمكان الانفكاك نظراً إلى الذات لا ينافي امتناعه لعارض تشخص أو غيره، من صور نوعية أو غاية صغر أو صلابة، أو عدم آلة قطاعة، أو نحو ذلك فلا يرد اعتراض الإمام بأن الامتدادات الجسمية غير باقية عند الانفصال، ومتجردة عند الاتصال، فهي أمور متشخصة، ولعلها تمنع الماهية المشتركة عن فعلها. وأما اعتراضه بمنع تساوي الأجسام في الماهية، فلا يندفع بأن مبنى الكلام على اعترافهم يكون تلك البسائط متساوية في الطبع، لأن مراده على ما صرح به في المباحث المشرقية هو أنه لو ادعى مدع أنها متخالفة بالماهية، وأنه لا يوجد جزآن متحدان في الماهية لم يثبت أن كل جسم قابل للقسمة والانفكاكية، فلم يتم دليل على إثبات الهيولي. لكن لا خفاء في أنه احتمال بعيد لأن الكلام في الجسم المفرد الذي لا يعقل فيه اختلاف طبيعة وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل قول من قال إن القسمة بأنواعها تحدث في المقسوم أثنينية تساوي طباع كل واحد طباع المجموع على القسمة الواردة على الجسم المفرد، وإلا ففساده واضح، وفسر الطباع بمصدر الصفة الذاتية الأولية للشيء حركة أو سكوناً، كان أو غيرهما، فيكون أعم من الطبيعة، وفسر أنواع القسمة بما يكون بحسب الفتك والقطع، أو بحسب الوهم. والفرض، أو بحسب اختلاف عرضين، قارين، أي ما هو للموضوع في نفسه كالسواد والبياض، أو غير قارين أي ما هو له بالقياس إلى الغير كالتماس والتحاذي، وذلك لأن الانقسام إن تأدى إلى الافتراق فالأول، وإلا فإن كان في مجرد الوهم، فالثاني وإلا فالثالث وبما ذكرنا من اعتبار مجرد الوهم صار هذا قسماً ثالثاً، وإلا فهو من قبيل الانقسام الوهمي والفرضي بدليل قولهم: إن الجزء ما لا ينقسم لا كسراً ولا قطعاً، ولا وهماً ولا فرضاً، من غير تعرض لما يكون باختلاف عرضين، وذلك للقطع بأن الجسم الذي يتسخن بعضه، أو وقع الضوء على بعضه، أو لاقى ببعضه جسماً آخر، لم يحصل فيه الانفصال بالفعل، وبحسب الخارج ولم يصر جسمين، ثم إذا زال التسخن أو الضوء أو الملاقاة عاد جسماً واحداً. ولو كان كذلك لكانت المسافة تصير أقساماً غير متناهية في الخارج بحسب موافاة المتحرك حدوداً، ثم تعود متصلة في نفسها واحدة في ذاتها عند انقطاع الحركة، وما يقال إنا قاطعون بأن محل البياض من الجسم غير محل السواد منه مسلم، لكن باعتبار اختلاف العرضين لا بالنظر إلى ذات الجسم بحيث يعرض له
He said: 'Then they refuted that the body [consists] of parts that are not divisible, in imagination not in actuality, because when they [the parts] were equal in nature according to their claim, it is permissible for each what is permissible for the whole according to essence, even if it is impossible due to an accidental individuation or other.' Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the atomist doctrine (that bodies are composed of indivisible parts) with responses to objections. It begins by quoting the author's statement that the refutation targets the view that bodies consist of parts indivisible in actuality but only imaginarible. The commentator (al-Taftazani) then identifies the target as Democritus and some ancients who held that observable bodies like water are not absolutely simple but arise from minute, hard, similar simples that cannot be actually divided—only imaginarily—distinguishing this from the view of those who affirm the atom (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazzā). The argument (taqrīr) proceeds: since these simples are admitted to be similar in nature, whatever is permissible for the aggregate is permissible for each part. Separative division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya) is not permissible for the aggregate (since the aggregate endures as one body), so it must be permissible for each part—otherwise the impossibility would apply to the aggregate as well. However, the possibility of separation by essence does not contradict its impossibility due to accidental factors like specific forms, extreme smallness, hardness, or lack of a cutting tool. This fends off the first objection by Imam al-Razi that bodily extensions are not persistent during separation and are abstract during connection, thus they are individuated and might prevent the common essence from causing division. The second objection denies the premise of equality of bodies in essence. The commentator responds that even if one denies equality, the argument still works for a single body where variation of nature is inconceivable; furthermore, the statement that division produces a duality equal in nature of each part to the whole should be understood as applying to the single body, not to composites. The passage then clarifies key terms: 'nature' (ṭabā‘i‘) is the source of the primary essential attribute (broader than nature/ṭabī‘a), and 'kinds of division' are three: 1) by tearing/cutting (leading to separation), 2) by imagination/supposition (no separation, merely conceptual), 3) by difference of two accidents (stable like color or non-stable like contact). The third was included as a distinct kind due to considering mere imagination. The passage ends by noting that when we say we are certain that the locus of whiteness differs from that of blackness, that is granted but only with respect to the accidents, not the essence of the body itself.

Checkpoint
What is al-Taftazani's main defense against al-Razi's second objection about equality of essences?
A) He admits the objection destroys the argument.
B) He says the argument only applies to a single body, where nature is uniform.
C) He argues that all bodies are indeed equal in essence.
D) He dismisses the objection as irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani effectively defends the refutation by narrowing its scope to the single body and reaffirming that accidental factors do not negate essential possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When doubts about your faith arise, do you engage with them precisely as al-Taftazani did, or do you dismiss them too quickly? What would it cost you to truly examine one objection today?
?
Al-Razi's objections were built on assumptions about individuation. What assumptions in your own life might be keeping you from accepting a truth that is right in front of you? Will you challenge them this week?
?
The passage teaches that accidental factors do not change essential realities. In what area of your life have you allowed external circumstances—people, habits, or fears—to obscure your core beliefs? What is one concrete step you can take to realign your actions with that essence?

Types of Division and Key Terminology
Classification of division and definition of nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three kinds of division distinguished by kalam scholars and the definition of 'nature' in this context.

Core Idea
Division is of three kinds: by cutting, by imagination, and by difference of accidents.

The final part of the passage is a terminological clarification. The commentator defines 'nature' (ṭabā‘i‘) as the source of primary essential attributes, and then classifies division into three types: that which leads to actual separation, that which is merely imaginary, and that which results from the difference of two accidents (stable or unstable). This framework is essential for understanding the atomist debate.

Guided Path
1
Learn the definition of 'nature'
The passage defines 'nature' (ṭabā‘i‘) as the source of the primary essential attribute of a thing, whether motion or rest, broader than the term 'nature' (ṭabī‘a).
This is a technical clarification for the argument.
2
Identify the three kinds of division
The passage lists three kinds: (1) by tearing/cutting leading to separation, (2) by imagination/supposition, (3) by difference of two accidents (stable like color or unstable like contact).
The third kind is highlighted as a distinct category.
3
Understand the example of heat and light
The passage uses the example of a body that is heated or lit on one part: it does not separate, and when the accident ceases it returns to one body.
This illustrates division by difference of accidents without actual separation.
4
Reflect on the locus of whiteness and blackness
The passage ends by noting that the certainty that the locus of whiteness differs from that of blackness is due to the accidents, not the essence of the body.
This ties back to the distinction between essential and accidental properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم إنهم أبطلوا) كون الجسم من أجزاء لا تتجزأ وهماً لا فعلاً بأنها لما كانت متساوية في الطبع بزعمهم جاز على كل ما جاز على الكل بحسب الذات، وإن امتنع بعارض تشخص أو غيره. يشير إلى إبطال ما ذهب إليه ديمقراطيس وجمع من القدماء، من أن ما يشاهد من الأجسام المفردة كالماء مثلاً ليست ببسائط على الإطلاق، بل إنما هي حاصلة من تماس بسائط صغار متشابهة الطبع، في غاية الصلابة، غير قابلة للقسمة الانفكاكية، بل الوهمية فقط وبهذا وبتسميتها أجساماً يمتاز هذا المذهب عن مذهب القائلين بالجزء، وتقريره أن تلك الأجزاء لما كانت متشابهة الطبع باعترافهم جاز على كل منها ما جاز على الآخرين، وعلى المجموع الحاصل من اجتماعها، والقسمة الانفكاكية مما لا يجوز على المجموع، فيجوز على كل جزء، إذ لو امتنعت على الجزء نظراً إلى ذاته لامتنعت على المجموع، ثم إمكان الانفكاك نظراً إلى الذات لا ينافي امتناعه لعارض تشخص أو غيره، من صور نوعية أو غاية صغر أو صلابة، أو عدم آلة قطاعة، أو نحو ذلك فلا يرد اعتراض الإمام بأن الامتدادات الجسمية غير باقية عند الانفصال، ومتجردة عند الاتصال، فهي أمور متشخصة، ولعلها تمنع الماهية المشتركة عن فعلها. وأما اعتراضه بمنع تساوي الأجسام في الماهية، فلا يندفع بأن مبنى الكلام على اعترافهم يكون تلك البسائط متساوية في الطبع، لأن مراده على ما صرح به في المباحث المشرقية هو أنه لو ادعى مدع أنها متخالفة بالماهية، وأنه لا يوجد جزآن متحدان في الماهية لم يثبت أن كل جسم قابل للقسمة والانفكاكية، فلم يتم دليل على إثبات الهيولي. لكن لا خفاء في أنه احتمال بعيد لأن الكلام في الجسم المفرد الذي لا يعقل فيه اختلاف طبيعة وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل قول من قال إن القسمة بأنواعها تحدث في المقسوم أثنينية تساوي طباع كل واحد طباع المجموع على القسمة الواردة على الجسم المفرد، وإلا ففساده واضح، وفسر الطباع بمصدر الصفة الذاتية الأولية للشيء حركة أو سكوناً، كان أو غيرهما، فيكون أعم من الطبيعة، وفسر أنواع القسمة بما يكون بحسب الفتك والقطع، أو بحسب الوهم. والفرض، أو بحسب اختلاف عرضين، قارين، أي ما هو للموضوع في نفسه كالسواد والبياض، أو غير قارين أي ما هو له بالقياس إلى الغير كالتماس والتحاذي، وذلك لأن الانقسام إن تأدى إلى الافتراق فالأول، وإلا فإن كان في مجرد الوهم، فالثاني وإلا فالثالث وبما ذكرنا من اعتبار مجرد الوهم صار هذا قسماً ثالثاً، وإلا فهو من قبيل الانقسام الوهمي والفرضي بدليل قولهم: إن الجزء ما لا ينقسم لا كسراً ولا قطعاً، ولا وهماً ولا فرضاً، من غير تعرض لما يكون باختلاف عرضين، وذلك للقطع بأن الجسم الذي يتسخن بعضه، أو وقع الضوء على بعضه، أو لاقى ببعضه جسماً آخر، لم يحصل فيه الانفصال بالفعل، وبحسب الخارج ولم يصر جسمين، ثم إذا زال التسخن أو الضوء أو الملاقاة عاد جسماً واحداً. ولو كان كذلك لكانت المسافة تصير أقساماً غير متناهية في الخارج بحسب موافاة المتحرك حدوداً، ثم تعود متصلة في نفسها واحدة في ذاتها عند انقطاع الحركة، وما يقال إنا قاطعون بأن محل البياض من الجسم غير محل السواد منه مسلم، لكن باعتبار اختلاف العرضين لا بالنظر إلى ذات الجسم بحيث يعرض له
He said: 'Then they refuted that the body [consists] of parts that are not divisible, in imagination not in actuality, because when they [the parts] were equal in nature according to their claim, it is permissible for each what is permissible for the whole according to essence, even if it is impossible due to an accidental individuation or other.' He indicates the refutation of what Democritus and a group of the ancients held, that what is observed of individual bodies like water, for example, are not absolutely simple, but rather they arise from the contact of small simples similar in nature, extremely hard, not susceptible to separative division, but only imaginary, and by this and by naming them bodies this doctrine is distinguished from the doctrine of those who affirm the atom. Its exposition is that when those parts were similar in nature according to their admission, it is permissible for each what is permissible for the others and for the aggregate resulting from their combination, and separative division is not permissible for the aggregate, so it is permissible for each part, for if it were impossible for the part with respect to its essence, it would be impossible for the aggregate. Then the possibility of separation with respect to essence does not contradict its impossibility due to an accidental individuation or other, such as specific forms or extreme smallness or hardness or lack of a cutting tool or the like, so the objection of the Imam does not apply: that bodily extensions are not remaining at separation and are abstracted at conjunction, so they are individuated things, and perhaps they prevent the common essence from its act. As for his objection by denying the equality of bodies in essence, it is not repelled by saying that the basis of the discourse is on their admission that those simples are equal in nature, because his meaning — as he stated in al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya — is that if someone claimed that they are different in essence and that there exist no two parts united in essence, it would not be established that every body is susceptible to division and separation, so the proof for establishing matter would not be complete. But it is not hidden that this is a remote possibility, because the discourse concerns the single body in which difference of nature is not conceivable. And on this should be carried the statement of whoever said that division by its kinds produces in the divided a duality equal in nature of each one to the nature of the aggregate — [this applies] to the division occurring on the single body, otherwise its corruption is clear. And he interpreted 'nature' (ṭabā‘i‘) as the source of the primary essential attribute of a thing, whether motion or rest or other, so it is more general than nature (ṭabī‘a). And he interpreted the kinds of division as being according to tearing and cutting, or according to imagination and supposition, or according to difference of two accidents, stable — i.e. what belongs to the subject in itself like blackness and whiteness — or non-stable — i.e. what belongs to it in relation to another like contact and juxtaposition. That is because if division leads to separation, then the first [kind]; otherwise if it is in mere imagination, then the second; otherwise then the third. And by what we mentioned of considering mere imagination, this became a third kind; otherwise it is of the type of imaginary and suppositional division, as evidenced by their saying: 'The part is that which does not divide, not by breaking, not by cutting, not by imagination, not by supposition,' without addressing what is due to difference of two accidents. That is because it is certain that the body which is heated in some part, or light falls on some part, or it touches with some part another body, does not undergo actual separation externally and does not become two bodies; then when the heat or light or contact ceases, it returns as one body. And if it were like that, the distance would become infinite parts externally according to the coincidence of the moving body with limits, then it would return continuous in itself, one in its essence, when motion ceases. And what is said: 'We are certain that the locus of whiteness in the body is other than the locus of blackness' is granted, but by consideration of the difference of the accidents, not by looking at the essence of the body such that it occurs to it.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the atomist doctrine (that bodies are composed of indivisible parts) with responses to objections. It begins by quoting the author's statement that the refutation targets the view that bodies consist of parts indivisible in actuality but only imaginarible. The commentator (al-Taftazani) then identifies the target as Democritus and some ancients who held that observable bodies like water are not absolutely simple but arise from minute, hard, similar simples that cannot be actually divided—only imaginarily—distinguishing this from the view of those who affirm the atom (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazzā). The argument (taqrīr) proceeds: since these simples are admitted to be similar in nature, whatever is permissible for the aggregate is permissible for each part. Separative division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya) is not permissible for the aggregate (since the aggregate endures as one body), so it must be permissible for each part—otherwise the impossibility would apply to the aggregate as well. However, the possibility of separation by essence does not contradict its impossibility due to accidental factors like specific forms, extreme smallness, hardness, or lack of a cutting tool. This fends off the first objection by Imam al-Razi that bodily extensions are not persistent during separation and are abstract during connection, thus they are individuated and might prevent the common essence from causing division. The second objection denies the premise of equality of bodies in essence. The commentator responds that even if one denies equality, the argument still works for a single body where variation of nature is inconceivable; furthermore, the statement that division produces a duality equal in nature of each part to the whole should be understood as applying to the single body, not to composites. The passage then clarifies key terms: 'nature' (ṭabā‘i‘) is the source of the primary essential attribute (broader than nature/ṭabī‘a), and 'kinds of division' are three: 1) by tearing/cutting (leading to separation), 2) by imagination/supposition (no separation, merely conceptual), 3) by difference of two accidents (stable like color or non-stable like contact). The third was included as a distinct kind due to considering mere imagination. The passage ends by noting that when we say we are certain that the locus of whiteness differs from that of blackness, that is granted but only with respect to the accidents, not the essence of the body itself.

Key Terms
طبع
عرض قار
عرض غير قار
قسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many kinds of division are distinguished in this passage?
A) Two: by cutting and by imagination.
B) Three: by cutting, by imagination, and by accident difference.
C) Four: by cutting, by imagination, by accident, and by essence.
D) One: only by imagination.

Key Takeaway
The threefold classification of division clarifies that atoms may be indivisible by cutting but divisible by accident difference, which is key to the atomism debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own body, do you perceive it as a single continuous whole, or do you recognize the different 'parts' defined by accidents like temperature, color, or touch? How does this classification challenge your intuitive sense of unity and division?
?
The passage defines 'nature' more broadly than you might have assumed, including both motion and rest. Does your concept of 'nature' encompass both? How might expanding your definition change how you see the world and your place in it?
?
If you were to apply this classification to your own spiritual or intellectual life, what divisions are merely imaginary, and which are due to real differences in 'accidents'? What would it take to reunite what has been only apparently divided?

Proof for the Persisting Substratum (Hayula)
This passage proves that a persisting substratum (hayula) remains when continuity changes to discontinuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a persisting substratum (hayula) must exist when continuity changes to discontinuity.

Core Idea
A substratum remains as pure potentiality when continuity breaks.

This passage presents a crucial proof in Islamic metaphysics: when a connected body separates, something must persist to account for the difference between annihilation and separation. The author uses a water example to show that the persisting thing is hyle (hayula), described as pure potentiality, not a fixed substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the contrast between annihilation and separation.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on the distinction between a body being entirely annihilated and two new bodies arising vs. a body being separated into two. This distinction forces the existence of a persisting thing.
The water jug example illustrates this.
3
Understand the nature of hyle
The passage describes hyle as pure potentiality (isti'dad mahd). It is not inherently one or many, but receives unity or multiplicity from the continuous form or its removal.
This counters the misconception that hyle is a fixed substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لاخفاء في أن تلك الهوية الاتصالية لا تبقى بنفسها (۱) عند طريان الانفصال، بل تنعدم ويحدث هويتان أخريان مع القطع بأنه يبقى في حالتي الاتصال والانفصال أمر واحد وهو القابل لهما بالذات للفرق الضروري بين أن ينعدم جسم بكليته ، ويحدث جسمان آخران، أو ينعدم جسمان ويحدث جسم ثالث، وبين أن ينفصل جسم فيصير جسمين، أو يتصل جسمان فيصير جسماً واحداً، كماء الجرة يجعل في كيزان أو ماء الكيزان يجعل في جرة ، فذلك أن (۲) الأمر الباقي في الحالين هو المراد بالهيولي ، وهو استعداد محض ليس في نفسه بواحد، ومتصل ليمتنع طريان الكثرة والانفصال عليه ، مع بقائه بحاله ولا كثير ومنفصل ليمتنع طريان الاتصال عليه، بل وحدته واتصاله بحلول الصورة الاتصالية فيه، وانفصاله وكثرته بطريان الانفصال عليه .
Then there is no concealment that that continuous identity does not remain by itself (1) when discontinuity occurs, but rather is annihilated and two other identities arise, while it is definitively known that in the two states of continuity and discontinuity there remains one thing which is receptive of them by itself, due to the necessary distinction between a body being entirely annihilated and two other bodies arising, or two bodies being annihilated and a third body arising, and between a body being separated and becoming two bodies, or two bodies being connected and becoming one body, like water from a jug put into small pitchers or water from small pitchers put into a jug. For the thing remaining in the two states is what is meant by hyle, and it is pure potentiality, not in itself one and continuous so as to prevent the occurrence of multiplicity and discontinuity upon it while it remains as it is, nor many and discrete so as to prevent the occurrence of continuity upon it; rather, its unity and continuity are due to the inherence of the continuous form in it, and its discontinuity and multiplicity are due to the occurrence of discontinuity upon it.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of a persisting substratum (hayula) during change of continuity. The argument begins by stating that the continuous identity (huwiyya ittisaliyya) does not remain itself when discontinuity occurs; it is annihilated and two new identities arise. However, we know necessarily that there is something that remains in both states—the state of continuity and the state of discontinuity—which is the receptive principle (al-qabil). This is necessary because there is a fundamental distinction between (a) the annihilation of one body and the creation of two new bodies, and (b) the separation of one body into two. In the latter, something persists. The example of water from a jug into small pitchers illustrates that the same water persists despite changes in container. This persisting thing is hyle (hayula), described as pure potentiality (isti'dad mahd). It is not in itself one or many, but it receives unity and continuity from the inherence of the continuous form, and receives multiplicity and discontinuity when that occurs.

Key Terms
الهوية الاتصالية
الهيولي
استعداد محض
الصورة الاتصالية
القابل
الانفصال
الاتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The persisting substratum is a substance with fixed properties.
Correction: The passage describes hyle as pure potentiality (isti'dad mahd), not a fixed substance. It is not inherently one or many but receives these attributes from the continuous form.

Checkpoint
What is the persisting thing that remains during change of continuity called?
A) The continuous form
B) The corporeal form
C) Hyle (hayula)
D) The mathematical body

Key Takeaway
The persisting substratum (hayula) is pure potentiality that receives unity or multiplicity from the inherence or removal of the continuous form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a major change in your life—such as a shift in identity, loss, or transformation—what do you sense remains constant? Could that constant be your pure potentiality before God, or do you cling to a fixed version of yourself? How can you live more intentionally as pure potentiality shaped by divine forms?
?
This passage teaches that the substratum is not inherently one or many. Are you attached to being 'one' person or having a singular narrative, afraid of multiplicity and change? What would it mean to surrender your fixed self-image and accept that your core is pure potentiality, receiving forms from Allah? Give up one label today that you have been holding onto.
?
The water from the jug remains the same when poured into separate pitchers. Is your soul the same 'substance' when you are in different modes—praying, working, sinning, repenting? If you believe something persists, how does that affect your accountability and hope for change? Commit to one act today that recognizes your persisting core while seeking a better form.

Second Objection: Extension as a Single Nature
Second objection: extension is a single nature, so cannot be both substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second objection claims that extension cannot be both substance and accident, and how it challenges the proof for hyle.

Core Idea
Extension is one nature — it cannot be split into substance and accident.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents the second objection against the proof for hyle. The objector argues that extension (al-imitidād) is a single, uniform nature. Therefore, it is logically impossible for some instances of extension to be substance and others accident. This objection forces us to examine whether extension can have both substantial and accidental modes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text once, then its literal translation. Notice the concise style and the logical structure: premise, conclusion.
2
Understand the key premise
Identify the claim that extension is a single nature. Why does the objector think this is a problem? Consider what it means for a nature to be uniform.
The term 'طبيعة' (nature) refers to the essence or quiddity of extension.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection concludes that there is no substance other than the body itself. Think about how this conclusion undermines the proof for a distinct hyle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الامتداد طبيعة واحدة فيمتنع كون بعض أفراده أو أصنافه جوهراً والبعض عرضاً، وإن وقع الاصطلاح على تسمية بعض الجواهر بذلك. فلا نسلّم أن في المتميز جوهراً غير نفس الجسم .
Second: That extension is a single nature, so it is impossible for some of its individuals or species to be substance and some accident, even if convention has arisen of calling some substances by that name. So we do not concede that in that which is distinguished there is a substance other than the body itself.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is the second objection (الوجه الثاني) against the proof for hyle. The objector argues that extension is a single nature (طبيعة واحدة). Therefore, it cannot have some of its instances or types be substances and others accidents. Even if by convention one calls some substances 'extension,' that does not change the nature. The conclusion is that there is no substance other than the body itself that is distinguished as extension. This objection assumes that extension as a universal category is uniform, so it cannot be divided into substantial and accidental kinds.

Key Terms
امتداد
طبيعة
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Extension can be both substance and accident in different contexts
Correction: The objection insists that because extension is a single nature, it cannot be divided into substantial and accidental instances. This is a metaphysical claim about uniformity of essence.

Checkpoint
What is the premise of the second objection?
A) Extension is multiple natures.
B) Extension is a single nature.
C) Extension is an accident.
D) Extension is a substance.

Key Takeaway
The second objection asserts that extension's single nature precludes it from being both substance and accident, undermining the notion of a separate substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is called by different names in different contexts, it must have a divided nature? How does this objection challenge that assumption in your own thinking?
?
In your life, do you treat your own nature—your soul's essence—as something that can be both good and evil depending on circumstances? How does the objector's insistence on a single nature force you to reconsider the consistency of your character before Allah?
?
When you hear an argument that uses linguistic conventions to justify a metaphysical position, do you accept it uncritically? This objection warns against confusing terminology with reality. Where in your faith or daily practice might you be doing the same, and what step can you take today to align your understanding with the truth?

Temporal Origination Does Not Necessitate Annihilation
The author argues that hadath implies imkan only, not actual annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal origination (ḥudūth) does not necessitate annihilation (fanā'), and how the concept of impossibility by another (imtināʿ bi al-ghayr) plays a key role.

Core Idea
Temporal origination implies possibility, not certainty, of non-existence.

This passage addresses a classic theological question: Do things that come into existence eventually have to cease to exist? Some argued that temporal origination (ḥudūth) logically leads to annihilation (fanā'). The author counters this by distinguishing between possibility in essence (imkān bi al-dhāt) and impossibility by another (imtināʿ bi al-ghayr). Here, he lays out his reasoning, drawing on Quranic evidence, rational arguments, and responses to objections. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the Quranic citation and the technical terms used.
2
Identify the core claim
Find where the author states what temporal origination (ḥudūth) implies. Pay attention to the phrase 'إنما يقتضي إمكان العدم بالذات'.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between possibility in essence (imkān bi al-dhāt) and impossibility by another (imtināʿ bi al-ghayr). Ask yourself how this distinction allows for the possibility of eternal existence.
This is the crux of the argument.
4
Explore the arguments and responses
The author presents arguments for the possibility of non-existence based on ḥudūth and imkān, then responds to objections. Work through each part and see how it reinforces the main thesis.
The response about 'impossibility by another' is repeated to emphasize the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكونها حادثة على ما سيأتي، ولقوله تعالى كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه وهلاك البسيط لا يتصور إلا بفنائه، ولا يخفي أن الحدوث إنما يقتضي إمكان العدم بالذات، وهو لا ينافي امتناعه بالغير وهو المتنازع . فإن استروح الى أن الحكم هو الإمكان حتى يثبت ما به الامتناع كان ذكر الحدوث مستدركا . أنا أي من أحكام الأجسام أنها باقية زمانين وأكثر بحكم الضرورة بمعنى نعلم بالضرورة أن كتبنا وثيابنا وبيوتنا ودوابنا هي بعينها التي كانت من غير تبدل في الذوات، بل إن كان ففي العوارض والهيئات، لا بمعنى أن الحس يشاهدها باقية ليرد الاعتراض بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك بتجدد الأمثال كما في الأعراض، وقد يفهم من البقاء الدوام وامتناع الفناء، وعليه يحمل ما قال في التجريد أن الضرورة قاضية ببقاء الأجسام وبين بأن غاية أمرها التفرق والانقسام، وهو لا يوجب الانعدام. وأنت خبير بأن دعوى الضرورة في ذلك في غاية الفساد. كيف وقد صرح بجوازه في بحث المعاد واستدل على جواز العدم تارة بالحدوث، فإن العدم السابق كالعدم اللاحق لعدم التمايز ، وقد جاز الأول فكذا الثاني، وتارة بالإمكان، فإن معناه جواز كل من الوجود والعدم نظراً إلى الذات. وأجيب بأن هذا لا ينافي الامتناع بالغير على ما هو المتنازع ، فإنه يجوز أن يكون الشيء في ذاته قابلاً للعدم السابق واللاحق جميعاً، ويمتنع أحدهما أو كلاهما لعلة، والحاصل أن الحدوث لا ينافي الأبدية كما في النفس الناطقة على رأي أرسطو، والإمكان لا ينافي الأبدية والأزلية كما في القدماء لزمانية دون الذاتية على رأي الفلاسفة ويمكن () أن يقال : إنه لما كان الحكم بجواز العدم نفي ذلك الحكم حتى يقوم دليل الامتناع . لا يقال على هذا لا حاجة إلى اعتبار الحدوث لأن الجسم ممكن جائز الوجود والعدم قديماً كان أو حديثاً لأنا نقول هو مراعاة لطرق المتكلمين حيث يقسمون الوجود إلى القديم والحادث لا الواجب والممكن وبيان لجواز حصول العدم بالفعل وذلك في الحدوث دون مجرد الإمكان.
Because they are temporally originated (ḥāditha) as will come, and from His saying the Exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (Quran 28:88), and the perishing of a simple being cannot be conceived except by its annihilation. And it is not hidden that temporal origination (ḥudūth) only implies possibility (imkān) of non-existence in essence, and it does not contradict its impossibility by another, which is the point of dispute. So if one takes comfort in the fact that the judgment is possibility until what establishes impossibility is proven, then the mention of temporal origination would be superfluous. I mean from the judgments of bodies is that they remain for two times or more by virtue of necessity, meaning that we know necessarily that our books, clothes, houses, and beasts are the very ones that were without any change in essences; rather, if there is change, it is in the accidents and states. Not in the sense that the sense perceives them as permanent, lest the objection be raised that [stated permanence] could be by renewal of similar instances as in accidents. And permanence (baqā') may be understood as perpetuity and the impossibility of annihilation (fanā'), and upon this is carried what was said in al-Tajrīd that necessity judges the permanence of bodies and it is shown that their ultimate fate is disintegration and division, which does not necessitate non-existence. And you are aware that the claim of necessity in that is utterly corrupt. How so? For it has been affirmed regarding its possibility in the discussion of the resurrection, and the possibility of non-existence has been argued sometimes by temporal origination, for prior non-existence is like posterior non-existence due to lack of differentiation, and since the first was possible, so is the second; and sometimes by possibility (imkān), for its meaning is the permissibility of both existence and non-existence with respect to the essence. And it was answered that this does not contradict impossibility by another, as is the point of dispute. For it is possible that a thing is in its essence receptive of both prior and posterior non-existence, and one or both are prevented by a cause. And the conclusion is that temporal origination does not contradict eternity (abadiyya) as in the rational soul according to Aristotle's opinion, and possibility does not contradict eternity and pre-eternity as in the temporal pre-eternals according to the philosophers' opinion, not essential pre-eternals. And it can be said: Since the judgment of the permissibility of non-existence is negated until proof of impossibility is established, it is not said that there is no need to consider temporal origination because the body is possible, permissible of existence and non-existence whether it is ancient or originated, for we say it is out of consideration for the ways of the mutakallimūn, since they divide existence into the ancient (qadīm) and the originated (ḥādith), not into the necessary (wājib) and the possible (mumkin), and it is a demonstration of the actual occurrence of non-existence, and that is in temporal origination, not merely possibility.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument defending the possibility of annihilation (fanā') for bodies against those who claim necessity of permanence (baqā'). The author begins by referencing the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' and notes that simple beings can only perish by annihilation. He then clarifies a key principle: temporal origination (ḥudūth) only implies possibility (imkān) of non-existence in itself (bi al-dhāt), not impossibility of non-existence by another (bi al-ghayr). The dispute is about the latter. If one accepts that the default judgment is possibility until impossibility is proven, then mentioning temporal origination would be redundant. [The text then transitions] The author then discusses the claim that bodies are necessarily observed to remain for two times or more, but notes that this is not a rational necessity and could be due to renewal of similar instances. He further criticizes the claim of necessity. Then he presents arguments for the possibility of non-existence: one based on hadath (since prior non-existence was possible, posterior is similar) and one based on imkan (the essence allows both existence and non-existence). The response to the objection that this contradicts impossibility by another is that a thing may be receptive to non-existence in itself but prevented by an external cause. The author concludes that hadath does not contradict eternity (as in Aristotle's soul) and imkan does not contradict temporal eternity. Finally, he explains that the mutakallimūn consider hadath rather than imkan because they divide existence into qadīm and ḥādith, and they want to show actual occurrence of non-existence which is in hadath only.

Misconception: Some think temporal origination guarantees eventual annihilation.
Correction: The author clarifies that temporal origination only implies possibility of non-existence in essence, not actual necessity.

Checkpoint

B) It only implies the possibility of non-existence in itself.
C) It proves that non-existence is impossible by another.
D) It guarantees eternal existence.

Key Takeaway
Temporal origination only indicates the possibility of non-existence in itself; actual annihilation is prevented if there is an external cause necessitating persistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author argues that temporal origination only implies possibility, not necessity, of annihilation. How does this truth affect your fear of death? Are you more afraid of losing worldly possessions or of standing before God? What change in your daily priorities would reflect the reality that only God's Face is everlasting?
?
You believe that God can sustain anything He wills indefinitely. Does your hope in God's preservation extend to your own faith? What are you doing daily to ensure that your iman is sustained by God, not by worldly circumstances?
