The Proof of Contact for the Finitude of Dimensions
Taftazani presents a logical proof that dimensions must be finite using a sphere and parallel lines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument known as the 'Proof of Contact' that kalām scholars use to demonstrate that all physical bodies have finite dimensions.

Core Idea
Infinite dimensions lead to logical impossibility, proving all bodies are finite.

Taftazani presents one of the most important arguments in Islamic theology for the finitude of the physical world. The argument uses a thought experiment involving a sphere moving towards an infinite line to show that the assumption of an infinite distance leads to a contradiction. This lesson unpacks the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: a claim, a hypothetical scenario, logical consequences, refutations of denials, an objection, and historical context.
The passage is dense; take your time to follow the logic.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify the key steps: assume an infinite distance, a sphere moves toward it, its diameter tilts to contact the line, this requires a first point of contact, but on an infinite line any point has a previous point, leading to an infinite regress.
The 'true imagination' (الوهم الصادق) plays a role in judging divisibility.
3
Examine the refutations and objection
Notice how Taftazani refutes three denials (of entailment, of the sought, and of the non-existence of the consequent). Then consider the objection from Imam al-Razi and its answer based on pure imagination.
This shows the robustness of the proof against challenges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها أنها متناهية الأبعاد) لوجوه : الأول: أنه لو وجد بعد غير متناه لامكن بالضرورة أن تتحرك إليه كرة فيميل قطرها الموازي له إلى مسامته ويلزم تعين نقطة في الوهم لأولية المسامتة ضرورة حدوثها مع استحالته في الخط الغير المتناهي، لأن كل نقطة تفرض فالمسامتة مع ما فوقها قبل المسامتة معها لأنها لا محالة تكون بزاوية وحركة وكل منهما بحكم الوهم الصادق يقبل الانقسام لا إلى نهاية، والمسامتة بالنصف منهما قبل المسامته بالكل . فعلى هذا سقط منع الملازمة مستنداً بما ذكر في انتفاء اللازم ومنع ثبوت المطلوب مستنداً بأن المحال إنما يلزم من لا تناهي البعد مع الفرض المذكور، ومنع انتفاء اللازم مستنداً بأن انقسام الزاوية والحركة لا إلى نهاية إنما هو بمجرد الوهم، وأما اعتراض الإمام بأن أطول ما يفرض من الخطوط المستقيمة هو محور العالم والمسامتة معه إنما تحصل بعد المسامتة مع نقطة فوقه خارج العالم، وهكذا لا إلى نهاية فيلزم عدم تناهي الأبعاد . فجوابه أن هذا من الوهميات الصرفة . جعل هذا من أحكام الأجسام نظراً إلى أن البعد الجسمي هو المتحقق بلا نزاع بخلاف الخلاء . ونقل القول بلا تناهي الأبعاد عن حكماء الهند وجمع من المتقدمين، وأبي البركات من المتأخرين والمشهور من أدلة المانعين ثلاثة : الأول : برهان المسامتة وتقريره ظاهر من المتن ، وإنما اعتبر حركة الكرة لأن الميل من الموازاة إلى المسامتة هناك في غاية الوضوح لا يتوقف فيه العقل، بل يكاد يشهد به الحس ومعنى موازاة الخطين أن لا يتلاقيا، ولو فرض امتدادهما
He said: And among them [the properties] is that they are finite in dimensions. With [several] proofs: First: that if there existed an infinite distance, then it would be necessarily possible for a sphere to move towards it, so its diameter parallel to it would incline to contact it, and it would necessitate the determination of a point in the imagination for the firstness of the contact by virtue of its occurrence, while it is impossible in the infinite line, because every point that is assumed, the contact with what is above it is before the contact with it, because it inevitably occurs with an angle and a motion, and each of them, by the judgment of the true imagination, is divisible without end, and the contact at half of them is before the contact at the whole. So on this basis, the denial of the entailment is refuted, based on what was mentioned of the non-existence of the consequent, and the denial of the establishment of the sought is refuted, based on that impossibility only follows from the infinitude of the distance with the mentioned assumption, and the denial of the non-existence of the consequent is refuted, based on that the division of the angle and motion without end is only by mere imagination. As for the objection of Imam that the longest that can be assumed of straight lines is the axis of the world, and contact with it only occurs after contact with a point above it outside the world, and so on without end, so the non-finiteness of dimensions would follow. Its answer: that this is from pure imaginings. And he made this from the rulings of bodies considering that bodily distance is actual without dispute, unlike void. And he transmitted the statement of non-finite dimensions from the sages of India, a group of early scholars, and Abu al-Barakat from the later ones. And the well-known proofs of those who deny [infinite dimensions] are three: First: the proof of contact, and its presentation is apparent from the text; and he only considered the motion of the sphere because the inclination from parallelism to contact there is utmost clearness, the intellect does not pause over it, rather sense almost witnesses it. And the meaning of the parallelism of two lines is that they do not meet, even if their extension is assumed.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a detailed proof (برهان) that all bodies have finite dimensions, a key position in kalām against the possibility of actual infinities. It begins by stating that among the properties of bodies is that their dimensions (الأبعاد) are finite. The first proof (الأول) uses a hypothetical sphere moving toward an infinite line. If an infinite distance (بعد غير متناه) existed, a sphere could move toward it; its diameter, parallel to the line, would tilt to become coincident (مسامتة) with it. This would require the imagination (الوهم) to fix a first point of contact—impossible for an infinite line because before any point's contact, there is contact with a point farther up. The angle and motion involved are infinitely divisible in the 'true imagination' (الوهم الصادق), so the half-contact comes before the whole, leading to an infinite regress. The author then refutes three denials: (1) denial of the entailment (منع الملازمة) is refuted by pointing out the impossibility of an infinite consequent; (2) denial of the sought conclusion (منع ثبوت المطلوب) is refuted because the impossibility only follows from the assumption of infinite distance; (3) denial that the consequent fails (منع انتفاء اللازم) is refuted because the infinite divisibility of angle and motion is only imaginary, not actual. Next, an objection from Imam (likely Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) is presented: the longest possible line is the world's axis; contact with it must follow contact with an external point, leading to an infinite regress. The answer (فجوابه) is that this is purely imaginary (من الوهميات الصرفة). Then the author notes that this proof treats bodily dimensions as actual (متحقق) unlike void (خلاء). He reports that the doctrine of infinite dimensions was held by Indian sages, some early scholars, and Abu al-Barakat. The well-known proofs (المشهور من أدلة المانعين) against infinite dimensions are three; the first is the 'proof of contact' (برهان المسامتة), whose presentation is clear from the text. The sphere motion is chosen because the tilt from parallelism to contact is intuitively obvious, almost sensed. Parallelism (موازاة) means the lines never meet, even if extended infinitely.

Key Terms
تناهي الأبعاد
برهان المسامتة
الوهم الصادق
الزاوية
الحركة
اللامتناهي
الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that infinite divisibility in the imagination proves the actual existence of an infinite regress, making the proof invalid.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that the infinite divisibility is only in the 'true imagination' (الوهم الصادق), not in actual reality. The impossibility of a first contact in the imagination suffices to demonstrate the logical contradiction of the original assumption.

Checkpoint
According to the proof of contact, why does assuming an infinite distance lead to an impossibility?
A) Because a sphere cannot move in a straight line.
B) Because it requires determining a first point of contact, which is impossible on an infinite line due to infinite previous contacts.
C) Because the diameter of the sphere is too short.
D) Because the angle of motion becomes zero.

Key Takeaway
The Proof of Contact demonstrates that the assumption of infinite dimensions leads to a logical impossibility, thereby establishing that all bodies must be finite in their dimensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that every dimension of the created world is finite—including your lifespan, your strength, your knowledge. If you truly believed that every aspect of your existence is bounded, how would you change your priorities today? What one finite resource are you wasting as if it were infinite?
?
The proof relies on the 'true imagination' to accept the impossibility of infinite regress. In your own life, do you follow your intellect to logical conclusions even when they are uncomfortable, or do you retreat into 'pure imagination' that lets you avoid accountability? What truth about yourself are you avoiding because admitting it would require change?
?
The author dismisses the objection about the world's axis as mere imagination. Is there a belief or habit in your life that you cling to simply because it is familiar, even though it cannot withstand logical scrutiny? What would it cost you to abandon that fantasy and submit to the truth?

The Proof of Tangency: Refuting Infinite Distances
Al-Taftazani presents the proof of tangency and its core logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic structure of the proof of tangency (برهان المسامتة) and why it shows infinite distances lead to a contradiction.

Core Idea
The first point of tangency cannot exist if distances are infinite.

Al-Taftazani introduces a key argument against infinite distances: the proof of tangency. This proof uses the simple geometry of a rotating sphere and a parallel line to show that assuming infinite distance forces an impossible regress. We will unpack the core reasoning and see how theologians deploy it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Visualize a sphere rotating around its axis. A line parallel to the diameter of the sphere will eventually become tangent to the sphere. This shift from being parallel to tangent requires a first point of tangency.
The proof relies on the transition from parallelism to tangency.
2
Trace the contradiction
If the distance from the sphere to the line is infinite, then before any point of tangency there must be another point, due to the infinite divisibility of the angle and motion. Thus, no first point exists, but tangency must begin somewhere – a contradiction.
3
Identify the entailment
The key logical step (ملازمة) is that the occurrence of tangency after not having been necessitates a first point. Denying this entailment is the main objection Al-Taftazani addresses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطرها الموازي له إلى مسامته (۱) ويلزم تعين نقطة في الوهم لأولية المسامتة ضرورة حدوثها مع استحالته في الخط الغير المتناهي، لأن كل نقطة تفرض فالمسامتة مع ما فوقها قبل المسامتة معها لأنها لا محالة (۲) تكون بزاوية وحركة وكل منهما بحكم الوهم الصادق يقبل الانقسام لا إلى (۳) نهاية، والمسامتة بالنصف منهما قبل المسامته بالكل . فعلى هذا سقط منع الملازمة مستنداً بما ذكر في انتفاء اللازم ومنع ثبوت المطلوب مستنداً بأن المحال إنما يلزم من لا تناهي البعد مع الفرض المذكور، ومنع انتفاء اللازم مستنداً بأن انقسام الزاوية والحركة لا إلى نهاية إنما هو بمجرد الوهم، وأما اعتراض الإمام بأن أطول ما يفرض من الخطوط المستقيمة هو محور العالم والمسامتة معه إنما تحصل بعد المسامتة مع نقطة فوقه خارج العالم، وهكذا لا [؟] إلى نهاية فيلزم عدم تناهي الأبعاد . فجوابه أن هذا من الوهميات الصرفة [؟] (٥) . جعل هذا من أحكام الأجسام نظراً إلى أن البعد الجسمي هو المتحقق بلا نزاع بخلاف الخلاء . ونقل القول بلا تناهي الأبعاد عن حكماء الهند وجمع من المتقدمين، وأبي البركات (1) من المتأخرين والمشهور من أدلة المانعين ثلاثة : الأول : برهان المسامتة وتقريره ظاهر من المتن [؟] (۷) ، وإنما اعتبر حركة الكرة لأن الميل من الموازاة إلى المسامتة هناك في غاية الوضوح لا يتوقف فيه العقل، بل يكاد (۸) يشهد به الحس ومعنى موازاة الخطين أن لا يتلاقيا، ولو فرض امتدادهما لا إلى نهاية، والمسامتة بخلافها، وإنما اعتبر النقطة بحسب الوهم، لأن ثبوتها بالفعل غير لازم ما لم ينقطع الخط بالفعل. وفيما أوردنا من تقرير البرهان (۱) إشارة إلى دفع اعتراضات تورد عليه فمنها منع لزوم أول نقطة المسامتة مستنداً بما ذكرنا في بيان استحالة اللازم وتقريره أنه على تقدير لا تناهي البعد لا يلزم أول نقطة المسامتة ، لأن الحركة والزاوية تنقسمان لا إلى نهاية فقبل كل مسامتة مسامتة (۲) لا إلى أول . ولاخفاء في أن هذا بعد الاحتجاج على الملازمة بأن المسامتة حصلت بعدما لم تكن فيكون لها أول بالضرورة ليس بموجه إلا أن تجعل معارضة في المقدمة ، وجوابها النقض بكل قياس (۳) استثنائي، استثنى فيه نقيض التالي، فإنه لو صح ما ذكر لصح فيه الاستدلال على نفي الملازمة بما يذكر في بيان استحالة اللازم وفساده بين والحل بأن هذا لا ينفي الملازمة، لأن الملزوم محال فجاز استلزامه للنقيضين مثلاً لو وجد بعد غير متناه مع الفرض المذكور لزم ثبوت أول نقطة المسامتة لما ذكرنا . وعدم ثبوته لما ذكرتم على أنه يتجه أن يقال لو وجد البعد مع الفرض المذكور، فإما أن تثبت أول نقطة المسامتة أو لا تثبت ، وكلاهما محال ، لما ذكر فيتم الاحتجاج .
Its diameter parallel to it to its tangency (1), and a point in imagination is necessarily determined for the first tangency, due to its occurrence while it is impossible in the infinite line, because every point assumed has tangency with what is above it before tangency with it, because it inevitably (2) involves an angle and motion, and each of them, according to sound imagination, is divisible ad infinitum (3), and tangency with half of them comes before tangency with the whole. Thus the denial of entailment falls, based on what was mentioned regarding the non-existence of the consequent, and the denial of the establishment of the desired conclusion, based on the impossibility arising only from the infinity of distance with the mentioned assumption, and the denial of the non-existence of the consequent, based on the division of angle and motion ad infinitum being merely by imagination. As for the Imam's objection that the longest assumed straight line is the axis of the world, and tangency with it occurs only after tangency with a point above it outside the world, and so on ad infinitum [؟], thus the non-finiteness of distances follows. Its answer is that this is from pure imaginaries [؟] (5). This is made from the properties of bodies, considering that bodily distance is undeniably actual, unlike void. The view of the infinity of distances is transmitted from the sages of India, a group of the ancients, and Abu al-Barakat (1) among the later scholars. The well-known proofs of the deniers are three: The first is the proof of tangency, and its formulation is apparent from the text [؟] (7), and only the motion of the sphere is considered because the inclination from parallelism to tangency there is extremely clear, reason does not hesitate about it, rather sense almost (8) testifies to it. The meaning of parallelism of two lines is that they do not meet, even if their extension is assumed ad infinitum, and tangency is contrary to it. The point is only considered according to imagination, because its actual existence is not necessary unless the line is actually cut. In what we have presented of the formulation of the proof (1) there is an indication of repelling objections brought against it. Among them is the denial of the necessity of the first point of tangency, based on what we mentioned in the explanation of the impossibility of the consequent, and its formulation is that on the assumption of infinite distance, the first point of tangency does not necessarily follow, because motion and angle are divisible ad infinitum, so before every tangency there is a tangency (2) without first. It is not hidden that this, after arguing for the entailment by saying that tangency occurred after not having been, so it necessarily has a first, is not effective unless it is made a counter-argument in the premise, and its answer is the refutation by every exceptive syllogism (3) in which the contradictory of the consequent is excepted. For if what was mentioned were valid, then the inference denying the entailment would be valid by what is mentioned in the explanation of the impossibility of the consequent, but its falsity is clear. The solution is that this does not negate the entailment, because the antecedent is impossible, so it is possible for it to entail contradictories. For example, if an infinite distance existed with the mentioned assumption, the affirmation of the first point of tangency would necessarily follow according to what we mentioned, and its non-affirmation according to what you mentioned. But it is fitting to say: if distance existed with the mentioned assumption, then either the first point of tangency is affirmed or it is not, and both are impossible for what was mentioned, so the argument is complete.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the proof of tangency (برهان المسامتة) used to refute the possibility of infinite distances (لا تناهي الأبعاد). The argument proceeds as follows: First, the proof is outlined: For a sphere rotating, a line parallel to its diameter will eventually become tangent. The first point of tangency must be determined, but if the line is infinite, such a point cannot be determined because between any two points, there is always another, and the angle and motion involved are divisible without end. This shows that assuming infinite distance leads to a contradiction. The author then addresses the denial of the entailment (منع الملازمة), the denial of the establishment of the desired conclusion (منع ثبوت المطلوب), and the denial of the non-existence of the consequent (منع انتفاء اللازم), each based on the imagination (وهم). An objection from the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) is mentioned: the longest line is the axis of the world, and tangency occurs only after passing through points beyond the world, leading to an infinite regress. The answer is that this is a mere imaginary (وهمية صرفة) not actual. The proof is then explicitly stated as the first of three proofs used by those who deny infinite distances. The motion of the sphere is considered because the transition from parallel to tangent is clearly sensed. The definition of parallel lines is given (they never meet). The point is considered only in imagination because actual points exist only if the line is cut. Finally, the author refines the proof against an objection that denies the necessity of a first point of tangency. The objection claims that because angle and motion are infinitely divisible, there is no first tangency. The author responds that this objection fails because the entailment (ملازمة) stands: if distance is infinite, then both affirming and denying a first point leads to contradiction, thus the original proof is sound.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof depends on actual physical observation of a sphere's rotation.
Correction: The proof is a logical argument using geometry; the sphere is just a model to clarify the concept of tangency.

Checkpoint
What does the proof of tangency conclude about infinite distances?
A) They are possible but unknowable.
B) They lead to a contradiction because a first point of tangency would be impossible.
C) They are only imaginary, not real.
D) They are necessary for the sphere's rotation.

Key Takeaway
The proof of tangency leverages the necessity of a first point to refute infinite distances.

?

Defending the Proof: Objections and Logical Refinements
Al-Taftazani addresses objections and refines the proof's logical structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani defends the proof of tangency against key objections, including the claim that the first point is not necessary and the argument from the axis of the world.

Core Idea
Even if infinite division is imaginary, the impossibility stands because the antecedent is impossible.

Having presented the proof of tangency, Al-Taftazani now addresses more sophisticated objections. The most important is the denial of a first point of tangency based on infinite divisibility. He also answers an objection from Imam al-Razi about the axis of the world. We will see how logical subtlety preserves the proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection from infinite divisibility
The opponent argues that before any tangency there is always another, so no first point exists. This tries to deny the necessity of a first point.
This is a direct challenge to the entailment (ملازمة).
2
See Al-Taftazani's response
He responds that if infinite distance existed, both affirming and denying a first point would be impossible. This shows the antecedent (infinite distance) is impossible, so the entailment holds vacuously.
The key insight: impossible antecedents can entail contradictories.
3
Examine the Imam's objection
Imam al-Razi objects that the longest line is the axis of the world, and tangency with points beyond leads to infinite regress. Al-Taftazani dismisses this as purely imaginary.
He distinguishes between actual bodily distance and imaginary extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اعتراضات تورد عليه فمنها منع لزوم أول نقطة المسامتة مستنداً بما ذكرنا في بيان استحالة اللازم وتقريره أنه على تقدير لا تناهي البعد لا يلزم أول نقطة المسامتة ، لأن الحركة والزاوية تنقسمان لا إلى نهاية فقبل كل مسامتة مسامتة (۲) لا إلى أول . ولاخفاء في أن هذا بعد الاحتجاج على الملازمة بأن المسامتة حصلت بعدما لم تكن فيكون لها أول بالضرورة ليس بموجه إلا أن تجعل معارضة في المقدمة ، وجوابها النقض بكل Its diameter parallel to it to its tangency (1), and a point in imagination is necessarily determined for the first tangency, due to its occurrence while it is impossible in the infinite line, because every point assumed has tangency with what is above it before tangency with it, because it inevitably (2) involves an angle and motion, and each of them, according to sound imagination, is divisible ad infinitum (3), and tangency with half of them comes before tangency with the whole. The First Point of Coincidence: Time and Space
Objection and answer on coincidence's spatial beginning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that coincidence (المسامتة) implies a beginning in both time and space, and why infinite distance is not inherently impossible.

Core Idea
Time, motion, and distance are linked — if distance had no beginning, time would have no beginning.

This lesson presents a classic objection and response. The objector tries to separate time from space, but the author shows they are inseparable. We examine the logical chain and the distinction between absolute and conditional impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the exchange.
Notice the structure: objection (فإن قيل) and answer (قلنا).
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says coincidence only requires a temporal beginning, not a spatial one. Ask yourself: why might someone think that?
The objection is subtle: it accepts a beginning in time but denies a first point in space.
3
Follow the author's first argument
The author points out that time is superimposed on motion, and motion on distance. If distance had no first point, motion and then time would have no beginning — an absurdity.
This argument uses the correspondence of these three things.
4
Understand the second argument
The author clarifies that the impossibility (المحال) arises only under the specific assumption of infinite distance combined with the assumption that coincidence occurs. It does not prove that infinite distance itself is impossible.
This distinguishes between conditional and absolute impossibility.
5
Check the misconception
A common error is to think the argument proves infinite distance is impossible. The author says it may arise from the aggregate, not from infinity alone.
Reflect on why this distinction matters in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : حدوث المسامتة لا يقتضي إلا أن يكون لها بداية بحسب الزمان ، فمن أين تلزم البداية بحسب المسافة ، أعني أول نقطة المسامتة .؟ قلنا : من جهة أن الزمان منطبق على الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة، فلو لم يكن لها أول لم يكن للحركة أول، فلم يكن للزمان أول، ومنها أن المحال إنما لزم على تقدير لا تناهي البعد مع الفرض المذكور وهو لا يستلزم استحالة لا تناهي البعد لجواز أن يكون ناشئاً من المجموع .
If it is said: The occurrence of coincidence does not entail except that it has a beginning with respect to time; so from where does the beginning with respect to distance follow, i.e., the first point of coincidence? We say: Because time is superimposed on motion which is superimposed on distance; so if it had no beginning, motion would have no beginning, and then time would have no beginning. And also because the impossibility only follows on the supposition of infinite distance with the mentioned assumption, and it does not entail the impossibility of infinite distance in itself, because it may arise from the aggregate.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer exchange. The objector argues that the occurrence of coincidence (المسامتة) only requires a beginning in time, not necessarily a beginning in space (i.e., a first point of distance). The author responds by pointing out the correspondence between time, motion, and distance: time is superimposed on motion, and motion is superimposed on distance. Therefore, if distance had no first point, motion would have no beginning, and consequently time would have no beginning—an absurdity. Additionally, the author clarifies that the impossibility (المحال) deduced from the assumption of infinite distance in this specific scenario does not prove that infinite distance is inherently impossible; rather, the impossibility arises from the aggregate of assumptions, not from infinite distance alone.

Correction: The author argues that the impossibility only arises under the specific assumption of infinite distance with the condition that coincidence occurs, so it does not establish the absolute impossibility of infinite distance.

Checkpoint

A) Coincidence requires a spatial beginning.
B) Coincidence only requires a temporal beginning, not a spatial one.
C) Coincidence proves the infinity of dimensions.

Key Takeaway
Coincidence requires a spatial beginning because time, motion, and distance are linked, but infinite distance is not inherently impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author carefully avoids concluding that infinite distance is absolutely impossible, showing restraint in reasoning. When have you jumped to a conclusion about something being 'impossible' when the real issue was the combination of assumptions you carried? Are you willing to examine the 'aggregate' of your own assumptions today?
?
Time, motion, and distance are tied together in this argument. Your daily life moves through time and space—your schedule, your journey, your habits. How does knowing that every motion and every distance has a first point change the way you think about the source of your actions? Will you trace your own motion back to its true beginning today?

Distinguishing Sound from False Imagination
Objection-answer on the role of imagination in geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between sound geometric imagination and false purely imaginary judgments, and why the objector's reliance on the unreliability of imagination is invalid in geometry.

Core Idea
Geometric imagination is sound; only applying sensory rules to intelligibles is false.

The objector denies the need for a first point in an infinite line, claiming that the division of motion ad infinitum is a judgment of imagination and therefore unreliable. The author responds by clarifying that there are two types of imagination: one that is sound and nearly sensory when dealing with geometric assumptions, and another that is false when it applies sensory-specific properties to abstract intelligibles. This distinction is crucial for understanding the validity of arguments in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the objector presents his case and how the author begins his response.
The objection claims we know by necessity the possibility of the assumed scenario.
2
Identify the two types of imagination
Look for the distinction the author makes: one type is sound for geometric matters, the other is false when applied to intelligibles. Mark the key terms.
The author uses 'الهندسيات' for geometry and 'الوهميات الصرفة' for purely imaginary things.
3
Understand why geometric imagination is sound
The author says geometric judgments are 'under the obedience of reason' and almost like sensory perceptions. Reflect on why this makes them reliable.
This is why only obstinacy would deny them.
4
Recognize the false type with the example
The author gives an example: judging that every existent has a position. This is false because it applies a sensory property to abstract entities like Allah or the soul.
This clarifies that not all imagination is unreliable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوابه أنا نعلم بالضرورة إمكان ما فرض، وإمكان اجتماعه مع البعد الغير المتناهي، فتعين كونه المنشأ للزوم المحال ومنها أنا لا نسلم استحالة أول نقطة في الخط الغير المتناهي، وما ذكر في بيانه باطل لأن انقسام الحركة والزاوية لا إلى نهاية حكم الوهم وهو كاذب . وجوابه : أن أحكام الوهم فيما يفرض من الهندسيات صحيحة تكاد تجري مجرى الحسيات لكونها على طاعة من العقل بحيث لا يمنع إلا مكابرة. ولهذا لا يقع فيها اختلاف آراء وإنما الكاذب هي الوهميات الصرفة مثل الحكم في المعقولات بما يخص المحسوسات كالحكم بأن كل موجود ذو وضع .
And its answer: We know by necessity the possibility of what was assumed, and the possibility of its coexistence with infinite distance, so it is determined that it is the source of the necessity of the impossible. And among them: We do not concede the impossibility of a first point in an infinite line, and what was mentioned in its proof is false because the division of motion and angle ad infinitum is a judgment of imagination (wahm), and it is false. And its answer: The judgments of imagination regarding what is assumed from geometrical matters are sound, almost running in the course of sensory perceptions because they are under the obedience of reason such that nothing prevents them except obstinacy. Therefore, no difference of opinions occurs in them. Rather, false are the purely imaginary things, like judging in intelligibles by what is specific to sensibles, such as judging that every existent has a position.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection and its answer. The objector claims that we know by necessity the possibility of the assumed scenario (the beginning of coincidence) and its compatibility with infinite distance, so the impossibility must arise from another source. Moreover, the objector refuses to admit the impossibility of a first point in an infinite line, asserting that the division of motion and angle ad infinitum is a judgment of the imagination (الوهم), which is unreliable. The author's answer distinguishes two types of imagination: sound geometric imaginations that are correct and nearly sensory because they follow reason—these are universally agreed upon and only denied by obstinacy. False imaginations (الوهميات الصرفة) are those that apply sensory-specific properties to intelligible objects, such as claiming that every existent has a position (i.e., spatial location), which is not true for abstract entities.

Key Terms
الوهم
الهندسيات
الحسيات
المعقولات
المحسوسات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All judgments of imagination (wahm) are false and unreliable.
Correction: The author clarifies that geometric imagination is sound and nearly sensory; only purely imaginary judgments (al-wahmiyyat al-sirfa) that apply sensory properties to intelligibles are false.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why are judgments of imagination in geometry considered sound?
A) Because they are purely imaginary.
B) Because they are under the obedience of reason and almost like sensory perceptions.
C) Because they are never disputed.
D) Because they apply sensory properties to intelligibles.

Key Takeaway
The author distinguishes sound geometric imagination from false purely imaginary judgments, preserving the validity of geometric reasoning in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever trusted your imagination in matters of faith, only to later realize you were applying physical assumptions to spiritual realities? How does this lesson's distinction between sound geometric imagination and false imaginary judgments challenge you to examine the source of your religious convictions?

This lesson shows that some imaginings are sound because they obey reason. ?

Imam's Objection and the Author's Answer on the Inversion of the Proof
Al-Razi objects that the proof is inverted; the author dismisses it as pure imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection that the proof of finitude is logically inverted and leads to infinite dimensions, and the author's response that this objection is based on an imaginary external space.

Core Idea
The universe has no external reference frame; dimensions end at its boundary.

In this passage, Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani records a sharp objection from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi against the proof for the finitude of dimensions. Al-Razi argues that the proof is logically inverted, because following it step by step would actually imply infinite dimensions. The author defends the original proof by exposing a hidden assumption in al-Razi's scenario—the assumption of an empty space beyond the world. This exchange illustrates how kalam theologians carefully distinguish between rational necessity and imaginative constructs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full literal translation. Let the objection and response unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two parts
Divide the passage into al-Razi's objection (up to 'لا إلى نهاية') and the author's answer (from 'فجوابه'). Note the structure.
3
Understand the geometric illustration
Al-Razi uses the world's axis and a sphere with an inclined diameter to argue that a line would extend infinitely. Visualize the scenario: the axis, the sphere, the angle, and the line bisecting it.
This is a key part of the objection.
4
Grasp the author's key point
The author says the scenario is purely imaginary because beyond the world there is no void or plenum for the line to extend into. This is the core of the answer.
The author denies the external reference frame.
5
Reflect on the mutual accusation
Both sides accuse each other of obstinacy (mukābara). Consider why the author turns the charge back.
This highlights the disagreement about what is rationally necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض الإمام بأن هذا الدليل مقلوب، لأنه لما كانت المسامتة لكل نقطة بعد المسامتة لما فوقها لزم عدم تناهي الأبعاد وبيانه على ما في المطالب العالية أعظم ما يفرض من الخطوط المستقيمة هو محور للعالم. أعني الخط المار بمركزه الواصل بين قطبيه . فإذا فرضنا كرة يميل قطرها الموازي للمحور إلى مسامته حدثت زاوية قابلة للقسمة، ولا محالة يكون الخط الخارج على نصفها مسامتاً لنقطة فوق طرف المحور ، ويكون هناك أبعاد يفرض نقط لا إلى نهاية . فجوابه أن هذا من الوهميات الصرفة التي لا يصدقها العقل، إذ ليس وراء العالم خلاء أو ملاء يمتد فيه الخط أو ينتهي إليه طرفه ، وما ذكر الإمام من أن صريح العقل شاهد لمسامتة طرف هذا الخط لشيء ووقوعه خارج العالم وإن إنكاره مكابرة في الضروريات مكابرة.
And the Imam (al-Razi) objected that this proof is inverted, because since coincidence for every point after coincidence for what is above it necessitates the infinity of dimensions. Its explanation as in al-Matalib al-Aliya: The greatest of what is assumed from straight lines is the axis of the world—I mean the line passing through its center connecting its two poles. If we assume a sphere whose diameter parallel to the axis inclines to coincide with it, an angle divisible occurs, and inevitably the line emerging on its half coincides with a point above the tip of the axis, and there are dimensions whose points are assumed ad infinitum. The answer: This is from the purely imaginary things that reason does not affirm, for beyond the world there is no void or plenum in which the line extends or its tip ends. What the Imam mentioned that clear reason witnesses the coincidence of the tip of this line with something and its occurrence outside the world, and that denying it is obstinacy regarding necessities, is itself obstinacy.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage records the objection of Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and the author's response. The Imam argues that the original proof is inverted (مقلوب): if coincidence for every point necessarily implies a higher point, then infinite dimensions follow. To illustrate, he describes the world's axis and a sphere with a diameter parallel to it; when the diameter inclines to coincide with the axis, a divisible angle is formed, and the line bisecting that angle will coincide with a point beyond the axis's tip, leading to an infinite regress of dimensions. The author's answer is that this reasoning is an instance of pure imagination (الوهميات الصرفة) that reason does not accept, because beyond the world there is no extension—neither void nor plenum—for the line to extend into. The author further accuses the Imam of obstinacy in claiming that clear reason supports the coincidence and that denial is obstinacy; the author turns the charge back, calling it obstinacy to insist on such a scenario.

Misconception: One might think al-Razi's geometric scenario is a valid refutation because it shows a logical inconsistency in the original proof.
Correction: The author argues that the scenario is only valid if there is an external space (void or plenum) beyond the world, which does not exist. Thus the objection is based on an imaginary assumption.

The author rejects al-Razi's objection because it presupposes an external space beyond the universe that does not exist, making the geometric scenario purely imaginary.

The Ladder Proof: Geometry of Finite Dimensions
The author presents a second geometric proof using an angle and triangle to show that infinite dimensions lead to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second geometric proof (the 'ladder proof') for the finitude of dimensions, including its geometric setup, the objection of circularity, and the author's rebuttal.

Core Idea
Infinite dimensions lead to contradiction via a simple angle and triangle geometry.

In this passage, the author presents a second geometric demonstration (known as al-burhan al-sullami) for the finitude of dimensions. He constructs a model using two lines forming a fixed angle, showing that if the lines extend infinitely, the distance between them must also be infinite yet confined — a contradiction. After presenting the proof, he addresses an objection that it assumes what it tries to prove, and clarifies that it is actually a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the geometric construction and the logical flow: setup, clearer formulation, objection, response, and naming of the proof.
2
Visualize the geometry
Imagine two lines from a point with a fixed angle. If the angle is 60°, the triangle formed is equilateral. The distance between the lines (the base) is equal to each side. If the sides extend infinitely, the base must also be infinite — but it is a finite segment between the endpoints.
Drawing on paper may help.
3
Identify the objection and rebuttal
The objector says the proof assumes there is an endpoint (the last distance), which is the very thing being proved — a circular argument. The author responds that the proof does not assume an endpoint; rather, it logically entails one, and denying that leads to a contradiction.
This is a common philosophical debate about circularity.
4
Reflect on the significance
This 'ladder proof' is a classic argument in Islamic kalam for the finitude of the physical world. Consider how it differs from the first proof (if you recall) and why geometric reasoning was valued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثاني) إنا نفرض من نقطة خطين يزداد البعد بينهما على نسبة زيادة امتدادهما بحيث توجد كل زيادة مع المزيد عليه في بعد، فلو امتدا إلى غير النهاية يلزم لضرورة المحافظة على النسبة لوجود بعد مشتمل على الزيادات الغير المتناهية زائد على البعد الأول بقدرها مع انحصاره بين الحاجزين والأوضح أن يفرض كون البعد دائماً بقدر الخطين بأن تجعل الزاوية ثلثي قائمة والمثلث متساوي الأضلاع فيلزم بالضرورة من عدم تناهيهما وجود بعد بينهما غير متناه . فإن قيل : هذا يتوقف على أن يكون للامتداد طرف فيتحقق بعد هو آخر الأبعاد وهو مصادرة . قلنا : لا بل يستلزمه وهو خلف . هذا هو البرهان السلمي وحاصله أنه لو كانت الأبعاد غير متناهية لزم إمكان عدم
He said (the second): We assume from a point two lines whose distance between them increases in proportion to the increase of their extension such that every increase occurs with the increased upon it in distance. So if they extend to infinity, then due to the necessity of maintaining the proportion, there necessarily exists a distance comprising infinite increases, additional to the first distance by an amount equal to them, while being confined between two barriers. And it is clearer to assume that the distance is always equal to the two lines by making the angle two-thirds of a right angle and the triangle equilateral; then from their non-finiteness there necessarily follows the existence between them of an infinite distance. Then if it is said: This depends on the extension having an end so that a distance that is the last of the distances is actualized, which is a begging of the question. We say: No, rather it entails it, and it is a contradiction. This is the 'ladder proof' (al-burhan al-sullami), and its summary is that if dimensions were infinite, then the possibility of non-existence would necessarily follow...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents a second geometric proof (called البرهان السلمي, the 'ladder proof') for the finitude of dimensions. The author proposes two lines emanating from a point such that the distance between them increases proportionally to their length. If the lines extend infinitely, the distance between them would accumulate infinitely many increments, resulting in an infinite distance that is nevertheless confined between two boundaries (the lines themselves)—an impossibility. A clearer formulation: assume the angle is two-thirds of a right angle (60 degrees) and the triangle is equilateral; then the two sides and the base are equal. If the sides extend infinitely, the base must also be infinite, but it is a finite line segment—contradiction. The objector counters that this argument presupposes that the extension has an endpoint (the final distance), which is exactly what is being proven (circular). The author responds that the argument does not beg the question; rather, it entails the conclusion, and the denial leads to a contradiction (خلف). The passage concludes by naming this the 'ladder proof' and stating its summary: if dimensions were infinite, then the possibility of non-existence would follow (text truncated).

Key Terms
البرهان السلمي
نسبة
مثلث متساوي الأضلاع
زاوية ثلثي قائمة
مصادرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes an endpoint.
Correction: The author clarifies that the proof does not assume an endpoint; rather, the impossibility of infinite dimensions logically entails that there must be an endpoint, and denying this leads to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's objection to the second proof?
A) The angle is not constant.
B) The proof assumes what it wants to prove, i.e., that there is an endpoint.
C) The lines cannot extend infinitely.
D) The triangle is not equilateral.

Key Takeaway
The ladder proof uses simple geometry to show that infinite dimensions lead to logical contradiction, establishing the finitude of the physical world.

The ladder proof shows that an infinite distance cannot be confined between two lines. How does the logical necessity of finitude challenge you to confront areas where you are avoiding an end?
?
The author refutes the objection of circularity by showing that the contradiction emerges from the premises, not from assuming the conclusion. The Triangle Proof and the Circular Proof for Finitude
The author presents two geometric proofs for the finitude of lines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two geometric proofs (the triangle proof and the circular proof) that establish the finitude of lines, and how they rely on Euclidean geometry.

Core Idea
Geometry shows that infinite lines lead to impossible infinite spaces.

This lesson presents two classical geometric arguments for the finitude of extended lines. The first, the triangle proof, uses a 60° angle and Euclidean triangle properties. The second, the circular proof invented by Suhrawardi, uses a sixfold division of a circle. Both demonstrate that assuming infinite lines forces an infinite base, which is impossible. We'll work through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage before we analyze.
Pay attention to the structure: first the triangle proof, then the circular proof, then a rebuke of doubters.
2
Understand the triangle proof
Follow the logic: assume a 60° angle at the vertex; using Euclidean theorems, the triangle becomes equilateral. If the two sides are infinite, the base must also be infinite, which is impossible.
The key lemma is that equal angles imply equal sides.
3
Understand the circular proof
Suhrawardi's method: divide a circle into six equal parts by radii. Each central angle is 60°, and the triangles formed are equilateral. If the radii (sides) are infinite, the chords (bases) are infinite, leading to the same contradiction.
This proof avoids the initial assumption of a specific angle by construction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion and the response
Note how the passage ends by dismissing doubters who lack geometric understanding. The proofs are considered demonstrative for those who know geometry.
This highlights the role of proper knowledge in accepting rational arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمكن منعه إلا مكابرة، لكن لما كان في إمكان المفروض نوع خفاء قرره بعضهم بأنا نفرض زاوية مبدأ الخطين ثلثي قائمة وللزوم تساوي الزاويتين الحادثتين من الخط الواصل بين كل نقطتين متقابلتين من ساقي المثلث، ولزوم كون زواياه مساوية لقائمتين، لزم أن يكون كل من الزاويتين ثلثي قائمة، ولزم من تساوي زوايا المثلث تساوي أضلاعه كل ذلك لما بينه إقليدس فيلزم من عدم تناهي الخطين عدم تناهي ما بينهما، وحاول صاحب الإشراق سلوك طريق يوجب كون زاوية مبدأ الخطين ثلثي قائمة، فاخترع البرهان الترسي، وتقريره : أنا نخرج من مركز جسم مستدير كالترس مثلاً ستة خطوط قاسمـة له إلى ستة أقسام متساوية، فيكون كل من الزوايا الست ثلثي قائمة وكذا كل من الزاويتين الحادثتين من الخط الواصل بين كل نقطتين متقابلتين من كل ضلعين، فيصير كل مثلثاً متساوي الزوايا والأضلاع، ويلزم من امتداد الخطين إلى غير النهاية، امتداد بعدما بينهما إلى غير النهاية . ومن تردد في لزوم تساوي الزوايا والأضلاع ، وجوز كون وتر زاوية مبدأ الخطوط الستة أقل من الضلعين أو أكثر فلعدم شعوره بالهندسة.
It can be denied only through obstinacy, but since there is a kind of obscurity in the possibility of the supposition, some scholars explained it by saying: We assume the angle at the origin of the two lines to be two-thirds of a right angle, and due to the necessity of equality of the two acute angles formed by the line connecting each pair of opposite points on the sides of the triangle, and the necessity that its angles equal two right angles, it follows that each of the two angles is two-thirds of a right angle, and from the equality of the angles of the triangle follows the equality of its sides; all that as demonstrated by Euclid. Hence from the non-finiteness of the two lines follows the non-finiteness of the space between them. The author of al-Ishraq (Suhrawardi) attempted to follow a method requiring the angle at the origin of the two lines to be two-thirds of a right angle, so he invented the circular proof. Its formulation: we draw from the center of a round body, e.g., a shield, six lines dividing it into six equal parts; then each of the six angles is two-thirds of a right angle, and likewise each of the two angles formed by the line connecting each pair of opposite points of any two sides; thus each becomes a triangle with equal angles and sides. From the extension of the two lines indefinitely follows the extension of the distance between them indefinitely. Whoever hesitates about the necessity of the equality of angles and sides, and allows the chord of the angle at the origin of the six lines to be less or more than the two sides, does so due to his lack of understanding of geometry.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents two geometric proofs for the finitude of lines (or extension). It begins by stating that denying finitude is only possible through obstinacy, but because the argument is somewhat subtle, scholars have articulated it clearly. The first proof (the triangle proof) assumes an angle of 60° at the vertex of a triangle. Using Euclidean geometry, it shows that if the two sides (legs) are infinite, the base must also be infinite, which is impossible because the base is bounded by the legs. The second proof, invented by Suhrawardi (the author of al-Ishraq), is the circular or shield proof. It constructs six equilateral triangles from a circle divided into six equal arcs; each triangle has equal angles (60°) and equal sides. If the radii (legs) are infinite, the chords (bases) must also be infinite, leading to the same contradiction. The passage ends by rebuking those who doubt the geometric necessity of these equalities, attributing such doubt to ignorance of geometry.

Key Terms
برهان المثلث
برهان الترسي
زاوية ثلثي قائمة
صاحب الإشراق
مثلث متساوي الزوايا والأضلاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can doubt geometric proofs for finitude of lines without understanding geometry.
Correction: Geometric necessity, as demonstrated by Euclid, is objective; doubt due to ignorance is not valid.

Checkpoint
According to the triangle proof, if the two sides of the triangle are infinite, what must be true of the base?
A) It must be finite.
B) It must also be infinite.
C) It must be half the length of the sides.
D) It must be equal to the sum of the sides.

Key Takeaway
Both geometric proofs demonstrate that the assumption of infinite lines leads to a logical contradiction, establishing the finitude of extension.

Is there a truth in your faith that you have resisted because it required intellectual effort? What will you do to change that?

The passage declares that hesitation about geometric necessity stems from 'lack of understanding of geometry.' Has your lack of deep knowledge ever led you to question a clear teaching of Islam? What step can you take today to strengthen your understanding of a foundational belief through study?

Objection and Answer to the Geometric Proofs
Objection to geometric proofs and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a key objection to the geometric proofs for finitude is answered through a reductio ad absurdum that avoids circularity.

Core Idea
The objection claims circularity, but the answer turns it into a decisive proof.

The geometric proofs for the finitude of bodies rely on the equality of sides in an equilateral triangle. Critics argue that these proofs presuppose finitude, making them circular. In this passage, Sanusi presents the objection and then offers a crisp refutation that turns the tables, showing that the objection actually strengthens the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's claim: the geometric proofs only show indefinite expansion, not an infinite whole, and that proving finitude by requiring a last distance is a petition of the principle.
This objection is a common fallacy accusation.
2
Read the objection in Arabic
Read the first part of the Arabic passage (up to 'وإذا سلّم') and compare with the translation. Notice the terms for 'begging the question' (مصادرة على المطلوب).
3
Examine the answer's structure
The answer first asserts the necessary equality of the base to the legs. Then it shows that if the legs are infinite, the base must also be infinite, but the base is finite because it is bounded (between two boundaries). Thus a contradiction arises.
This is a classic reductio argument.
4
See why it is not circular
The answer does not assume the finitude of the legs; it deduces it from the finitude of the base (which is independently known). The objection's claim of circularity is refuted because the impossibility comes from the assumption alone.
5
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice the phrase 'حاصله أن لا تناهي القاعدة ليس موقوفاً على تناهي الساقين حتى تلزم المصادرة، بل مستلزماً له فيلزم الخلف'. This clarifies that the non-finiteness of the base is not dependent on the finitude of the legs; rather, it entails it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على هذه البيانات بأنها إنما تفيد زيادة الأبعاد والاتساعات فيما بين الخطين إلى غير النهاية لا وجود سعة وبعد ممتد إلى غير النهاية، وإنما يلزم ذلك لو كان هناك بعد هو آخر الأبعاد يساوي الخطين اللذين هما ساقا المثلث فلا يتصور ذلك إلا بانقطاعهما وتناهيهما، فيكون إثبات التناهي بذلك مصادرة على المطلوب. ولو سلّم إنما لزم من المجموع المفروض ، وهو لا يستلزم استحالة لا تناهي الخطين . والجواب : أنه لما لزم تساوي أضلاع مثل هذا المثلث كان لزوم عدم تناهي قاعدته على تقدير لا تناهي ساقيه ظاهراً لا يمكن منعه، وأما السند فلنا لا علينا، لأنه لما لزم مساواة القاعدة للساقين، وكانت متناهية لانحصارها بين حاصرين لزم تناهي الساقين على تقدير لاتناهيهما، فيكون اللاتناهي محالاً، وحاصله أن لا تناهي القاعدة ليس موقوفاً على تناهي الساقين حتى تلزم المصادرة، بل مستلزماً له فيلزم الخلف . وتقريره أنه لو كان الساقان غير متناهيين لزم ثبوت قاعدة مساوية لهما، لما ذكر من الدليل لكن القاعدة لا تكون إلا متناهية ضرورة انحصارها بين حاصرين فيلزم تناهي الساقين، لأن المتناهي لا يكون مساوياً لغير المتناهي، وقد فرضناهما غير متناهيين هذا خلف وأما كون المحال ناشئاً من لاتناهي الخطين، فللعلم الضروري بإمكان ما عداه من الأمور المذكورة.
An objection was raised against these proofs: they only indicate an increase of dimensions and expansions between the two lines indefinitely, not the existence of an infinite extended distance. That would only follow if there were a distance that is the last of distances equal to the two lines that are the legs of the triangle, which is conceivable only if they are terminated and finite; thus proving finitude by this is a petition of the principle. Even if conceded, it follows from the assumed totality, which does not entail the impossibility of the non-finiteness of the lines. The answer: Since the equality of the sides of such a triangle is necessary, the necessity of the non-finiteness of its base on the assumption of the non-finiteness of its legs is apparent and cannot be denied. As for the objection, it is in our favor, not against us, because when the equality of the base to the legs is necessary, and the base is finite due to being confined between two boundaries, the finitude of the legs follows on the assumption of their non-finiteness; thus non-finiteness is impossible. The summary: the non-finiteness of the base is not dependent on the finitude of the legs such that the petition would follow; rather, it entails it, so a contradiction arises. Its formulation: if the legs were non-finite, the existence of a base equal to them would follow, by the proof mentioned; but the base can only be finite, necessarily due to being confined between two boundaries; so the finitude of the legs follows, because a finite cannot be equal to a non-finite, and we have supposed them non-finite—this is a contradiction. As for the impossibility arising from the non-finiteness of the lines, it is due to necessary knowledge of the possibility of the other matters mentioned.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the geometric proofs for finitude. The objector argues that the proofs only show an indefinite increase in distance between the lines, not the existence of an actually infinite extended distance. To conclude that an infinite distance exists would require a 'last distance' equal to the legs, which is only possible if the legs are finite—thus the argument begs the question. The answer refutes this by pointing out that the base of the equilateral triangle is necessarily equal to the legs. If the legs are assumed infinite, the base must also be infinite. But the base is necessarily finite because it is bounded by the two legs. This creates a contradiction: the base is both infinite (by equality) and finite (by being bounded). Therefore, the assumption that the legs are infinite is impossible. The argument does not beg the question; it is a reductio ad absurdum. The impossibility stems directly from the assumption of infinite legs, not from any hidden premise.

Key Terms
مصادرة على المطلوب
خلف
حاصرين
برهان التطبيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The geometric proof for finitude is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it is a reductio ad absurdum. Assuming infinite legs leads to a contradiction (base both infinite and finite), so the assumption is false. The finitude of the base is independently known (it is bounded), so no circularity occurs.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection raised against the geometric proofs?
A) They rely on Euclidean geometry which is not universally accepted.
B) They only demonstrate an infinite increase in distance, not an actually infinite whole.
C) They assume the lines are finite from the start.
D) They require the existence of a last distance which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the geometric proofs are circular is refuted by showing that assuming infinite legs leads to a contradiction, thus proving finitude without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that assuming infinite legs leads to a contradiction with the finitude of the base. In your life, are you building on assumptions that will ultimately collapse into contradiction on the Day of Judgment? What 'infinite legs'—like unlimited time or unchecked desires—are you assuming, and how can you replace them with the firm foundation of finitude and accountability?
?
Just as the base is necessarily finite because it is bounded, your life is bounded by birth and death. Do you truly live as if these boundaries are real, or do you deceive yourself into acting as if you have infinite days? What can you do today to align your actions with the certainty of your finitude?
?
The objection seemed plausible until the answer exposed its flaw. What seemingly plausible objections do you make to avoid submitting to the truth of Allah's signs? Are you willing to let your assumptions be dismantled by clear proof, even when it demands a change in your life?

The Proof of Application for Finitude
Sanusi presents the proof of application for finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof of application (برهان التطبيق) as the third rational proof for the finitude of distances and why the assumption of an infinite distance leads to contradiction.

Core Idea
Subtract a cubit from infinity and compare: equality or cut-off, both impossible.

Sanusi now presents the third proof for finitude, known as the proof of application. This argument cleverly uses the operation of subtracting a unit from an assumed infinite distance and then comparing the original and the diminished distances. The resulting contradiction shows that the infinite cannot exist. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Notice the key terms: ذراع (cubit), تطبيق (application). The argument is condensed.
The text is cut off at the end, but the conclusion is clear from context.
2
Understand the setup of the proof
The proof assumes an infinite distance. Then it subtracts one cubit from it, creating a deficient distance. The complete infinite distance is then applied (compared) to the deficient one.
This is the 'application' — a term in kalam logic.
3
Examine the two possibilities
Two possibilities arise: (1) every cubit of the complete corresponds to a cubit of the deficient, implying equality of whole and part; (2) they do not correspond, implying the distances are cut off (i.e., not properly aligned). Both are impossible.
4
Recognize the contradiction
Since both outcomes are absurd, the original assumption of an infinite distance must be false. Thus, distance is finite.
This is a classic kalam proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثالث) إنا أن ننقص من البعد الغير المتناهي ذراعاً نطبق، فإما أن يقع بإزاء كل ذراع ذراع فيتساوان، أو لا فينقطعان، وقد مر مثله، وهذه الثلاثة هي الأصول في براهين التناهي. هذا برهان التطبيق، وتقريره أنه لو وجد بعد غير متناه، نفرض نقصان ذراع منه، ثم نطبق بين البعد التام والناقص، فإما أن يقع بإزاء كل ذراع من التام ذراع من الناقص، وهو محال لامتناع تساوي الزائد والناقص، بل الكل والجزء، أو لا يقع
He said, "Third: We take a cubit from the infinite distance and apply [it]. Either every cubit falls opposite a cubit, so they are equal, or they do not, so they are cut off. A similar example has passed. These three are the principles among the proofs of finitude. This is the proof of application. Its formulation: If there existed an infinite distance, we assume subtracting a cubit from it, then we apply the complete distance to the deficient [distance]. Then either every cubit of the complete falls opposite a cubit of the deficient—which is impossible because the equal does not exceed the [or: because of the impossibility of equality between the greater and the lesser], rather the whole and the part; or they do not fall opposite."
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for finitude, known as the proof of application (برهان التطبيق). The argument assumes an infinite distance and then subtracts a cubit from it, creating a 'deficient' distance. The 'complete' infinite distance is then applied (compared) to the deficient one. Two possibilities arise: either every cubit of the complete corresponds to a cubit of the deficient—which would imply equality between the whole and the part, an impossibility—or the correspondence fails, meaning the infinite distance is not uniformly divisible, which also leads to contradiction. Hence the assumption of an infinite distance is impossible. The proof relies on the principle that the whole cannot equal its part. The passage notes that this is one of the three fundamental proofs for finitude (the other two being the triangle and circular proofs). The text is cut off, but the intended conclusion is that an infinite distance cannot exist.

Key Terms
برهان التطبيق
ذراع
تطبيق
الكل والجزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that subtracting a unit from an infinite distance still leaves an infinite distance, so comparison is futile.
Correction: The proof shows that even the act of subtraction and comparison forces a contradiction: either equality of whole and part (absurd) or mismatch (absurd). The infinite cannot be consistently compared in this way.

Checkpoint
In the proof of application, what is the problem if every cubit of the complete distance falls opposite a cubit of the deficient distance?
A) It would prove that the distances are equal, which is impossible because the complete is larger.
B) It would prove that the deficient distance is actually finite.
C) It would show that the infinite distance is not truly infinite.
D) It would imply that the two distances are incommensurable.

Key Takeaway
The proof of application demonstrates that assuming an infinite distance leads to contradiction, so all distances must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been handed a finite existence. The proof of application shows that even distance cannot be infinite—so your life has a definite limit. Are you living as though that limit is fixed tomorrow or distant? What would change if you truly felt that limit today?
?
The proof demolishes the notion of an infinite physical distance. But you might still live as if your time is infinite—postponing repentance, good deeds, or reconciliation. Why do you assume infinite tomorrows when your Creator has shown that everything finite is the only possibility?
?
If you believe that God alone is infinite, then why do you cling to finite things as if they will satisfy you? How would your desires shift if you internalized the finitude of all creation?

The First Proof: Negating the Indivisible Atom
Al-Sanudi explains the basis of the first proof: denial of the indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundation of the parallelism proof (برهان المسامتة) and why it depends on denying the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد).

Core Idea
The first proof requires rejecting the indivisible atom to allow infinite division.

Al-Sanudi begins by outlining the three proofs for the finitude of dimensions. The first proof, known as the proof of parallelism, rests on a crucial premise: the negation of the indivisible atom. Without this, the infinite division of motion and angle would be impossible. This lesson explores that foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author first summarizes all three proofs and then elaborates on the first one.
The general summary helps contextualize the first proof.
2
Focus on the basis of the first proof
Identify the phrase 'برهان المسامتة على نفي الجوهر الفرد'. This is the key premise: the proof is built on denying the indivisible atom.
3
Understand why the indivisible atom must be negated
The author says this is done 'ليصح انقسام الحركة والزاوية إلى غير النهاية'. The infinite division of motion and angle is necessary for the proof to work.
Without this denial, the proof cannot proceed.
4
Connect to the overall purpose
Reflect on how this premise fits into the broader argument for the finitude of dimensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومبنى الأول) على نفي الجزء، والثاني على أن يكون اللاتناهي من جهات، والثالث على مقدمات واهية. أي برهان المسامتة على نفي الجوهر الفرد ليصح انقسام الحركة والزاوية إلى غير النهاية، ومبنى البرهان السلمي على أن يكون لا تناهي البعد من جهات حتى نفرض انفراج ساقي المثلث لا إلى نهاية بل في الترسى [؟] لا بد من فرض اللا تناهي في جميع الجهات وكان طرق السلمى مبنية على طريق إلزام القائلين بلا تناهي الأبعاد في جميع الجهات. ومبنى برهان التطبيق على مقدمات ضعيفة سبقت الإشارة إليها في إبطال التسلسل مثل اقتدار الوهم على التطبيق، ومثل استلزام وقوع ذراع بإزاء ذراع للتساوي، ومثل اختصاص ذلك بما له وضع وترتيب ليحصل التقصي عن النقض بمراتب الأعداد، وحركات الأفلاك.
He said: The basis of the first [proof] is on the negation of the indivisible part, the second on that infinity is from [multiple] directions, and the third on weak premises. That is, the proof of parallelism (برهان المسامتة) is based on the negation of the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) so that the division of motion and angle into infinity is valid. The basis of the ladder proof (البرهان السلمي) is that the infinity of distance is from [multiple] directions so that we suppose the opening of the legs of the triangle to infinity, but in the [??] (الترسى) it is necessary to assume infinity in all directions, and the path of the ladder proof was based on the method of obligating those who affirm infinity of dimensions in all directions. The basis of the application proof (برهان التطبيق) is on weak premises previously indicated in the refutation of infinite regress, such as the power of estimation (الوهم) to apply, and such as the entailment of a cubit opposite a cubit for equality, and such as the specificity of that to what has position and order to obtain refutation of the objection by the ranks of numbers and the motions of the spheres.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage explains the foundational premises of the three famous proofs for the finitude of dimensions. The author first cites the claim that each proof rests on a distinct basis: the first on denying the indivisible atom, the second on assuming infinity from multiple directions, the third on weak premises. Then he elaborates: The proof of parallelism (برهان المسامتة) depends on rejecting the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) to allow infinite division of motion and angle. The ladder proof (البرهان السلمي) requires that distance be infinite from multiple directions, meaning the triangle's legs diverge infinitely. The application proof (برهان التطبيق) relies on weak premises like the estimative faculty's ability to apply, the need for a cubit to match a cubit for equality, and the restriction to things with position and order to avoid counterexamples from numbers and celestial motions. Each premise is explained briefly.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
برهان المسامتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first proof (برهان المسامتة) according to the author?
A) Denial of the indivisible atom
B) Assumption of infinite directions
C) Weak premises of estimation
D) The ladder of the triangle

Key Takeaway
The first proof for finitude of dimensions depends on denying the indivisible atom to permit infinite division of motion and angle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The first proof is dependent on negation. Sometimes in our spiritual journey, we must first negate falsehood before embracing truth. What falsehood have you been tolerating that you now feel compelled to reject? Write it down and take one step to remove it today.

The Second Proof: Infinity from Multiple Directions
Al-Sanudi explains the basis of the second proof: assuming infinity from all directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundation of the ladder proof (البرهان السلمي) and its requirement that infinity be assumed from multiple directions.

Core Idea
The ladder proof assumes infinity in all directions to force a contradiction.

The second proof for the finitude of dimensions, known as the ladder proof, relies on a specific premise: that the distance is infinite from multiple directions, not just one. This lesson unpacks that premise and its role in the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that it focuses on the premise of infinity from multiple directions.
The passage follows the explanation of the first proof.
2
Identify the premise of infinity from multiple directions
The key phrase is 'على أن يكون لا تناهي البعد من جهات'. The proof requires that distance be infinite from multiple directions.
3
Understand the triangle analogy
The author mentions the opening of the legs of a triangle to infinity. This illustrates how the proof works geometrically.
The unclear word الترسى may be a corruption, but the general idea is clear.
4
Note the method of obligation
The proof proceeds by 'إلزام القائلين بلا تناهي الأبعاد في جميع الجهات'. It forces those who claim infinite dimensions to accept a contradiction.

Source Text

The Third Proof: Weak Premises of Application
Al-Sanudi explains the weak premises of the application proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundation of the application proof (برهان التطبيق) and the three weak premises it relies on.

Core Idea
The third proof rests on weak premises about estimation, equality, and order.

The third proof for the finitude of dimensions, the application proof, is considered less rigorous. Its premises—including the power of estimation, the requirement of equality when applying one object to another, and the restriction to ordered things—are all weak. This lesson examines these premises and their role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note that it describes weak premises previously mentioned in the refutation of infinite regress.
2
Identify the three weak premises
Look for 'مثل اقتدار الوهم على التطبيق، ومثل استلزام وقوع ذراع بإزاء ذراع للتساوي، ومثل اختصاص ذلك بما له وضع وترتيب'. These are the three premises.
3
Understand each premise
The first is the power of estimation to apply; the second is that placing a cubit opposite a cubit entails equality; the third restricts to things with position and order to avoid counterexamples from numbers and celestial motions.
4
Note the context
These premises were previously indicated in the refutation of infinite regress, as mentioned in the passage.

The Proof of Extrication (برهان التخلص) and its Objection
Modification of the parallelism proof and response to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of extrication modifies the parallelism argument to avoid earlier objections, and why the author dismisses the objection by Abu al-Barakat.

Core Idea
The proof of extrication uses a finite moving line to derive a contradiction from infinite dimensions.

The author has already presented three proofs for the finitude of dimensions. Now he introduces modifications to the parallelism proof. The first modification, called the proof of extrication, imagines a rotating sphere with a finite diameter to avoid the problem of moving an infinite line. However, some philosophers objected, and the author responds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the proof begins with a conditional statement and constructs a scenario.
The proof is presented in a logical, step-by-step manner.
2
Identify the logical steps
Trace the argument: if infinite dimensions possible → suppose sphere with infinite line → rotation → contradiction.
Look for the key words: 'لو أمكن', 'فبالضرورة', 'لكن ذلك محال'.
3
Understand the objection
What is the objection raised by Abu al-Barakat? It denies the possibility of moving an infinite line.
The objection is mentioned after 'ویرد عليه'.
4
Analyze the author's rebuttal
The author says the objection has no basis because the moving line is the sphere's diameter, which is finite.
The phrase 'لأن المفروض هناك حركة قطر الكرة وهو متناه' is key.
5
Reflect on the significance
This modification shows how later scholars refined the proofs to counter criticisms.
