⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ( وقد كثرت الوجوه) بتصرف في الثلاثة. أي وجوه الاستدلال على تناهي الأبعاد بتصرف في البراهين الثلاثة، واستعانة ببراهين إبطال التسلسل، أما وجوه التصرف في السلمى، فقد سبقت وأما في المسامتة فوجهان: أحدهما: برهان التخلص وتقريره أنه لو أمكن لا تناهي الأبعاد لأمكن أن نفرض كرة يخرج من مركزها خط غير متناه ملازم له مقاطع لخط آخر غير متناه، وأن تتحرك تلك الكرة على نفسها، فبالضرورة يصير الخط الخارج من مركزها بعد المقاطعة مسامتاً ثم موازياً، لكن ذلك محال لتوقفه على تخلص أحد الخطين عن الآخر وهو يتصور إلا بنقطة هي من أحد الخطين، وقد فرضناهما غير متناهيين هذا خلف. ویرد عليه منع إمكان حركة الخط الغير المتناهي سيما بحيث ينتهي من المقاطعة إلى المسامتة إلى الموازاة. وأورد أبو البركات هذا المنع على برهان المسامتة وتبعه صاحب الإشراق في المطارحات، ولا يظهر له وجه، لأن المفروض هناك حركة قطر الكرة وهو متناه. وثانيهما: برهان الموازاة وهو أن نفرض قطر الكرة مسامتاً للخط الغير المتناهي، ثم موازياً له بحركة الكرة فلانتهاء المسامتة يلزم في الخط الغير المتناهي نقطة آخر نقطة المسامتة وهو محال، لأن كل نقطة تفرض كذلك. فالمسامتة بما فوقها بعد المسامتة بها، وأما على برهان التطبيق فمثل أن نفرض البعد الغير المتناهي أذرعاً، ثم نعتبر التطبيق بين عدة الألوف منها. وعدة الآحاد على ما مر في التسلسل أو يقال ما بين المبدأ المحقق أو المفروض، وبين كل ذراع متناه، لكونه محصوراً بين حاصرين فيتناهى الكل، لأنه لا يزيد على ذلك إلا بواحد، أو يقال إلا ذراع مترتبة في الوضع، فنطبق بين قبلياتها وبعدياتها، فإن لم يتساويا [ بطل التضايف وإن تساويا ] لزم وجود ذراع له بعدية لا قبلية، لأن للمبدأ قبلية لا
He said: 'And the aspects have multiplied' with modification in the three. That is, the aspects of reasoning for the finitude of dimensions with modification in the three proofs, and with the help of the proofs for refuting infinite regress. As for the aspects of modification in the ladder proof, they have preceded. As for the parallelism proof, there are two aspects: First: the proof of extrication (برهان التخلص), and its explanation is that if infinity of dimensions were possible, then we could suppose a sphere from whose center a non-finite line emerges, accompanying it, intersecting another non-finite line, and that sphere rotates on itself. Then necessarily the line emerging from its center after intersection becomes parallel and then parallel [overlapping?]. But that is impossible because it depends on the extrication of one line from the other, which is conceivable only by a point that is from one of the two lines, and we have supposed them both non-finite. This is a contradiction. It is objected to by denying the possibility of motion of the non-finite line, especially such that it goes from intersection to parallelism to coincidence. Abu al-Barakat raised this objection against the parallelism proof and was followed by the author of al-Ishraq in al-Mutarahat, but it appears to have no basis, because the supposed motion there is of the diameter of the sphere, and it is finite. Second: the proof of parallelism (برهان الموازاة), which is that we suppose the diameter of the sphere is parallel to the non-finite line, then parallel to it by the rotation of the sphere. Then from the ending of the parallelism, there necessarily follows in the non-finite line a point that is the last point of the parallelism, and that is impossible, because every point is supposed likewise. So the parallelism of what is above it after the parallelism with it. As for the application proof, for example we suppose the non-finite distance in cubits, then we consider the application between the number of thousands thereof and the number of units as has passed in the infinite regress. Or it is said: what is between the actual or supposed beginning and every cubit is finite because it is confined between two confines, so the whole becomes finite, because it does not exceed that except by one. Or it is said: except cubits arranged in position, so we apply between their anterior parts and their posterior parts. If they are not equal, the mutual relation is invalidated; if they are equal, there follows the existence of a cubit that has a posteriority without anteriority, because the beginning has an anteriority without [posteriority?]...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage discusses modifications to the three proofs for finitude of dimensions and addresses an objection. It begins by noting that many modifications have been made, using the refutation of infinite regress. The ladder proof's modifications are already mentioned; for the parallelism proof, there are two modifications. The first, called برهان التخلص (proof of extrication), supposes a sphere with a finite diameter and an infinite line, and rotation leads to a contradiction because it would require extrication of one infinite line from another. An objection by Abu al-Barakat and the author of al-Ishraq denies the possibility of moving an infinite line. The author dismisses this objection because the moving line (the diameter) is finite. The second modification, برهان الموازاة (proof of parallelism), also involves a sphere diameter and infinite line, leading to the conclusion that the infinite line would have a last point of parallelism, which is impossible. For the application proof, modifications include applying infinite distances in cubits and examining correspondence between parts, similar to arguments against infinite regress. The example concludes that if correspondence leads to a cubit with only posteriority, it is impossible.

Key Terms
برهان التخلص
برهان المسامتة
أبو البركات
صاحب الإشراق
المطارحات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection by Abu al-Barakat shows that the parallelism proof is invalid because an infinite line cannot move.
Correction: The author explains that the moving line is not the infinite line but the finite diameter of the sphere, so the objection does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the supposed moving object in the proof of extrication that leads to the contradiction?
A) The infinite line emerging from the sphere
B) The diameter of the sphere
C) The sphere itself
D) The point of intersection

Key Takeaway
The proof of extrication overcomes the objection of moving an infinite line by using a finite rotating diameter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof of extrication forces you to accept that infinite dimensions are impossible. How does this logical certainty about the physical world affect your confidence in using reason to know God? In what area of your life are you still trying to 'extricate' yourself from infinity (e.g., endless desires, worries, or ambitions) rather than accepting finitude and submitting to the Creator?
?
The objection of Abu al-Barakat was based on a misunderstanding of which line was moving. Are there any objections to Islamic teachings that you have accepted without fully understanding the argument? What step can you take this week to properly investigate one such objection using primary sources?
?
The proof assumes a finite diameter moving. Your life is that diameter—finite, bounded by birth and death. Are you living as if your days are infinite? What specific finite resource (time, energy, focus) are you wasting on meaningless pursuits that could be directed towards what truly matters for your salvation?

The Proof of Parallelism and Applications of the Application Proof
Second modification of parallelism proof and variations of the application proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second modification of the parallelism proof and the various formulations of the application proof for finitude.

Core Idea
The parallelism proof is reinforced by a finite diameter, and the application proof uses correspondence of parts to demonstrate finitude.

After presenting the proof of extrication, the author introduces another modification called the proof of parallelism. He then turns to the application proof, which offers several ways to argue for finitude by comparing parts of an infinite series. These arguments supplement the earlier proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the second modification
Examine the proof of parallelism: it assumes a finite diameter rotating to become parallel to an infinite line, leading to an impossible last point.
2
Understand the logic
Why does the assumption of parallelism ending force a last point? Because the infinite line would have to have a point where the diameter is no longer parallel.
The text says 'يلزم في الخط الغير المتناهي نقطة آخر نقطة المسامتة'.
3
Analyze the application proof versions
The author gives three versions: cubit-counting, between beginning and each cubit, and applying anterior/posterior parts.
Look for 'برهان التطبيق' and the different formulations.
4
Note the incomplete ending
The passage ends abruptly, but the intended conclusion is that infinite dimensions lead to a contradiction.
The final sentence is incomplete; the OCR shows a break.

Infinite Regress Invalidates Correlation
Proof that infinite regress breaks priority-posteriority correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite series of priorities and posteriorities is impossible due to the breakdown of correlation.

Core Idea
Priority without posteriority breaks the correlation, making infinite regress impossible.

This lesson builds on the concept of correlation (التضايف) to show that an infinite chain of priority and posteriority cannot exist. The author argues that if such a series were possible, the first term would have priority without a corresponding posterior, which contradicts the nature of correlation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the logical flow.
The passage is concise but dense.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
Notice that the argument consists of two related points: first about applying the series, then about the first term specifically.
Both point to the same conclusion.
3
Understand the contradiction
Ask yourself: Why does priority without posteriority invalidate the correlation? The commentary will clarify.
Correlation requires each term to have a counterpart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعدية، وأيضاً إذا طبقنا وقعت قبلية الأول بإزاء بعدية الثاني، وقبلية الثاني بإزاء بعدية الثالث وهكذا إلى غير النهاية، فتبقى قبلية بلا بعدية فيبطل التضايف. وأيضاً للأول قبلية بلا بعدية فلو كان لكل ما عداه قبلية وبعدية معاً لزم قبلية بلا بعدية.
Posteriority, and also if we apply, the priority of the first occurs opposite the posteriority of the second, and the priority of the second opposite the posteriority of the third, and so on to infinity, so priority remains without posteriority, and thus the correlation (التضايف) becomes invalid. And also the first has priority without posteriority, so if everything else had both priority and posteriority together, then priority without posteriority would be necessary.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a proof that an infinite regress in the relation of priority and posteriority is impossible. The author argues that if we consider a series where each item is prior to the next, the first item has priority but no posterior because the series is infinite with no last item. This breaks the mutual correlation (تضايف) between priority and posteriority, since they require each other. Furthermore, if every other item in the series has both priority and posteriority, the first still lacks a posterior, which is absurd. Thus, such an infinite series cannot exist.

Key Terms
قبلية (priority)
بعدية (posteriority)
تضايف (correlation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that any infinite series is impossible.
Correction: The argument only applies to series involving the relational concepts of priority and posteriority which require mutual correlation. Other infinite series, like numbers, are not affected.

Checkpoint
What does the argument claim about the first item in an infinite series of priorities and posteriorities?
A) It has both priority and posteriority.
B) It has priority without posteriority.
C) It has posteriority without priority.
D) It has neither.

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress in priority and posteriority is impossible because the first term would lack a posterior, invalidating the correlation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that an infinite chain of causes cannot exist—there must be a First. Do you live as if you are your own first cause, or do you acknowledge that your existence depends on a First? What would change today if you truly lived in the awareness that you are not self-sufficient?
?
The argument reveals that priority without posteriority breaks the correlation. In your spiritual life, have you prioritized intentions (قبلية) without following through with actions (بعدية)? How can you restore the correlation between your beliefs and your deeds?
?
If infinite regress is impossible, then every chain of dependency must terminate in an independent First. Are you willing to examine the chains of dependency in your own life—what you rely on for meaning, security, and identity—and see if they terminate in the only truly Independent? What step can you take today to anchor yourself in the First?

First Objection: Beyond the World is Not Pure Nothing – Refuted as Illusion
First opponent objection and its refutation as mere illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection based on spatial differentiation is refuted by the concept of illusion (وهم) in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Spatial differences are mere illusions, not proofs of a real beyond.

The passage presents the first objection against the claim that beyond the world is absolute nothing. The opponent argues that since we can distinguish south from north and measure relative quantities, there must be something there. The author cuts through the argument with a single word: وهم (illusion). Let's see how this response works.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the opponent uses spatial distinctions (south vs north, quarter vs half) to argue for a real beyond.
The opponent assumes that differentiation implies existence.
2
Identify the author's response
Observe the succinct refutation: "We say: mere illusion." The author dismisses the entire argument.
3
Understand the concept of 'وهم'
In kalam, 'وهم' refers to a cognitive faculty that can produce false impressions. This refutation means the differentiation has no extra-mental reality.
It is not a refutation of the distinction itself, but of its reality outside the mind.
4
Connect to broader themes
See how this preserves the doctrine that beyond the world is sheer nothingness, a key position in Islamic cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الأول: أن ما يلي الجنوب غير ما يلي الشمال وما يوازي ربع العالم أقل مما يوازي نصفه فلا يكون عدماً محضاً. قلنا مجرد وهم.
The first objection: That what adjoins the south is different from what adjoins the north, and what parallels a quarter of the world is less than what parallels half of it, so it is not pure nothing. We say: mere illusion (وهم).
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection from the opponent against the claim that beyond the world is nothing. The opponent argues that since there is differentiation (south vs north, quantitative differences), there must be something real beyond. The author responds succinctly that this differentiation is mere illusion (وهم)—it has no basis in reality.

Key Terms
وهم (illusion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial differences such as direction and quantity imply real extramundane space beyond the world.
Correction: Such differences are mere illusion (وهم); they have no extra-mental reality and therefore do not disprove that beyond the world is absolute nothing.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the first objection that the beyond cannot be pure nothing because of differences?
A) He agrees and provides additional arguments.
B) He says it is mere illusion.
C) He says it is a contradiction.
D) He says it is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first objection based on spatial differentiation is dismissed as mere illusion, preserving the doctrine of absolute nothingness beyond the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been convinced by an argument that seemed logical but was actually based on an illusion in your mind? How can you train yourself to distinguish between real reasoning and mere imagination in your own beliefs about God and the unseen?
?
The author dismisses the opponent's spatial distinctions as mere وهم. Consider the 'spatial distinctions' in your own life—comparisons of status, wealth, location—that you treat as real. Are they mere illusions? What would it mean for you to see them as such and focus on what truly matters?
?
This lesson shows that even seemingly rational arguments can be false if based on faulty perception. Are you willing to re-examine your own deeply held convictions that rely on sensory or imaginative evidence? What would it take to surrender your illusions to the truth of revelation?

Refuting the Hand Extension Dilemma
Second opponent objection about hand extension and its refutation via condition absence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's dilemma about extending a hand at the world's edge and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between the absence of a condition and the presence of an impediment.

Core Idea
Impossibility can stem from a missing condition, not just an obstacle.

This lesson presents a classic kalam objection against the idea that beyond the world is a void. The opponent tries to force a dilemma: either there is empty space or an impediment. Al-Sanusi's response sharpens our understanding of rational categories by introducing the notion of a condition whose absence prevents an action without requiring an obstacle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice the opponent presents two possibilities: extension implies dimension, inability implies impediment.
The objection uses 'إما... أو' (either...or) structure.
2
Identify the two horns
First horn: if the hand can extend, there is dimension (بعد). Second horn: if it cannot, there is an impediment (مانع). The opponent thinks these are the only options.
3
Analyze the response
The author admits the hand cannot extend, but denies it is due to an impediment. Instead, it is due to the absence of a condition (شرط). The condition for extending a hand is having a space to extend into, which is absent beyond the world.
The Arabic emphasizes 'لا لوجود المانع' (not due to existence of an impediment).
4
Reflect on the distinction
This distinction is crucial: impossibility can be of two kinds – due to an obstacle or due to lack of prerequisite. The opponent's error was to conflate them.
This shows the importance of precise rational analysis in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: أن الواقف على طرف العالم إما أن يمكنه مد اليد فثمة بعد، أو لا فثمة مانع. قلنا: لا يمكن لعدم الشرط لا لوجود المانع.
The second objection: That the one standing at the edge of the world either can extend his hand—then there is dimension (بعد), or cannot—then there is an impediment (مانع). We say: It is not possible due to the absence of the condition (شرط), not due to the existence of an impediment.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection from the opponent. The opponent argues that if a person at the world's edge can extend his hand, there must be empty space (بعد); if not, there must be an obstacle. The author replies that the hand cannot be extended because the condition for extension (i.e., the existence of a place to extend into) is absent, not because an obstacle exists. This response preserves the possibility that beyond the world is nothing without needing an obstacle.

Key Terms
بعد
مانع
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something cannot happen, there must be an obstacle preventing it.
Correction: The author shows that inability can also stem from the absence of a necessary condition, not only from an impediment.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can the person at the edge of the world not extend his hand?
A) Because an obstacle blocks the hand.
B) Because the condition for extension is absent.
C) Because the hand cannot move beyond the world.
D) Because the world is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The absence of a condition can explain impossibility without requiring an impediment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to do something good—like praying at night or seeking knowledge—do you immediately assume an external obstacle is blocking you, or do you honestly ask whether you are missing a condition (like sincerity, discipline, or time)? Pause and identify one such condition you have neglected this week.
?
You believe there is absolutely nothing beyond this universe but your Lord. In your daily life, do you live as if the world's edge is real—that your hand cannot reach beyond it—or do you still grasp for things that only this world can give? What 'space' are you trying to extend your hand into that has no real existence?
?
The author makes a fine distinction between 'no impediment' and 'missing condition.' In your spiritual journey, are you blaming false impediments (e.g., 'I'm too busy') when the real issue is that you have not set the condition for action? Name one condition you can establish today.

Body as a Universal: Third Objection and Refutation
Third objection: universality of body implies infinite individuals; refutation by conceptual possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the universality of body does not necessitate an actual infinite number of individuals, and how conceptual possibility differs from actual existence.

Core Idea
Universality only allows possible individuals; it does not force infinite actual existence.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that since 'body' is a universal concept, it can have infinite possible individuals. The opponent claims these individuals must exist due to the generality of emanation. The author clarifies that universality only implies conceptual possibility, not actual simultaneous existence, and that the impossibility of an actual infinite prevents such a conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponent argues that a universal like 'body' is not confined to one particular, so its possible individuals are infinite and must exist due to emanation.
Notice the key terms: 'كلي' (universal) and 'فيض' (emanation).
2
Understand the refutation
The author distinguishes between conceptual possibility and actual existence. Universality only allows hypothetical individuals, and the impossibility of an actual infinite (proved earlier) shows they cannot all exist together.
3
Grasp the precise meaning of 'possibility of infinite individuals'
It means there is no limit to potential individuals, not that all can simultaneously exist. This resolves the objection.
This is often misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: أن الجسم كلي لا ينحصر في شخص، فلا تتناهى أفراده الممكنة كسائر الكليات، بل توجد لعموم الفيض. قلنا الكلية لا تقتضي سوى إمكان كثرة أفراده المفروضة بالنظر إلى مفهومه، ولا ينافي امتناع كلها أو بعضها بموجب كلزوم المحالات المذكورة، على أن معنى إمكان افراده الغير المتناهية ليس اجتماعها في الوجود على ما هو المطلوب، بل عدم انتهائها إلى حد لا يمكن بعده وجود فرد آخر.
The third objection: That body is a universal (كلي), not confined to a particular, so its possible individuals are infinite like other universals; rather they exist due to the generality of emanation (عموم الفيض). We say: Universality does not imply more than the possibility of the multiplicity of its hypothetical individuals with respect to its concept, and it does not contradict the impossibility of all or some of them due to the necessary implications of the aforementioned absurdities. Moreover, the meaning of the possibility of its infinite individuals is not their simultaneous existence in reality, as is required, but rather their not ending at a limit beyond which another individual cannot exist.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection from the opponent and the author's response. The opponent argues that 'body' is a universal concept, so it can have infinite individuals, just like other universals, and these individuals exist due to the general emanation (عموم الفيض). The author replies that universality only indicates the conceptual possibility of many individuals; it does not necessitate actual existence of all. The previously demonstrated absurdities of infinite regress show that an actual infinite is impossible. Furthermore, the 'possibility of infinite individuals' means that there is no final limit to potential individuals, not that all actually exist simultaneously.

Key Terms
كلي (universal)
فيض (emanation)
إمكان (possibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Body as a universal necessitates infinite actual individuals.
Correction: The author explains that universality only implies conceptual possibility, not actual existence; the impossibility of an actual infinite prevents simultaneous existence.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the meaning of 'the possibility of infinite individuals'?
A) All individuals actually exist simultaneously.
B) There is no limit to potential individuals.
C) Individuals are infinite in concept only.
D) The universal necessitates infinite actual individuals.

Key Takeaway
Universality of a concept allows possible individuals but does not require their actual infinite existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says universality only implies possibility, not actuality. How does this challenge your tendency to assume that what you can imagine must happen in your life? Can you recall a time you felt frustrated because a possible outcome didn't occur? How might this lesson change your perspective?
?
Consider the concept of 'عموم الفيض' (general emanation). Do you ever feel that God's generosity is incomplete if He doesn't give you everything you think is good for you? What does this refutation say about the relationship between possibility and divine action?

Further Response: The First Two Objections Prove a Real Beyond
Response to criticism that first two objections only show something exists, not its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first two objections, while not directly proving an infinite body, refute the claim that the beyond is pure nothing and, with additional premises, establish that it is an infinite body.

Core Idea
The first two objections prove at least that the beyond is not nothing—more follows with reasoning.

This passage addresses a criticism that the first two objections (from differentiation and hand extension) only show that something exists beyond the world, not its nature. The author responds that even this is enough to refute pure nothingness, and with other premises we can deduce that the beyond is an infinite body.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: the critic says the first two objections only show something exists, not that it is a body or infinite.
The objection is a common one in theology: does the proof go far enough?
2
Analyze the response
The author grants that the first two objections alone don't prove a body or infinity, but they do refute the claim of pure nothing. Then with additional premises (about void and edge), they can show it's a body and infinite.
Notice the author uses a 'yes, but also' move to strengthen the argument.
3
Reflect on the dialectic
Consider how this response defends the overall argument by showing that the objections are part of a cumulative case, not standalone proofs.
This shows the importance of premises in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الأولان لا يفيدان، سوى أن وراء العالم أمراً له تحقق ما من غير دلالة على أنه جسم أو بعد، ولو سلّم فلا دلالة على أنه غير متناه. قلنا يفيد أن بطلان رأى من زعم أنه عدم محض، ثم يدلان على تمام المطلوب بمعونة مقدمات معلومة، مثل أن ما يلاقي طرف العالم لا يكون إلا خلاء، وهو بعد أو جسم، وهو ذو بعد بل إذا بين استحالة الخلاء تعين أنه جسم، ولا يكون متناهياً وإلا لكان له طرف فيعود الكلام، ويثبت أن ما وراءه ليس عدماً محضاً.
If it is said: The first two [objections] do not prove anything other than that beyond the world there is something with some reality, without indicating that it is a body or dimension, and even if conceded, they do not indicate that it is infinite. We say: They prove the falsity of the view of one who claims that it is pure nothing, and then they prove the full required conclusion with the aid of known premises, such that whatever touches the edge of the world can only be void (خلاء), and it is a dimension (بعد) or a body (جسم), and it is extended (ذو بعد); rather, if the impossibility of void is demonstrated, it is determined to be a body, and it is not finite, otherwise it would have an edge and the argument would recur, and it is established that what is beyond it is not pure nothing.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage addresses a criticism that the first two objections (from differentiation and hand extension) only show that something exists beyond the world, but not its nature (whether it is a body or dimension) or whether it is infinite. The author responds that these objections at least refute the view that the beyond is pure nothing. Moreover, with additional known premises—such as that whatever touches the edge of the world must be void, and that void is either a dimension or a body, and that void is impossible—it follows that the beyond must be a body. Furthermore, this body must be infinite, for if it were finite it would have an edge, leading to the same question again, thus establishing that the beyond is not pure nothing but an infinite body.

Key Terms
خلاء
بعد
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first two objections alone prove that the beyond is an infinite body.
Correction: The author explicitly states that they require additional known premises to reach the full conclusion; they only directly refute pure nothing.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do the first two objections definitively prove?
A) That the beyond is an infinite body.
B) That the beyond is a dimension.
C) That the beyond is not pure nothing.
D) That the beyond is void.

Key Takeaway
The first two objections, though initially seeming weak, can be built upon with additional premises to prove that the world is bounded by an infinite body, which is not nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that even minimal evidence (the first two objections) disproves the claim that the beyond is 'pure nothing.' In your own life, where have you dismissed something as 'nothing'—a doubt, a nudge, a truth you avoided—even though evidence for its reality was already there? Will you honestly re-examine that area today?
?
The argument builds from simple premises to a profound conclusion about an infinite reality beyond this world. If you truly believe that the beyond is not empty but an infinite body (or something greater), how does that change the way you view your current struggles and ambitions? Are you living as if this world is all there is?
?
If you were to accept that the beyond is real and not nothing, what one concrete action would you be compelled to take this week to align your life with that reality? What is stopping you?

Direction as the Indivisible Terminus of Extension
Conclusion defining direction as the indivisible end of extension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of direction (جهة) as an existing, indivisible position at the limit of extension.

Core Idea
Direction is an existent, indivisible point—not a void—at the end of extension.

In this concluding passage, the author precisely defines 'direction' (جهة) within the context of the finitude of dimensions. He distinguishes it from extension itself, showing that direction is an existing, indivisible position that serves as the endpoint for movement and indication. This narrow definition prevents infinite regress and clarifies the nature of spatial limits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the literal translation. Note the key terms 'امتداد', 'جهة', 'وضع', 'منتهى الإشارة'.
2
Identify the three conceptual components
The passage defines direction from three angles: as the extremity of extension, as the limit of indication, and as the goal of movement. Then it asserts its existence, position, and indivisibility.
3
Understand why indivisibility is necessary
If direction were divisible, movement could occur within it, making that direction itself have a further direction, leading to infinite regress. Hence, it must be a simple point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (خاتمة) فطرف الامتداد بالنسبة إليه نهاية، ومن حيث كونه منتهى الإشارة، ومقصد المتحرك بالحصول فيه جهة، فتكون موجودة ذات وضع لا يقبل الانقسام وإلا لوقعت الحركة فيها فتكون الجهة منتهاها.
He said (Conclusion): The extremity of extension in relation to it is an end, and from the perspective of its being the limit of indication (منتهى الإشارة) and the goal of the mover by occurring in it, it is a direction (جهة). So it is existent, possessing a position (وضع) that does not accept division, otherwise movement would occur in it, and thus the direction would be its limit.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage provides a conclusion (خاتمة) on the topic of direction (جهة) as it relates to the discussion of the finitude of dimensions. The author defines the extremity of extension as an end (نهاية) relative to the extension itself. From the perspective of being that to which pointing (الإشارة) is directed and that which a moving object seeks to reach, it is called a direction. This direction is existent and has a position (وضع) that is indivisible. If it were divisible, then movement could occur within it, but then that direction would itself have a further direction as its limit, leading to regress. Thus, the direction is an indivisible point at the end of extension.

Key Terms
امتداد (extension)
جهة (direction)
وضع (position)
منتهى الإشارة (limit of indication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Direction is a void or empty space.
Correction: Direction is an existent, indivisible terminus, not a void.

Checkpoint

A) It is divisible.
B) It is a void.
C) It is indivisible.
D) It is a body.

Key Takeaway
Direction is an existing, indivisible position that forms the limit of extension, ruling out divisibility and voidness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is a continuous movement through time and space. What is the indivisible point – the direction you are truly seeking? Is it a singular, undivided goal, or is it fragmented by distractions?
?
The direction here is a point that does not accept division. In your pursuit of knowledge and faith, do you treat your ultimate purpose as an indivisible reality, or do you allow it to be split into competing loyalties?
?
If direction is the limit of extension, then your lifespan has a finite extension. Are you consciously moving toward your ultimate direction, or are you merely continuing in motion without regard for the endpoint?

Three Views on Bodies: Originated or Pre-Eternal?
The author enumerates three possibilities for bodies: originated in both, eternal in both, or eternal in essence only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical positions on whether bodies are originated or pre-eternal, as presented by the author in his commentary.

Core Idea
Three views on bodies: originated by creation, pre-eternal in full, or pre-eternal in essence alone.

The author of the commentary now expands on the matn's claim that bodies are temporally originated by surveying the major philosophical and theological positions. Understanding these three views is crucial for situating the orthodox Muslim position within the broader history of thought about the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is enumerative: 'first', 'second', 'third'.
2
Identify the first view
The religious view holds that bodies are originated in both their essences and attributes. This is the position of Muslims and other religious groups.
This view affirms creation ex nihilo.
3
Examine the second view in detail
The Aristotelian view claims pre-eternity for bodies, but the author clarifies that particular movements are originated and elemental bodies have originated specific properties.
Distinguish between celestial and elemental bodies in this view.
4
Analyze the third view
The earlier philosophers argue for pre-eternity of essences only, not attributes. They disagreed on whether that essence is a body and what it consists of.
This view is less unified than the first two.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author presents these views and how each impacts the Islamic theological position on creation.
The first view is the only one fully consistent with Islamic orthodoxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي ومن أحكام الجسم أنها محدثة بالزمان والاحتمالات الممكنة ههنا ثلاثة : الأول : حدوث الأجسام بذواتها وصفاتها وإليه ذهب أرباب الملل من المسلمين وغيرهم. والثاني: قدمها كذلك وإليه ذهب أرسطو وشيعته، ونعني بالصفة ما يعم الصور والأعراض وتفصيل مذهبهم أن الأجسام الفلكية قديمة بموادها وصورها وأعراضها من الضوء والشكل وأصل الحركة والوضع بمعنى أنها متحركة حركة واحدة متصلة من الأزل إلى الأبد. إلا أن كل حركة تفرض من حركاتها فهي مسبوقة بأخرى، فتكون حادثة، وكذا الوضع والعنصريات قديمة بموادها وصورها الجسمية، وكذا صورها النوعية بحسب الجنس بمعنى أنها لم تزل عنصراً ما، لكن خصوصية النارية أو الهوائية أو المائية أو الأرضية، لا يلزم أن تكون قديمة لما سيجيء من قبول الكون والفساد. والثالث : قدمها بذواتها دون صفاتها، وإليه ذهب المتقدمون من الفلاسفة، واختلفوا في تلك الذات التي ادعوا قدمها أنها جسم أو ليست بجسم، وعلى تقدير الجسمية أنها العناصر الأربعة جملتها أو واحد منها، والبواقي بتلطيف أو تكثيف، دخان يرتفع من ذلك الجسم أو جوهره غير العناصر حدثت منها والسماء من دخان
That is, among the rulings of the body is that it is temporally originated. The possible probabilities here are three: First: the origination of bodies in their essences and attributes, and this is the position of the people of religions, Muslims and non-Muslims. Second: their pre-eternity likewise, and this is the position of Aristotle and his followers; by 'attribute' we mean what includes forms and accidents. The detail of their doctrine is that celestial bodies are pre-eternal in their matters, forms, and accidents such as light, shape, the origin of motion and position, meaning that they are in motion with one continuous motion from eternity past to eternity future, except that every particular motion that is supposed is preceded by another, so it is originated, and likewise for position. Elemental bodies are pre-eternal in their matters and corporeal forms, and likewise their specific forms according to genus, meaning that they have never ceased to be some element; but the particularity of being fiery, airy, watery, or earthy is not necessarily pre-eternal, due to what will come regarding the acceptance of generation and corruption. Third: their pre-eternity in their essences without their attributes, and this is the position of the earlier philosophers. They disagreed about that essence which they claimed to be pre-eternal, whether it is a body or not a body, and on the assumption of corporeality, whether it is the four elements collectively or one of them, and the rest are by subtlety or density, smoke rising from that body, or its substance other than the elements from which they originated, and the sky from smoke.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is the author's commentary on the matn. He begins by clarifying the meaning of the matn's statement that bodies are temporally originated. Then he presents three possible views. The first view is that of the milliyun. The second view, of Aristotle and his followers, is elaborated: celestial bodies are eternal but particular movements are originated; elemental bodies are eternal in matter and form but not in specific elemental properties. The third view, of earlier philosophers, holds that essences are eternal but not attributes, with various opinions on what that eternal substance is. The author lists several possibilities mentioned by them.

Key Terms
حدوث
قدم
جسم
صورة
عرض
فلكي
عنصري
كون وفساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Aristotelian view holds all bodies are eternal without any originated aspects.
Correction: The author clarifies that for Aristotle, individual motions of celestial bodies are originated, and elemental bodies have originated specific properties due to generation and corruption.

Checkpoint
According to Aristotle and his followers, what is not pre-eternal in elemental bodies?
A) Their matter
B) Their corporeal form
C) The particular elemental property (e.g., being fiery)
D) Their species form

Key Takeaway
The author classifies three positions on the temporal origination of bodies: full origination (religious), full pre-eternity with qualifications (Aristotelian), and pre-eternity of essence only (earlier philosophers).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first view holds that bodies are created in both essence and attributes—this is the religious position. Have you ever quietly entertained the possibility that matter might be eternal, even while professing belief in a Creator? What would it take to uproot that seed of doubt from your heart? Resolve to read Qur'anic verses on creation (e.g., Al-Anbiya 30) daily for a week and reflect on them.
?
The third view is a compromise: essence eternal, attributes created. Where in your own life do you compromise full submission to Allah's sovereignty? Do you act as though your own essence is self-sufficient, needing only external polish? Ask yourself: Is there an area of your faith where you accept the idea in theory but deny it in practice? Write down one specific area and make a concrete plan to align your actions with the truth that everything—including you—is entirely dependent on Allah. Read this lesson again and commit to living it.

First Proof: Bodies Are Never Free from Accidents
Al-Taftazani presents the first proof for the temporality of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the temporality of bodies, which argues that bodies always have accidents, and that motion is a key example of a temporal accident.

Core Idea
Bodies are never free from change; change proves they are not eternal.

Al-Taftazani begins his proofs for the temporality (createdness) of the physical world by establishing that bodies are never free from accidents—specifically, motion and rest. Since these accidents are inherently temporal, the bodies that bear them must also be temporal. In this lesson, we examine the first proof and its focused argument for motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the overall structure: a general claim, then two supporting reasons, then a detailed elaboration on motion.
The argument for rest is incomplete in this passage.
2
Identify the two parts of the proof
Distinguish the general argument (bodies are never free from accidents, and accidents are temporal) from the specific argument (motion and rest are temporal).
The passage uses 'لأنها لا تخلو عن الأعراض قطعاً' for the general argument.
3
Focus on motion's temporality
Notice the two reasons given: motion entails a prior state with non-coexistence of prior and posterior (the essence of temporality), and motion is perishable, which contradicts eternity.
The phrase 'سبقاً لا تجامع فيه للمتقدم والمتأخر' defines temporality.
4
Reflect on the incomplete proof for rest
The passage begins arguing that rest is existent ('وجودي') but is cut off. Consider how this might parallel the argument for motion.
The full proof for rest is not available in this window.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدله على أن الأجسام لا تخلو من الحوادث قال لنا وجوه) الأول: أن الأجسام لا تخلو عن الحوادث، لأنها لا تخلو عن الأعراض قطعاً، وهي حادثة لما تقرر من امتناع بقائها على الإطلاق، ولأنها لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون، لأن للجسم كوناً في الحيز لا محالة، فإن كان مسبوقاً بكون في ذلك الحيز فسكون وإلا فحركة، وكل منهما حادث. أما الحركة فلاقتضائها المسبوقية بالغير سبقاً لا تجامع فيه للمتقدم والمتأخر وهو معنى الحدوث، ولكونها في معرض الزوال قطعاً وهو ينافي القدم، وأما السكون فلأنه وجودي لكونه من
The proofs that bodies are never free from accidents. He said: We have [several] aspects. The first: that bodies are never free from accidents, because they are never free from accidents absolutely, and they are temporal due to the established impossibility of their persistence absolutely, and because they are never free from motion and rest, because a body must have a location in space; if it is preceded by a being in that location, it is rest, otherwise motion, and each of them is temporal. As for motion, because it entails being preceded by another with a precedence not admitting coexistence of the prior and posterior, which is the meaning of temporality, and because it is subject to passing away absolutely, which contradicts eternity. As for rest, because it is existent due to its being from...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a proof for the temporality of bodies. The author states the general proof: bodies are never free from accidents, and accidents are temporal. Then he specifies two reasons: (1) bodies are never free from accidents because they always have accidents, and accidents cannot persist, so they are temporal; (2) bodies are never free from motion and rest, and each is temporal. He then argues for motion's temporality in detail: motion requires a prior state, and this precedence involves non-coexistence of prior and posterior, which is the essence of temporality; additionally, motion is inherently perishable, contradicting eternity. The argument for rest is cut off, but it begins by stating rest is existent (wujudiyy) because it is...

Misconception: If bodies always have motion or rest, then motion and rest themselves must be eternal.
Correction: The passage shows that motion is temporal because it requires a prior state (non-coexistence) and is perishable. Thus bodies that always have motion are never free from temporality, implying their own temporality.

The first proof for the temporality of bodies relies on the fact that bodies always have temporal accidents, with motion clearly shown to be temporal due to its necessary prior state and perishability.

First Proof: Bodies Are Always Accompanied by Accidents
Al-Sanūsī's first proof that bodies are contingent via their inseparable accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalām proof for the contingency of bodies based on their never being free from accidents, specifically motion and rest.

Core Idea
Bodies are never free from motion or rest — both are contingent.

In this passage, al-Sanūsī presents the first of several proofs that bodies are contingent (ḥādith). The starting point is a simple observation: bodies are never without some accident, especially motion or rest. If we can show that both motion and rest are contingent (created, perishable), then the body itself, which is always in one of these states, must also be contingent. This lesson unpacks the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's argument come through clearly.
The passage is a single continuous proof.
2
Identify the main claim and sub-arguments
Notice that the passage first states the claim (bodies never free of accidents), then gives two separate arguments for motion and rest being contingent.
The argument for motion and rest are parallel.
3
Understand the reasoning for motion
Motion requires being preceded by another state and is perishable — both point to contingency.
4
Understand the reasoning for rest
Rest is an existent state that can cease (since bodies can move), so it is also contingent.
5
Reflect on the similarity argument
The passage ends by noting all bodies are alike in this respect, whether simple or composite, reinforcing the universality of the proof.
This ties the argument together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدله على أن الأجسام لا تخلو من الحوادث قال لنا وجوه الأول: أن الأجسام لا تخلو عن الحوادث، لأنها لا تخلو عن الأعراض قطعاً، وهي حادثة لما تقرر من امتناع بقائها على الإطلاق، ولأنها لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون، لأن للجسم كونا في الحيز لا محالة، فإن كان مسبوقاً بكون في ذلك الحيز فسكون وإلا فحركة، وكل منهما حادث. أما الحركة فلاقتضائها المسبوقية بالغير سبقاً لا تجامع فيه للمتقدم والمتأخر وهو معنى الحدوث، ولكونها في معرض الزوال قطعاً وهو ينافي القدم، وأما السكون فلأنه وجودي لكونه من الأكوان، وجائز الزوال لكون كل جسم قابلاً للحركة بالاتفاق، وبدليل أنها متماثلة يجوز على كل ما يجوز على الآخر، وأنها إما بسائط يصح لكل من أجزائها المتشابهة ما يصح للآخر من الحيز، وإما مركبات يصح لكل جزء من بسائطها أن يماس الآخر، وما ذاك إلا بالحركة
The proofs that bodies are not devoid of accidents: he said we have aspects. The first: that bodies are not devoid of accidents, because they are not devoid of attributes definitely, and they are contingent due to what has been established of the impossibility of their subsisting at all, and because they are not devoid of motion and rest, because for a body there is a being in space inevitably, so if it is preceded by a being in that space, then it is rest, otherwise it is motion, and both of them are contingent. As for motion, because it requires being preceded by another with a precedence that does not allow the conjunction of the prior and the posterior, and that is the meaning of contingency, and because it is subject to perishing definitely, which contradicts pre-eternity. And as for rest, because it is existent, being one of the states, and is possible of perishing because every body is capable of motion by agreement, and by the evidence that they are similar, what is permissible for one is permissible for the other, and that they are either simples so that it is valid for each of their similar parts what is valid for the other from space, or composites so that it is valid for each part of their simples to touch the other, and that is only through motion.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that bodies are not devoid of accidents (hawadith), which is a standard kalam argument for the contingency of bodies. The proof begins by stating that bodies are never free from accidents, especially attributes (a'rad) which are known to be contingent because they cannot subsist permanently. More specifically, bodies are never free from motion or rest. A body must have a 'being' (kawn) in space: if that being is preceded by another being in the same space, it is rest; if not preceded, it is motion. Both motion and rest are shown to be contingent. Motion requires being preceded (by a prior state) in a way that prevents the conjunction of prior and posterior—this is the essence of huduth. Motion is also perishable, contradicting qidam. Rest is an existent (wujudi) state that can cease, since every body is capable of motion. The argument from similarity indicates that all bodies are alike: if one can move, all can. Bodies are either simple (basit) or composite (murakkab). For simples, each part shares the same spatial properties; for composites, parts touch each other through motion. Hence bodies are inherently tied to motion.

البسائط

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies can be free from accidents at some point.
Correction: The proof shows that bodies always have at least motion or rest, so they are never free from accidents.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is an existent state that can cease
C) Because it is always accompanied by motion
D) Because it is the absence of motion

Key Takeaway
Bodies cannot be free of motion or rest; since both are fleeting, bodies themselves are contingent.

First Objection and Answer: The Body at Origination
Objection and answer regarding a pre-eternal body's being not preceded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a pre-eternal body's being is not preceded by another being, and how the answer redefines pre-eternity to show that each being in infinite time is still preceded.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity means continuous existence through infinite time, not a state without prior being.

This lesson presents the first objection to the definition of motion as a being in space not preceded by another being. The objector uses the case of a pre-eternal body to challenge this definition. The answer clarifies the meaning of pre-eternity and resolves the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the structure: objection summarily and in detail, then answer.
The Arabic uses 'نوقض' to signal an objection.
2
Identify the objection's two parts
The objection is given 'summarily' (by the body at origination) and 'in detail' (they don't concede that a being not preceded in the same space is motion). Understand the reasoning: motion requires being preceded in a different space; in pre-eternity, no prior different space exists because pre-eternity negates being preceded.
The objector argues that the definition fails for a pre-eternal body.
3
Understand the answer's core point
The answer states that the discussion concerns a 'being' that is preceded by another being. Pre-eternity is not a state where a being exists with no prior being; rather, it means continuous existence through infinite past times.
Thus every being in an eternal body is still preceded by another being.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the answer preserves the definition of motion while accounting for the pre-eternal case. The key is redefining pre-eternity as a temporal continuum, not a timeless state.
This objection–answer pair illustrates a common dialectical move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونوقض إجمالاً بالجسم حال الحدوث، وتفصيلاً بأنا لا نسلّم أن الكون إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالكون في ذلك الحيز كان حركة، وإنما يلزم لو كان مسبوقاً يكون في حيز آخر ولا كذلك في الأزل، لأن أزليته ينافي المسبوقته والجواب : أن الكلام في الكون المسبوق بكون آخر وليس الأزل حالة يتحقق فيها كون لا كون قبله، بل معنى الأزلية الاستمرار في الأزمنة المقررة الماضية الغير المتناهية
And it was objected summarily by the body at the moment of origination, and in detail by [the claim] that we do not concede that the being, if not preceded by a being in that same space, is motion; it would only be necessary if it were preceded by a being in another space, and not so in pre-eternity, because its pre-eternity negates being preceded. And the answer: that the discussion is about the being preceded by another being, and pre-eternity is not a state in which there is a being with no being before it; rather the meaning of pre-eternity is the continuation through the determined past infinite times.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the proof and its answer. The objector argues against the definition of motion as a being in space not preceded by a being in that same space. He points to the case of a pre-eternal (azali) body at the moment of its origination (huduth). He claims that if a body is pre-eternal, its being in space is not preceded by a being in that space, but this does not entail motion because motion requires being preceded by a being in a different space, which does not apply in pre-eternity. In pre-eternity, there is no prior different space. The answer clarifies that the discussion is about a 'being' that is preceded by another being (i.e., contingent beings). Pre-eternity (azaliyya) should not be understood as a state where a being exists without any prior being. Rather, the meaning of azaliyya is continuous existence through infinite past times. Thus, every being in an eternal body is still preceded by another being.

Correction: Pre-eternity means continuous existence through infinite past times, so every being in that sequence is still preceded by another being.

Refutation of Eternal Universal Motion
Objection and three-part refutation regarding eternal motion as a universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi refutes the objection that motion could be eternal as a universal, using three arguments targeting its essence, the universal-particular relation, and the impossibility of infinite succession.

Core Idea
Motion's essence requires being preceded, so even its universal cannot be eternal.

This passage addresses a sophisticated objection to the proof of the world's contingency. The objector concedes that individual motions cannot be eternal, but suggests that motion as a universal category could be, even if each particular is preceded by another in an infinite regress. Sanusi responds with three decisive arguments that dismantle this possibility from different angles: from the definition of motion, from the metaphysics of universals, and from the absurdity of an infinite series.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's claim: while particular motions cannot be eternal, the universal essence of motion could be, since each motion is preceded by another without beginning.
Note that this objection targets the premise 'whatever is never devoid of accidents is contingent' by assuming accidents could be infinite.
2
Identify the three answers
List the three answers given by Sanusi. Each attacks a different aspect: the definition of motion, the universal-particular relationship, and the validity of an infinite succession.
3
Examine the first answer
Reflect on why motion's reality as 'change from state to state' necessarily implies being preceded by a prior state, making pre-eternity impossible.
This shows that being preceded is not accidental to motion but essential.
4
Examine the second answer
Consider why a universal cannot be eternal if all its particulars are contingent. Since the universal exists only through its particulars, its alleged pre-eternity would require at least one particular to be eternal, which is false.
5
Examine the third answer
Understand that an infinite succession of accidents (whether motions or not) is inherently impossible, as argued elsewhere in kalam.
This answer appeals to the established principle that an actual infinite is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : امتناع أزلية الحركات الجزئية لا يوجب امتناع أزلية ماهيتها الكلية فيجوز أن يكون كل حركة مسبوقة بحركة لا إلى بداية، ويكون الجسم متحركاً أزلاً وأبداً بمعنى أنه لا يقرر زمان إلا وفيه شيء من جزئيات الحركة، وبهذا يقع القدح في أن ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث، لأن ذلك إنما هو على تقدير تناهي الحوادث، فالعمدة الوثقى في هذا الباب أن تبين امتناع تعاقب حوادث لا نهاية لها . أجيب: أولاً بأن حقيقة الحركة هي التغير من حال إلى حال. فالمسبوقية المنافية للأزلية من لوازم ماهيتها . وثانياً: بأن الكلي لا يوجد إلا في ضمن الجزئي، فقدم الحركة مع حدوث كل من الجزئيات غير معقول. وثالثاً: بأن تعاقب الحوادث لا إلى بداية سواء كانت حركات أو غيرها باطل
For if it is said: The impossibility of the pre-eternity of particular motions does not necessitate the impossibility of the pre-eternity of their universal essence; so it is possible that every motion is preceded by a motion without beginning, and that the body is moving eternally past and future, meaning that no time is determined except that there is something of the particular motions in it; and by this the objection is raised against the claim that whatever is not devoid of accidents is contingent, because that is only on the assumption of the finitude of accidents. So the most reliable basis in this chapter is to demonstrate the impossibility of the succession of accidents that have no end. The answer: first, that the reality of motion is change from state to state; thus being preceded, which contradicts pre-eternity, is a necessary concomitant of its essence. Second, that the universal does not exist except within the particular; so the pre-eternity of motion along with the contingency of each particular is unintelligible. Third, that the succession of accidents without beginning, whether motions or others, is false.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the proof and a three-part answer. The objector argues that while individual motions cannot be eternal (since each is preceded), the universal essence of motion could be eternal even if each particular motion is preceded by another in an infinite regress. This would allow the body to be moving eternally without beginning. This objection aims to undermine the argument that whatever is never devoid of accidents must be contingent, as that argument assumed the accidents are finite. The objector claims that the real issue is to prove the impossibility of an infinite succession of accidents. The answer refutes this: (1) The reality of motion is change from state to state, so being preceded by a prior state is essential to motion, making it impossible to be eternal. (2) A universal only exists within its particulars; so eternal motion while each particular is contingent is contradictory. (3) The succession of accidents without beginning, whether motions or other accidents, is false in itself.

Correction: The three arguments show that motion's essence necessitates being preceded, the universal depends on particulars, and an infinite succession is impossible, so the universal cannot be eternal.

The 'Now' Method for Proving a First Event
Taftazani presents the 'now' method as a proof against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the 'now' method uses the comparison of two infinite series to demonstrate that an infinite regress of past events is impossible.

Core Idea
Mapping two infinite 'now' series forces either absurd equality or a finite end.

Taftazani begins his proof of a first event with a clever thought experiment. Imagine two infinite sequences of moments—one starting from the present and the other from an ancient point like the Flood. By aligning them, we either get a contradiction or a necessary stopping point. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
The method assumes two infinite series of 'nows': one from the present moment backward, and another from the day of the Flood backward, each without end. Take a moment to grasp this initial assumption.
This is a hypothetical used to test the opponent's claim of infinite past.
2
The mapping procedure
We map the first 'now' of one series to the first of the other, and so on. This is a one-to-one correspondence by rational assumption.
3
Two possibilities
Either the series coincide perfectly (every now in one has a match in the other) or they diverge. Consider what each outcome would mean.
4
First outcome: coincidence
If they coincide, then the whole set from now to infinity equals the part from the Flood to infinity—an absurdity because the part (Flood onward) is shorter than the whole (now onward).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Second outcome: divergence
If they diverge, the flood-series must end (since it maps onto a subset of the now-series), forcing the now-series to be finite as well. Hence an infinite past is impossible.
The conclusion: a first event must exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الآن، وأخرى من يوم الطوفان كل منهما لا إلى نهاية ، ثم نطبق بينهما بحسب فرض العقل إجمالاً، بأن تقابل الأول من هذه بالأول من تلك وهكذا، فإما أن يتطابقا فيتساوى الكل والجزء أو لا فتنقطع الطوفانية ويلزم انتهاء الآنية (١) لأنها لا تزيد عليها إلا بقدر متناه .
وثانيهما: طريق التكافؤ ، وهو أنا نفرض سلسلة من الحادث المعين الذي هو مسبوق بحادث وليس سابقاً على حادث آخر بمنزلة المعلول الأخير، فلضرورة تضايف السابقية والمسبوقية وتكافؤ المتضايفين في الوجود لزم أن تشتمل السلسلة على سابق غير مسبوق، وهو المنتهى، وتقرير آخر أنا نفرض سلسلة من المسبوقية وأخرى من السابقية ثم نطبق بينهما فتقع مسبوقية الأخير بإزاء سابقية ما فوقه، فيلزم الانتهاء إلى ما له السابقية دون المسبوقية تحقيقاً للتضايف.
The 'now' and another from the day of the flood, each of them without end, then we compare them according to the assumption of reason in summary, by making the first of these correspond to the first of those and so on. Either they coincide, so the whole and the part are equal, or not, so the flood-series is interrupted and the finitude of the 'nows' (al-āniyya) is necessitated, because they do not exceed it except by a finite amount.
And the second: the method of equivalence (al-takāfu'), which is that we assume a series from a particular contingent (al-ḥādith al-muʿayyan) that is preceded by an event and is not prior to another event, like the last effect. Due to the necessity of the mutual relation (al-taḍāyuf) of priority and posteriority and the equivalence of the two correlatives in existence, it is necessary that the series contains a prior that is not preceded, and that is the ultimate term. Another formulation: we assume a series of posteriority and another of priority, then we compare them, so the posteriority of the last corresponds to the priority of what is above it, necessitating arriving at that which has priority without posteriority, as a realization of the mutual relation.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines two logical methods to prove the impossibility of an infinite regress of past events. The first method, called "the now" (al-ān), involves assuming two series: one of "nows" from the present moment backward, and another of "nows" from a past point (e.g., the day of the flood) backward, each infinite. By mapping them one-to-one, either they correspond exactly—implying the whole and part are equal, which is absurd—or they diverge, forcing the shorter series to end, thus proving the original series must also be finite. The second method, "equivalence" (al-takāfu'), starts from a particular contingent event that is preceded by another but not prior to any other (like the last effect). Due to the mutual relation (taḍāyuf) of priority and posteriority, the series must contain a first term that is prior without being posterior. This is demonstrated by assuming a series of posteriorities and a series of priorities and mapping them: the posteriority of the last aligns with the priority of the one above, leading necessarily to a term with priority only, the ultimate beginning. Both methods aim to show that an infinite regress of past events is impossible and a first term must exist.

Key Terms
الآن
الآنية
الطوفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'now' method arbitrarily assumes two infinite series to prove a first event, but it actually assumes the opponent's position to derive a contradiction.
Correction: The method is a reductio: it starts from the opponent's claim of infinite past and shows it leads to absurdity, thus proving the necessity of a first event.

Checkpoint
What is the absurdity if the two series of 'nows' coincide perfectly?
A) The whole series equals its part.
B) The flood-series is longer than the now-series.
C) The now-series becomes infinite.
D) The mapping fails.

Key Takeaway
The 'now' method uses a one-to-one mapping of two hypothetical infinite series to prove that an infinite past leads to absurdity or finitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the chain of moments that led to your existence. Taftazani shows that chain cannot go back forever—it must have a starting point. If that starting point is God, does your life today feel anchored to that origin, or do you live as if you are self-caused? What would change if you truly believed every moment depends on that first cause?
?
The proof forces you to choose: either infinity ends in absurdity or it stops. When you examine your own habits—your excuses, your procrastination—do you ever trace them back to a first decision? Where does your own chain of rationalizations end? Could stopping one chain of behavior today be a small reflection of that ultimate stopping point?
?
If the whole equals the part in an infinite series, then meaning collapses. Your individual actions would be just a drop in an endless ocean. But the proof shows infinity is impossible, meaning your actions are part of a finite, purposeful sequence. Does that realization press you to examine what you do with the limited moments you have?

The Equivalence Method: Priority and Posteriority
Taftazani presents the method of equivalence (al-takāfu') as a proof against infinite regress via correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the equivalence method uses the mutual relation of priority and posteriority to prove a first cause.

Core Idea
Correlative priority and posteriority force a term with only priority.

The second method is more subtle, focusing on the necessary relationship between priority and posteriority. If every event is preceded by another, then there must be a first event that is prior to all others without being posterior itself. This lesson explains that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting point
We start with a particular contingent event that is preceded (mashuq) but not prior to anything else—like the last effect in a chain.
This is a hypothetical last event.
2
The correlation of priority and posteriority
Priority and posteriority are correlatives (mutadāyifān); one cannot exist without the other. So if the series has posteriority, it must have a corresponding priority.
This is a key logical principle.
3
Conclusion from the correlation
The series of events with posteriority must contain a term that has priority without being posterior—the ultimate beginning (al-muntahā).
4
Alternative formulation: two series
Taftazani offers another version: assume a series of posteriorities and a series of priorities. Map the last posteriority to the priority above it, and this forces a term with only priority.
Reinforces the same conclusion.
5
Final result
Both formulations lead to the necessity of a first term that is prior but not posterior.
Thus an infinite regress is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الآن، وأخرى من يوم الطوفان كل منهما لا إلى نهاية ، ثم نطبق بينهما بحسب فرض العقل إجمالاً، بأن تقابل الأول من هذه بالأول من تلك وهكذا، فإما أن يتطابقا فيتساوى الكل والجزء أو لا فتنقطع الطوفانية ويلزم انتهاء الآنية (١) لأنها لا تزيد عليها إلا بقدر متناه .
وثانيهما: طريق التكافؤ ، وهو أنا نفرض سلسلة من الحادث المعين الذي هو مسبوق بحادث وليس سابقاً على حادث آخر بمنزلة المعلول الأخير، فلضرورة تضايف السابقية والمسبوقية وتكافؤ المتضايفين في الوجود لزم أن تشتمل السلسلة على سابق غير مسبوق، وهو المنتهى، وتقرير آخر أنا نفرض سلسلة من المسبوقية وأخرى من السابقية ثم نطبق بينهما فتقع مسبوقية الأخير بإزاء سابقية ما فوقه، فيلزم الانتهاء إلى ما له السابقية دون المسبوقية تحقيقاً للتضايف.
The 'now' and another from the day of the flood, each of them without end, then we compare them according to the assumption of reason in summary, by making the first of these correspond to the first of those and so on. Either they coincide, so the whole and the part are equal, or not, so the flood-series is interrupted and the finitude of the 'nows' (al-āniyya) is necessitated, because they do not exceed it except by a finite amount.
And the second: the method of equivalence (al-takāfu'), which is that we assume a series from a particular contingent (al-ḥādith al-muʿayyan) that is preceded by an event and is not prior to another event, like the last effect. Due to the necessity of the mutual relation (al-taḍāyuf) of priority and posteriority and the equivalence of the two correlatives in existence, it is necessary that the series contains a prior that is not preceded, and that is the ultimate term. Another formulation: we assume a series of posteriority and another of priority, then we compare them, so the posteriority of the last corresponds to the priority of what is above it, necessitating arriving at that which has priority without posteriority, as a realization of the mutual relation.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines two logical methods to prove the impossibility of an infinite regress of past events. The first method, called "the now" (al-ān), involves assuming two series: one of "nows" from the present moment backward, and another of "nows" from a past point (e.g., the day of the flood) backward, each infinite. By mapping them one-to-one, either they correspond exactly—implying the whole and part are equal, which is absurd—or they diverge, forcing the shorter series to end, thus proving the original series must also be finite. The second method, "equivalence" (al-takāfu'), starts from a particular contingent event that is preceded by another but not prior to any other (like the last effect). Due to the mutual relation (taḍāyuf) of priority and posteriority, the series must contain a first term that is prior without being posterior. This is demonstrated by assuming a series of posteriorities and a series of priorities and mapping them: the posteriority of the last aligns with the priority of the one above, leading necessarily to a term with priority only, the ultimate beginning. Both methods aim to show that an infinite regress of past events is impossible and a first term must exist.

Key Terms
طريق التكافؤ
التضايف
السابقية
المسبوقية
المنتهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The equivalence method requires that the series of events is actually infinite to work, but it actually proves that an infinite series is impossible.
Correction: The method assumes the opponent's position of an infinite regress and deduces a contradiction from the correlation of priority and posteriority, showing that an infinite series cannot satisfy the correlation.

Checkpoint
According to the equivalence method, what must the series of events contain?
A) An infinite number of terms.
B) A term that is both prior and posterior.
C) A term that is prior without being posterior.
D) A term that is posterior without being prior.

Key Takeaway
The equivalence method proves a first cause by showing that an infinite regress of events would violate the necessary correlation between priority and posteriority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The method shows that an infinite regress of dependencies is impossible. How does this truth press you to rely on Allah as the sole ultimate priority, rather than getting lost in secondary causes?
?
You live in a world that often treats the universe as self-sufficient. Does the logical necessity of a First Cause change how you view your own daily actions as being sustained by Him alone?
