Six Arguments Against Infinite Regress: An Overview and Critique
Taftazani enumerates six arguments against infinite regress and declares them all weak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six arguments theologians have used to prove the impossibility of an infinite past series, and why Taftazani considers them all weak.

Core Idea
Taftazani catalogs six weak arguments against infinite regress before offering stronger ones.

Theologians have advanced several arguments to show that a series of past events cannot stretch back infinitely without a beginning. Taftazani lists six such arguments in a compact passage. However, as we will see, he does not endorse them—he explicitly says they are all weak. This lesson walks through each argument and then explains why they fail, setting the stage for more robust proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice how Taftazani introduces the list with 'Wajh ukhar' (other aspects). Pay attention to the flow of the arguments.
The passage is dense; it's okay if not everything is clear at first.
2
Identify the six arguments
Each argument is introduced by 'anna' (that). List them: 1) occurrence of each necessitates whole, 2) increase/decrease implies finitude, 3) non-finiteness of past motions implies impossibility of completion, 4) each part is effect of free agent, 5) if motion preceded by another, not eternal; else has beginning, 6) each motion preceded by eternal non-existence, leading to contradiction.
3
Understand the reasoning of each argument
For each argument, ask: what is the logical structure? For example, the second argument: if something can be increased or decreased, it must be finite. Since the number of past events can be increased (adding more), it must be finite.
4
Note Taftazani's verdict
The passage ends with 'wa-l-kull ḍaʿīf' (and all is weak). Reflect on why each might be weak. The commentary gives hints.
This is crucial: Taftazani is not presenting these as his own proofs.
5
Reflect on the purpose of listing weak arguments
Ask yourself: why would Taftazani list weak arguments? Perhaps to show that not all arguments are sound, and to prepare for stronger ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة امتناع تعاقب الحوادث لا إلى بداية ونهاية
قال (وقد يذكر )
وجوه أخر مثل أن حدوث كل يستلزم حدوث الكل، وأن قبول الزيادة والنقصان يستلزم التناهي، وأن عدم تناهي الحركات الماضية يستلزم امتناع انقضائها، وأن كل جزء من الحركة لزواله يجب أن يكون أثر المختار دون الموجب فيكون حادثة، وأن الحركة إن كانت مسبوقة بأخرى امتنع أزليتها، وإلا تحقق أوليتها، وأن كلا من جزئيات الحركة مسبوق بعدم أزلي فيتقارن عدماها في الأزل ضرورة أن تأخر البعض ينافي أزليته، فلو وجد حركة في الأزل لزم اجتماع النقيضين والكل ضعيف .
Proofs of the impossibility of the succession of events without a beginning and an end.
He said: 'And other aspects are mentioned,' such as: that the occurrence of each necessitates the occurrence of the whole; that accepting increase and decrease necessitates finitude; that the non-finiteness of past motions necessitates the impossibility of their completion; that each part of motion, due to its passing, must be the effect of the agent of choice (al-mukhtār) not the necessitating agent (al-mūjib), thus it is contingent (ḥāditha); that if motion is preceded by another, its eternity is impossible, otherwise its beginning is realized; and that each particular motion is preceded by an eternal non-existence, so their non-existences coincide in pre-eternity (al-azal) necessarily, because the delay of some contradicts its eternity, so if a motion existed in pre-eternity, the conjunction of two opposites would be necessary. And all is weak.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage lists six arguments (wujūh) that have been advanced to prove that a series of events without beginning or end is impossible. The first argument: if every individual event is contingent, the whole series must be contingent as well. The second: the series of past events can be increased or decreased (e.g., more days than months), and anything that admits increase or decrease is finite. The third: if past motions were infinite, they could never be completed, but we observe that they have been completed (the present day depends on the completion of all earlier days). The fourth: each part of motion passes away, so it must be the effect of a free agent (al-mukhtār) not a necessitating cause, hence it is a contingent event; this proves contingency of each part, implying the whole series is contingent. The fifth: if a particular motion is preceded by another, it cannot be eternal; if it is not preceded, it has a first moment, proving it is not eternal. The sixth: each motion is preceded by an eternal non-existence, so all non-existences are concurrent in pre-eternity; if one motion were later, that would contradict its being in pre-eternity, so assuming an eternal motion leads to contradiction. After listing these, Taftazani remarks that all of them are weak (wa-l-kull ḍaʿīf). This sets the stage for discussing stronger arguments.

Key Terms
الزيادة والنقصان
التناهي
المختار
الموجب
الأزل
حادثة
النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Taftazani endorses these six arguments as proofs for the impossibility of infinite regress.
Correction: Taftazani explicitly states at the end that all these arguments are weak (ḍaʿīf). He is reporting them for critical examination, not advocating them.

Checkpoint
What is Taftazani's assessment of the six arguments listed in this passage?
A) They are all strong and convincing.
B) They are all weak.
C) Some are strong, some are weak.
D) He does not give an assessment.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani catalogues six arguments against infinite regress but dismisses all as weak, setting the stage for stronger proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a core belief about Allah with an argument that you later realized was weak? How does Taftazani's willingness to call even widely used arguments 'ḍaʿīf' challenge you to critically examine the foundations of your own faith?
?
Taftazani dismisses all six arguments without a second thought. Is there an area of your life—your practice, your understanding, your certainty—where you are relying on a weak foundation? Will you commit to seeking stronger, more rigorous proofs?
?
If the strongest arguments for the Creator require intellectual effort, are you willing to invest that effort rather than settling for comfortable but flawed reasoning? What might you need to change in your study habits or priorities to dig deeper into true knowledge?

The First Argument: From Contingency of Parts to Whole
Taftazani presents the first additional argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against an infinite past that attempts to transfer the attribute of contingency from each event to the entire series, and its refutation by denying the universality of this transfer.

Core Idea
The fallacy of composition: what applies to each part does not automatically apply to the whole.

In his discussion of the impossibility of an infinite past series, Taftazani presents three additional arguments. The first argument attempts to show that if every individual event is contingent (preceded by non-existence), then the entire series must also be contingent, implying a beginning. Taftazani's refutation warns against the logical error of transferring a property from each member of a collection to the collection itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Identify the two parts: the argument and the refutation.
Notice the structure: 'منها: أنه ...' then 'وردّ ...'
2
Understand the argument
The argument claims: every event is preceded by non-existence, so the entire series is also preceded by non-existence. This would mean the series has a beginning.
The argument uses the logical principle: if every A is B, then all A's together are B.
3
Examine the refutation
Taftazani denies the universality of that principle (الرد بالمنع). He gives an example: every Zanjī is an individual, but the whole group is not an individual.
This shows the fallacy of composition.
4
Reflect on the logical lesson
Consider why properties of parts do not always transfer to the whole. This is a key distinction in logic and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لبيان امتناع تعاقب الحوادث لا إلى بداية ونهاية وجوه أخرى منها : أنه لما كان كل حادث مسبوقاً بالعدم، كان الكل كذلك، فإنه إذا كان كل زنجي أسود، كان الكل أسود ضرورة وردّ بمنع كلية هذا الحكم، ألا ترى أن كل زنجي فرد، وبعض من المجموع بخلاف الكل. ومنها أن الحوادث الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان للقطع بأن دورات الفلك من الآن إلى ما لا يتناهى أكثر من دوراتها من الطوفان ودورات الشمس أكثر من دورات زحل (۱) ، وعدد الأيام أكثر من عدد الشهور ،والسنين وكل ما يقبل الزيادة والنقصان فهو متناه، لأن الشيء من الشيء أن يكون بحيث لا يبقى شيء في مقابلة ما بقي من الزائد فيتناهى الناقص، ويلزم منه تناهي الزائد حيث لم يزد عليه إلا بقدر متناه. ورد بعد تسليم المقدمة الأولى بمنع الثانية، وإنما يصح لو لم تكن الزيادة والنقصان من الجانب المتناهى ولا معنى للزيادة والنقصان ههنا، إلا أن يحصل في إحدى الجملتين شيء لم يحصل في الأخرى، وهو لا يجب الانقطاع كما في مراتب الأعداد ومنها أنه لو كانت الحركات الماضية غير متناه لامتنع انقضاؤها، لأن ما لا يتناهى لا ينقضى ضرورة واللازم ،باطل لأن حصول اليوم الذي نحن فيه موقوف على انقضاء ما قبله . وردّ بالمنع فإن غير المتناهي إنما يستحيل انقضاؤه من
To explain the impossibility of the succession of events without a beginning and an end, there are other aspects. Among them: that since every contingent is preceded by non-existence, the whole is likewise. For if every Zanjī (black) is a slave, then the whole is a slave by necessity. It is refuted by denying the universality of this judgment: do you not see that every Zanjī is an individual, and some of the totality is different from the whole? Among them: that past events are subject to increase and decrease, since it is certain that the rotations of the celestial sphere from now to infinity are more than its rotations from the flood, and the rotations of the sun are more than the rotations of Saturn, and the number of days is more than the number of months and years. And everything that admits increase and decrease is finite, because a thing is such that nothing remains in comparison to what remains of the increased, so the decreased is finite, and the finitude of the increased follows from it, since it does not exceed it except by a finite amount. It is refuted after granting the first premise by denying the second: it would be valid only if the increase and decrease were not from the finite side; and there is no meaning of increase and decrease here except that something occurs in one set that does not occur in the other, and this does not necessitate interruption, as in the ranks of numbers. Among them: that if past motions were infinite, their completion would be impossible, because what is infinite does not finish necessarily, but the consequent is false because the occurrence of the day we are in is dependent on the completion of what preceded it. It is refuted by denying: the infinite is impossible to complete only from
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents three additional arguments against an infinite past series, each followed by a refutation (radd). The first argument: since every contingent event is preceded by non-existence, the entire series must also be preceded by non-existence, i.e., the whole is contingent. The refutation denies the general rule: just because every Zanjī (a black person) is an individual does not mean the whole set is an individual; similarly, the property 'preceded by non-existence' does not transfer from parts to the whole. The second argument: past events are subject to increase and decrease (e.g., more solar rotations than Saturn rotations), and anything that admits increase and decrease is finite. The refutation grants the first premise (that there is increase and decrease) but denies the second (that this implies finitude). It argues that increase and decrease here merely mean one set has elements not in the other, which does not force finitude, just as in the infinite series of numbers. The third argument: if past motions were infinite, they could never be completed, but the present day depends on the completion of all past days. The refutation denies that the infinite cannot be completed from one side; an infinite series in the past can be traversed if we start from a point and go backward, but the argument is cut off. Taftazani shows that these objections are not decisive, and the debate requires careful logical analysis.

Key Terms
الرد بالمنع
الكل
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If every part of a series is contingent, the whole series must be contingent and thus have a beginning.
Correction: The property of being contingent may not transfer to the whole; the whole can be considered differently. Taftazani's example shows the fallacy.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani refute the first argument?
A) By denying that every event is contingent.
B) By denying the universality of the judgment that what applies to each part applies to the whole.
C) By arguing that the whole is not a real entity.
D) By accepting the argument but saying it only proves finitude, not a beginning.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against an infinite past is refuted by showing that the attribute of contingency does not necessarily transfer from each event to the whole series.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own reasoning, when have you assumed that because something is true for every part, it must be true for the whole? How might this fallacy affect your understanding of Allah's attributes or His creation? What will you do to check such reasoning in the future?
?
The refutation shows that the whole series of events does not need a beginning even if each event is contingent. Does this challenge your certainty that the universe must have a beginning? If not, why? If so, how will you seek a stronger foundation for your belief?
?
If the argument from contingency of parts fails, are you tempted to doubt the necessity of a Creator? Reflect: is your faith based solely on arguments, or on something deeper? What step will you take today to strengthen that foundation?

The Second Argument: Increase and Decrease Imply Finitude
Taftazani presents the second argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that past events being subject to increase and decrease requires finitude, and its refutation that such increase does not entail finitude, as shown by infinite number series.

Core Idea
Increase and decrease can occur within infinite sets without contradiction.

The second argument against an infinite past appeals to the intuitive notion that if something can be increased or decreased, it must be limited. Taftazani provides counterexamples from astronomy and calendars to show that past events can be compared in size. The refutation cleverly uses the analogy of infinite number series to show that one infinite set can be larger than another without either being finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage. Note the structure: the argument is presented with examples, then the refutation.
The argument begins with 'ومنها أن الحوادث الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان'
2
Understand the argument
The argument gives examples: more solar rotations from now to infinity than from the flood, etc. It claims that anything that admits increase and decrease is finite because the decreased would be limited, implying the increased must also be finite.
The reasoning: if A is greater than B, and B is finite, then A is also finite.
3
Examine the refutation
Taftazani grants the first premise (there is increase and decrease) but denies the second (that this implies finitude). He notes that increase/decrease here merely means one set contains elements not in the other, which does not force finitude, just as in number series.
The refutation uses 'كما في مراتب الأعداد' (as in the ranks of numbers).
4
Reflect on the mathematical analogy
Consider how infinite sets can be compared: the set of natural numbers is larger than the set of even numbers, yet both are infinite. Similarly, past events can be compared without being finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لبيان امتناع تعاقب الحوادث لا إلى بداية ونهاية وجوه أخرى منها : أنه لما كان كل حادث مسبوقاً بالعدم، كان الكل كذلك، فإنه إذا كان كل زنجي أسود، كان الكل أسود ضرورة وردّ بمنع كلية هذا الحكم، ألا ترى أن كل زنجي فرد، وبعض من المجموع بخلاف الكل. ومنها أن الحوادث الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان للقطع بأن دورات الفلك من الآن إلى ما لا يتناهى أكثر من دوراتها من الطوفان ودورات الشمس أكثر من دورات زحل (۱) ، وعدد الأيام أكثر من عدد الشهور ،والسنين وكل ما يقبل الزيادة والنقصان فهو متناه، لأن الشيء من الشيء أن يكون بحيث لا يبقى شيء في مقابلة ما بقي من الزائد فيتناهى الناقص، ويلزم منه تناهي الزائد حيث لم يزد عليه إلا بقدر متناه. ورد بعد تسليم المقدمة الأولى بمنع الثانية، وإنما يصح لو لم تكن الزيادة والنقصان من الجانب المتناهى ولا معنى للزيادة والنقصان ههنا، إلا أن يحصل في إحدى الجملتين شيء لم يحصل في الأخرى، وهو لا يجب الانقطاع كما في مراتب الأعداد ومنها أنه لو كانت الحركات الماضية غير متناه لامتنع انقضاؤها، لأن ما لا يتناهى لا ينقضى ضرورة واللازم ،باطل لأن حصول اليوم الذي نحن فيه موقوف على انقضاء ما قبله . وردّ بالمنع فإن غير المتناهي إنما يستحيل انقضاؤه من
To explain the impossibility of the succession of events without a beginning and an end, there are other aspects. Among them: that since every contingent is preceded by non-existence, the whole is likewise. For if every Zanjī (black) is a slave, then the whole is a slave by necessity. It is refuted by denying the universality of this judgment: do you not see that every Zanjī is an individual, and some of the totality is different from the whole? Among them: that past events are subject to increase and decrease, since it is certain that the rotations of the celestial sphere from now to infinity are more than its rotations from the flood, and the rotations of the sun are more than the rotations of Saturn, and the number of days is more than the number of months and years. And everything that admits increase and decrease is finite, because a thing is such that nothing remains in comparison to what remains of the increased, so the decreased is finite, and the finitude of the increased follows from it, since it does not exceed it except by a finite amount. It is refuted after granting the first premise by denying the second: it would be valid only if the increase and decrease were not from the finite side; and there is no meaning of increase and decrease here except that something occurs in one set that does not occur in the other, and this does not necessitate interruption, as in the ranks of numbers. Among them: that if past motions were infinite, their completion would be impossible, because what is infinite does not finish necessarily, but the consequent is false because the occurrence of the day we are in is dependent on the completion of what preceded it. It is refuted by denying: the infinite is impossible to complete only from
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents three additional arguments against an infinite past series, each followed by a refutation (radd). The first argument: since every contingent event is preceded by non-existence, the entire series must also be preceded by non-existence, i.e., the whole is contingent. The refutation denies the general rule: just because every Zanjī (a black person) is an individual does not mean the whole set is an individual; similarly, the property 'preceded by non-existence' does not transfer from parts to the whole. The second argument: past events are subject to increase and decrease (e.g., more solar rotations than Saturn rotations), and anything that admits increase and decrease is finite. The refutation grants the first premise (that there is increase and decrease) but denies the second (that this implies finitude). It argues that increase and decrease here merely mean one set has elements not in the other, which does not force finitude, just as in the infinite series of numbers. The third argument: if past motions were infinite, they could never be completed, but the present day depends on the completion of all past days. The refutation denies that the infinite cannot be completed from one side; an infinite series in the past can be traversed if we start from a point and go backward, but the argument is cut off. Taftazani shows that these objections are not decisive, and the debate requires careful logical analysis.

الزيادة والنقصان
التناهي
مراتب الأعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one set of past events is larger than another, the smaller must be finite, and thus the larger also finite.
Correction: Infinite sets can have different sizes; a larger infinite set does not imply finitude. The number series shows this.

Checkpoint

A) He denies that past events can be compared in size.
B) He accepts the increase but denies that it implies finitude, using the analogy of numbers.
C) He argues that increase and decrease are illusions.
D) He says the argument only works if the increase is from the finite side.

Key Takeaway
The second argument is refuted by showing that increase and decrease can exist between infinite sets without requiring finitude.

The Third Argument: Completion of the Infinite
Taftazani presents the third argument and begins its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that an infinite past cannot be completed, and the refutation that the infinite can be traversed from one direction.

Core Idea
An infinite past can be traversed backward even if not forward.

The third argument focuses on the intuition that one cannot reach the end of an infinite series. If past events were infinite, we could never have arrived at the present. Taftazani's refutation begins by denying the impossibility of completing an infinite series from the past direction, hinting at the distinction between infinite in the past (already traversed) and infinite in the future. The passage here is unfinished in the source, but the gist is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the third argument. Note that it is cut off mid-sentence.
The passage ends with 'وردّ بالمنع فإن غير المتناهي إنما يستحيل انقضاؤه من'.
2
Understand the argument
The argument states: if past motions were infinite, they could never be completed (ما لا يتناهى لا ينقضى). But the present day depends on completing all past days, so the past must be finite.
This is a classic argument against an infinite temporal series.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation begins with 'وردّ بالمنع' (refuted by denial). It asserts that the impossibility of completion applies only to completion from one side, not from the other. The full refutation likely continues on the next page.
The key is that an infinite series in the past has already been traversed to reach the present.
4
Reflect on the directionality of infinity
Consider the difference between an infinite regress (past) and an infinite progress (future). An infinite past does not require a 'traversal' from the beginning; it simply has no beginning.

Proof from Increase/Decrease and Its Refutation
Author argues past events admit increase/decrease therefore finite, and gives refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from increase/decrease seeks to prove the finitude of past events and why the refutation shows that comparative increase does not necessarily imply finitude.

Core Idea
Comparative increase does not force finitude—infinite sets can have different sizes.

This passage presents a second kalam proof that past events must be finite based on the idea that they can be increased or decreased. But the refutation challenges the move from comparative increase to actual finitude, using the analogy of infinite number series. Let's read the argument carefully and see how the objection dismantles it.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument and refutation.
2
Identify the two premises of the proof
Find where the author states that past events admit increase/decrease (first premise) and that everything admitting increase/decrease is finite (second premise).
The examples of cycles of spheres, days vs months help illustrate the first premise.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation grants the first premise but denies the second. Notice how it argues that increase/decrease here only means one set has something the other lacks, which does not necessitate an end.
The number series example is key: the set of all numbers is infinite, but it is larger than the set of even numbers, yet both are infinite.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Does this refutation prove that past events are infinite? No, it only shows that the proof is invalid. The question of finitude remains open from this argument.

Source Text

It argues that increase and decrease here only mean that one set has something the other lacks, which does not require an end, like the infinite series of numbers (1,2,3,...) where some parts are larger than others but both are infinite.

Proof from Motion Precedence and Its Refutation
Author presents proof and its refutation regarding motion's beginning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that every motion must be preceded by another does not prove that the entire sequence of motions has a first beginning.

Core Idea
The kalam argument fails if it confuses the origin of each part with the origin of the whole.

This passage presents a classic kalam argument used to prove that the world has a beginning by examining motion. The author then provides a crucial refutation that points out a logical gap: while each individual motion may be temporally originated, this does not entail that the entire series had a beginning. Learning to distinguish between the properties of parts and the whole is essential in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two options given for any assumed motion: either it is preceded by another motion (so it is not eternal) or it is not preceded (so it is the first motion). The proof concludes that motion has a beginning.
The proof tries to force a choice between two exclusive possibilities.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation accepts the first option: that every motion is preceded. But it claims this only proves that each individual motion is temporally originated, not that the whole series must have a beginning.
The refutation distinguishes between the parts (each event) and the whole (the infinite series).
3
Identify the logical distinction
Realise the key point: proving that each part of a series is originated does not automatically prove that the series as a whole has an absolute beginning. An infinite series of originated events is not logically impossible.
This distinction is crucial for evaluating cosmological arguments.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this refutation matters: it weakens a common argument for the temporal creation of the world. It shows that one cannot simply move from 'every event is originated' to 'the world must have a first event'.
The debate between eternal creation and temporal creation hinges on such logical nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن كل حركة تفرض لا تخلو من أن تكون مسبوقة بحركة أخرى، فلا تكون أزلية ضرورة سبق العدم عليها ، أو لا تكون مسبوقة بأخرى بل يتحقق حركة لا حركة قبلها، فتكون للحركة بداية وهو المطلوب . ورد بأنا نختار الأول ولا يفيد إلا حدوث كل من جزئيات الحركة، ولا نزاع فيه، وإنما النزاع في أن ينتهي إلى حادث لا يكون قبله حادث آخر،
And among them: that every motion that is assumed can only be preceded by another motion, so it is not eternal, necessarily because non-existence precedes it; or it is not preceded by another, but rather a motion exists with no motion before it, so motion has a beginning, and that is what is sought. It is refuted by saying: we choose the first [possibility], and it only yields the temporal origination of each individual motion, and there is no dispute about that. Rather the dispute is whether it ends at an event after which there is no other event.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage gives another proof: every motion is either preceded by another motion (so it is not eternal) or it is the first motion (so motion has a beginning). The refutation accepts that each individual motion is preceded (temporally originated) but says this does not prove that the entire series must have a beginning. The dispute is about whether there is a first event, not whether each event is originated.

جزئيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If every event in a series is caused or originated, then the entire series must have a beginning.
Correction: The refutation shows that each event being preceded does not entail that the series has a first event. An infinite regress of contingent events is logically possible.

Checkpoint

A) That motion is eternal.

C) That the series of motions must have a beginning.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that an infinite regress of temporally originated events is logically possible, so the argument from motion does not prove an absolute beginning.

Proof from Coexistence of Non-Existences and Its Objection
Author argues infinite past events imply coexistence of non-existences in eternity; objection redefines eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that an infinite regress leads to a contradiction through the gathering of non-existences in eternity, and the objection that eternity is not a container.

Core Idea
Eternity is not a container—'eternal non-existence' just means not preceded by existence.

This passage presents a proof that if events had no beginning, their prior non-existences would all have to coexist in eternity, leading to impossibility. But an objection challenges the very notion of eternity as a 'container' for non-existences. Let's examine both the proof and the objection carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author builds the argument: succession without beginning → each event preceded by eternal non-existence → all non-existences gather in eternity → then if any existence occurs in eternity, contradictions arise.
The proof aims to show that an infinite regress is impossible.
2
Understand the objection
The objection redefines 'eternity'. It says eternity is not a state like a container. Instead, 'eternal non-existence' merely means the non-existence is not preceded by existence.
This objection undermines the idea of 'gathering' of non-existences.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If eternity is not a container, does the proof still hold? The passage cuts off before the full answer, but you can see the direction of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And among them: if we suppose the succession of events without a beginning, then each of them is preceded by eternal non-existence because that is the meaning of temporal origination. And it entails the gathering of those non-existences in eternity, because if any of them were delayed from eternity, it would not be eternal. And if the non-existences gather in eternity, then if any of the existences occurs in eternity, it necessitates the concurrence of the prior and the posterior, or rather the conjunction of contradictories, which is impossible; and if it does not occur, that is what is sought. And it is objected that eternity is not a particular state like a container in which the non-existences of events gather such that if something of their existences occurred in it, it would necessitate the conjunction of contradictories. Rather, the meaning of the eternity of non-existences is that they are not preceded by existences, and this does not necessitate
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that an infinite regress would force all non-existences of events to coexist in eternity, leading to contradiction if any existence occurred in eternity. The objection redefines 'eternal non-existence' as simply not being preceded by existence, not as occupying a temporal container. Therefore, there is no literal gathering. The passage cuts off before the objection is fully explained.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Eternity is not a container; 'eternal non-existence' simply means the non-existence is not preceded by existence.

C) It means the non-existence is located in a container.
D) It means non-existence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The objection to the proof from coexistence of non-existences hinges on redefining eternity: it is not a container but a description of something not being preceded by existence.

The Sphericity of the Determiner and the Flaw in the Simplicity Argument
Al-Taftazani presents and critiques the argument for the determiner's sphericity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that the determiner must be spherical and why the argument from simplicity is considered unsound.

Core Idea
The argument for sphericity from simplicity is flawed; direction must be prior to parts, not the whole.

In this passage, al-Taftazani delves into the physical nature of the determiner that bounds the universe. He first resolves which of two bodies qualifies as the determiner, then turns to its necessary shape—sphericity—and subjects the supporting argument to critical scrutiny. His analysis reveals a subtle but decisive flaw.

Guided Path
1
Follow the opening dilemma
Read how al-Taftazani sets up the scenario of two bodies and why he chooses the surrounding body as the determiner.
Note the distinction between 'enclosing' and 'not enclosing'.
2
Examine the sphericity argument
Understand why the determiner must be spherical based on its simplicity and the impossibility of rectilinear motion for its parts.
The argument ties simplicity to roundness.
3
Identify the critique
Notice the author's objection: the argument only works for the parts, not the composite whole, rendering it unsound.
This is the key flaw al-Taftazani highlights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو بأن يكون جسمين مثلاً. فإما أن يحيط أحدهما بالآخر أو لا. فإن أحاط كان المحدد، إذ إليه الانتهاء دون المحاط، وإن لم يحط كان كل منهما في جهة من الآخر، فيكون متأخراً عن الجهة أو مقارناً لها سابقاً عليها، ليصلح محدداً لها، وأيضاً كل منهما . إنما يحدد جهة القرب منه دون البعد فإنه غير متحدد. والمطلوب إثبات ما يحدد الجهتين المتقابلتين معاً وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون الجسماني بحيث يكون غاية القرب من كل منهما غاية البعد من الآخر، فيتحدد بهما الجهتان، فلذا كان المختار هو الوجه الأول. وأما وجوب كونه كرياً فلأنه بسيط يمتنع زواله عن مقتضى طبعه أعني الاستدارة، إذ لو كان مركباً أو بسيطاً زال عن استدارته لزم جواز الحركة المستقيمة على أجزائه وهو محال ضرورة أنها لا تكون إلا من جهة إلى جهة فتكون الجهة قبله أو معه فلا تكون متحددة به، وجه اللزوم، أما في البسيط الزائل عن الاستدارة فظاهر، وأما في المركب فلأن تألفه لا يتصور إلا بحركة بعض الأجزاء إلى البعض، ولأن من لوازمه جواز الانحلال، لأن كل واحد من بسائطه يلاقي بأحد طرفيه شيئاً غير ما يلاقيه بالطرف الآخر مع تساويهما في الحقيقة، فيجوز أن يلاقي ذلك الشيء بالطرف الآخر، وذلك بالحركة من جهة إلى جهة، وفي هذا نظر لأنه إنما يستدعي تقدم الجهة على حركة الأجزاء لا على نفس المركب، وبهذا يظهر أن الاستدلال بهذا الوجه على بساطة المحدد ليس بنام .
This is by being two bodies, for example. Either one surrounds the other or not. If it surrounds, then it is the determiner, because the termination belongs to it and not to the surrounded. If it does not surround, then each of them is in a direction from the other, so it is posterior to direction or concomitant with it or prior to it, in order to be suitable as a determiner for it. Also each of them only determines the direction of proximity to it, not distance, for it is undetermined. The goal is to prove what determines both opposite directions together. And there is a consideration: it is possible that the corporeal being is such that the extreme proximity to each of them is the extreme distance from the other, so both directions are determined by them. Therefore the chosen view is the first one. As for the necessity of it being spherical, it is because it is simple, it is impossible for it to deviate from what its nature necessitates, namely roundness. For if it were composite or simple having deviated from its roundness, then rectilinear motion would be possible for its parts, which is impossible, because such motion can only be from one direction to another, so direction would be prior to it or with it, and thus it would not be determined by it. The implication: as for the simple that has deviated from roundness, it is obvious; as for the composite, because its composition cannot be conceived except by the movement of some parts towards others, and because one of its concomitants is the possibility of dissolution, since each of its simple components meets at one of its ends something different from what it meets at the other end, while they are equal in reality, so it is possible for it to meet that thing at the other end, and that by motion from one direction to another. And there is a consideration: because it only requires priority of direction over the movement of the parts, not over the composite itself. And by this it becomes apparent that the argument by this aspect for the simplicity of the determiner is not sound.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques a proof for the necessity of the determiner (al-muḥaddid) being spherical. It opens with a hypothetical scenario involving two bodies: either one surrounds the other, making the surrounding one the determiner, or they do not surround each other, in which case each is in a direction relative to the other. The argument aims to establish something that determines opposite directions simultaneously. The author notes a consideration: it is possible for a corporeal being to be such that proximity to each of two points is equivalent to distance from the other, thereby determining both directions. Hence the first view (that one body surrounds the other) is chosen. Then the author moves to the necessity of the determiner being spherical (kurī). The reason given is that the determiner is simple (basīṭ), and it cannot deviate from its natural property of roundness (istidāra). If it were composite or simple but deviated from roundness, rectilinear motion would be possible for its parts, which is impossible because such motion requires direction prior to or concomitant with it, so the determiner would not be determined by it. The implication is explained for both the simple deviated from roundness and the composite. For the composite, composition requires movement of parts, and dissolution is possible, leading to motion from one direction to another. However, the author finds a flaw: this reasoning only requires priority of direction over the movement of parts, not over the composite itself. Therefore, the argument for the simplicity (and thus sphericity) of the determiner is not sound (laysa bi-tāmm).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument that the determiner must be spherical because it is simple is valid and conclusive.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that the argument only establishes priority of direction over the movement of parts, not over the composite itself, so it fails to prove simplicity directly.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The argument that the determiner must be spherical because it is simple fails, as it only requires direction prior to the movement of parts, not the composite whole.

Refutation of Two Objections to the Determiner's Sphericity
Al-Taftazani refutes objections that non-spherical shapes cannot determine distance and that movement causes void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes two key objections against the sphericity of the celestial determiner.

Core Idea
Non-spherical shapes can determine distance, and their motion need not cause void.

In this passage, al-Taftazani defends the sphericity of the celestial determiner (al-muḥaddad) against two rational objections. The first objection claims that only a spherical shape can determine both proximity and distance; the second argues that a non-spherical body moving on a spherical surface would inevitably create void. By presenting counterexamples, al-Taftazani shows that these objections are not decisive.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first objection and its refutation
Read the part where the objection states that a non-spherical shape can only determine proximity, not distance. Notice how al-Taftazani refutes it by pointing to oval, lenticular, and even polygonal shapes, each of which has a center that determines all directions.
The key point: these shapes do have a center, but the distances to the sides are unequal.
2
Understand the second objection and its refutation
Now read the objection that motion of a non-spherical body on a spherical one would create void due to unfilled corners. Examine the refutation: if the concave part is spherical and the convex part is oval or lenticular moving on a specific axis, void does not occur.
Consider how the geometry avoids gaps.
3
See the overall argument for sphericity
Finally, step back and see that these objections were raised against the sphericity argument. Al-Taftazani's refutations strengthen the case for the determiner being spherical while clarifying that alternative shapes are not impossible.
The determiner's sphericity is not the only logical possibility, but it is the best explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رد وجهين من الاستدلال على كرية المحدد
قال (لا لما قبل)
إن غير الكري إنما يحدد القرب دون البعد فإنه ممنوع أو أن حركته سيما على الجسم المستدير يستلزم وقوع الخلاء، فإنه لو فرض مقعر المحدد مستديراً، ومحدبه بيضياً يتحرك على قطره الأطول أو عدسياً يتحرك على قطره الأقصر، لم يلزم الخلاء، وأما الإحاطة، فلأن غير المحيط لا يحدد سوى القرب، وهو ظاهر.
إشارة إلى رد وجهين آخرين استدل بهما على كرية المحدد، أحدهما أنه لولم يكن كرياً لم يتحدد به إلا جهة القرب، لأن البعد غير محدد، أو ردّ بالمنع ، فإن الشكل البيضي أو العدسي، بل المضلع أيضاً يشتمل على وسط هو غاية البعد عن جميع الجوانب، بحيث إذا تجاوزته صرت في القرب من جانب السنة غاية الأمر أن الأبعاد الممتدة منه إلى الجوانب لا تكون متساوية . وثانيهما: أنه لو لم يكن كرياً لزم من حركته خصوصاً على الجسم المستدير وقوع الخلاء إذ لا ماليء لفرج الزوايا. ورد بأنه لو فرض مقعره مستديراً، ومحدبه بيضياً ، ويتحرك على قطره الأطول أو عدسياً، ويتحرك على قطره الأقصر لم يلزم الخلاء
Refutation of two aspects of the argument for the sphericity of the determiner.
He said (no before?): 'That the non-spherical only determines proximity, not distance' is denied, or 'that its motion, especially on the spherical body, necessitates the occurrence of void, for if it is assumed that the concave of the determiner is spherical and its convex is oval moving on its longer diameter, or lenticular moving on its shorter diameter, void would not occur.' As for surrounding, because that which does not surround determines only proximity, and that is obvious.
Reference to the refutation of two other aspects argued for the sphericity of the determiner: First, that if it were not spherical, only the direction of proximity would be determined by it, because distance is not determinate; or it is refuted by denial, for the oval or lenticular shape, even the polygonal, contains a center which is the extreme distance from all sides, such that when one passes it, one becomes in proximity to the side; except that the distances extending from it to the sides are not equal. Second, that if it were not spherical, its motion especially on the spherical body would necessitate the occurrence of void, since there is no filler for the gaps of the corners. It is refuted by that if its concave is assumed spherical and its convex oval moving on its longer diameter, or lenticular moving on its shorter diameter, void would not occur.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage provides a refutation of two objections to the determiner's sphericity. First, the author quotes an objection: a non-spherical shape can only determine proximity, not distance. This is denied (mamnū‘). Also, the motion of a non-spherical body on a spherical one is claimed to necessitate void (khalā’). The author counters by positing a scenario: if the concave part of the determiner is spherical and its convex part is oval (bayḍī) moving along its longer diameter, or lenticular (‘adasī) moving along its shorter diameter, void does not occur. The point about surrounding (iḥāṭa) is that anything that does not surround determines only proximity, which is obvious. Then the author summarizes the refutation of two other arguments used to prove sphericity. The first argument: if the determiner were not spherical, it would only determine the direction of proximity, not distance, because distance is not determinate. The refutation: this is denied because even oval, lenticular, or polygonal shapes have a center that is the extreme distance from all sides; when one passes it, one becomes in proximity to a side, though distances are unequal. The second argument: if not spherical, its motion (especially on a spherical body) would necessitate void since there is no filler for the gaps of corners. The refutation: if the concave is spherical and the convex is oval moving on its longer diameter, or lenticular moving on its shorter diameter, void would not occur.

Refuting the Necessity of Sphericity for Direction
Refutes the claim that non-spherical muhaddid only determines nearness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument for sphericity is refuted by showing that non-spherical shapes still have a farthest point, thus determining both near and far directions.

Core Idea
Sphericity is not required; any shape with a center determines directions.

This passage directly addresses a key objection to the non-spherical model of the encompassing sphere (muhaddid). The argument claimed that only a sphere can determine both near and far directions. The author refutes this by pointing out that even ovals, lenticular shapes, and polygons have a central point that is the farthest from all sides, allowing direction determination without equality of distances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a claim followed by its refutation.
The text is dense; take your time to parse the logic.
2
Understand the original claim
The argument states that if the muhaddid is not spherical, it can only determine the direction of nearness, not the far side. Why would that be?
The claim relies on the idea that without sphericity, the far side is indeterminate.
3
Identify the refutation
The author denies (man‘) this premise, giving examples of non-spherical shapes that still have a center: oval, lenticular, and polygonal. Each has a point that is farthest from all sides.
Passing that farthest point leads to nearness on the opposite side.
4
Consider the implication
Thus, even without equal distances, the far side is determined. The refutation shows that the original claim is false.
5
Connect to the broader debate
This refutation supports the possibility that the muhaddid could be non-spherical, a key point in the larger discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إشارة إلى رد وجهين آخرين استدل بهما على كرية المحدد، أحدهما أنه لولم يكن كرياً لم يتحدد به إلا جهة القرب، لأن البعد غير محدد، أو ردّ بالمنع ، فإن الشكل البيضي أو العدسي، بل المضلع أيضاً يشتمل على وسط هو غاية البعد عن جميع الجوانب، بحيث إذا تجاوزته صرت في القرب من جانب الستة) غاية الأمر أن الأبعاد الممتدة منه إلى الجوانب لا تكون متساوية .
Reference to the refutation of two other arguments used to argue for the sphericity of the muhaddid: one of them is that if it were not spherical, it would only determine the direction of nearness, because the far side is not determined. It is refuted by denial, for the oval or lenticular shape, even the polygonal, includes a center that is the utmost distance from all sides, such that when you pass it you come into nearness from the side of [?]. The point is that the distances extending from it to the sides are not equal.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage refutes the first argument for the sphericity of the encompassing sphere (muhaddid). The argument claimed that if the muhaddid were not spherical, it could only determine the direction of nearness, not the far side. The refutation denies this premise, explaining that even a non-spherical shape like an oval (bayḍī), lenticular (ʿadasī), or polygon has a central point that is the farthest from all sides. Once that point is passed, one begins to approach the opposite side. The only difference is that distances from the center to the sides are not equal, but the far side is still determined.

Refuting the Second Argument for Sphericity
Refutes the second argument for sphericity by counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for sphericity and how it is refuted by considering alternative shapes that avoid void.

Core Idea
A non-spherical muhaddid can still move without creating void.

The second argument claims that a non-spherical muhaddid moving on a sphere would leave gaps, implying void. The refutation shows that if the concave is spherical and the convex is oval or lenticular moving on the appropriate axis, no void arises. Let us examine this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument
The argument states that if the muhaddid were not spherical, its motion on a spherical body would leave empty gaps (void) because the corners would not be filled.
Note the key assumption: motion on a spherical body with a non-spherical shape must create void.
2
Identify the refutation
The refutation offers two counterexamples: concave spherical and convex oval moving on its longest diameter, or concave spherical and convex lenticular moving on its shortest diameter. In both cases, no void results.
These shapes are specifically designed to fit without gaps.
3
Appreciate the logical point
The refutation shows that the original claim is not necessary; it is possible to have non-spherical shapes that move without void, thus invalidating the argument.
This is a standard method of refutation by counterexample.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the second of them: that if it were not spherical, its motion, especially upon a spherical body, would necessitate the occurrence of void, since there is nothing filling the gaps of the corners. It is refuted by that if one supposes its concave spherical, and its convex oval, moving on its longest diameter, or lenticular, moving on its shortest diameter, void does not result.

Commentary
This passage refutes the second argument for sphericity: that if the muhaddid were not spherical, its motion on a spherical body would create empty gaps (void). The refutation supposes a scenario where the concave surface is spherical, but the convex is oval (moving on its longest axis) or lenticular (moving on its shortest axis). In such cases, the motion does not produce void because the shapes can fit without gaps. Thus the argument fails.

Objection on Uniform Nature of the Muhaddid and Answer
Responds to the objection that if the muhaddid has one nature, its convex must be spherical like its concave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of a non-spherical muhaddid by defining oval and lenticular shapes and by arguing that a non-encompassing body cannot determine all directions.

Core Idea
The muhaddid's uniform nature does not force sphericity; convex and concave can differ.

This lesson tackles a natural objection to the concept of the muhaddid: if its nature is one, why should its convex be spherical like its concave? The author dismisses the objection as an unfounded assumption and then clarifies the definitions of oval and lenticular shapes, showing that different curvatures are possible. Finally, he argues that the muhaddid must encompass directions, otherwise it would only determine nearness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Carefully read the objection that the convex must be spherical because the nature is one, and the author's brief reply that this claim is a self-standing assertion not dependent on motion or void.
Notice that the author does not reject that the nature is one; he rejects the conclusion of sphericity.
2
Learn the definitions
Read the definitions of the oval (bayḍī) and lenticular (ʿadasī) shapes. Understand how each is bounded by arcs of circles.
Oval: arcs smaller than a semicircle; lenticular: arcs larger.
3
Understand rotation into solids
The passage states that each shape, when rotated about itself, produces a solid. Consider how a two-dimensional shape revolving gives a three-dimensional body.
This shows that different curvatures can yield different solids, not necessarily spheres.
4
Grasp the encompassing argument
The author concludes that the muhaddid must encompass directions; otherwise it would only determine nearness to itself, contradicting its function.
The final 'خلف' indicates a contradiction if the muhaddid were not encompassing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : طبيعة المحدد واحدة لما سيجيء، فيكون محدبه مستديرا كمقعره . قلنا : فيكون ذلك استدلالاً برأسه لا يفتقر إلى ذكر الحركة، ولزوم الخلاء ، والشكل البيضي سطح يحيط به قوسان متساویتان، كل منهما أصغر من دائرة. والعدسي ما هما أعظم. وكل منهما إذا أدير على نفسه حصل مجسمه، وأما كون المحدد محيطاً بذوات الجهات، فلأن غير المحيط إنما يتحدد به القرب منه وهو ظاهر، فلا يكون محدداً للجهتين. هذا خلف.
For it is said: The nature of the muhaddid is one, as will come later, so its convex should be spherical like its concave. We say: That would be an argument from its own head, not needing to mention motion and the occurrence of void. The oval shape is a surface bounded by two equal arcs, each smaller than a circle. The lenticular is that which both are larger. And each of them, when rotated about itself, yields its solid. As for the muhaddid encompassing things with directions, it is because the non-encompassing only determines nearness to it, which is obvious, so it does not determine both directions. That is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that since the muhaddid has a single nature (as will be explained later), its convex must be spherical like its concave. The author replies that this claim is a self-standing assertion that does not rely on arguments about motion or void; it simply assumes sphericity without proof. The author then clarifies the definitions: an oval (bayḍī) is bounded by two equal arcs each smaller than a semicircle, and a lenticular (ʿadasī) has arcs larger than a semicircle; rotating these shapes produces solids. Finally, the author argues that if the muhaddid were non-encompassing, it would only determine nearness, not both directions—which contradicts its role as a determinant of all directions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The muhaddid must be uniformly spherical because its nature is one.
Correction: The author shows that spherical shape is not necessary; oval and lenticular shapes are possible, and the muhaddid must encompass directions.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that the muhaddid's convex must be spherical like its concave?
A) By agreeing and providing additional proof.
B) By stating that such an assertion is independent and does not rely on motion or void.
C) By denying that the nature is one.
D) By saying that the concave is not spherical.

Key Takeaway
The muhaddid's uniform nature does not force sphericity; it can have different convex and concave curvatures as long as it encompasses all directions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector assumed that a single nature forces uniform shape. Have you ever assumed that God's attributes must fit a certain human mold, limiting your trust in His infinite wisdom? What specific expectation will you surrender to Him this week?
?
The muhaddid encompasses every direction, leaving nothing ungoverned. Is there any area of your life—your finances, relationships, or thoughts—that you have kept outside the encompassment of divine guidance? What one step will you take today to bring it under His direction?
?
If the muhaddid were not encompassing, it would fail to determine all directions. Reflect: What partial boundaries in your life—like incomplete repentance or selective obedience—limit your spiritual direction? What will you do to make your devotion encompassing?

The Definition and First Proof of Istihala
Sanusi defines istihala and gives the first proof from gradual change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of istihala (transformation) and the argument that it is a gradual change in quality.

Core Idea
Istihala is change in quality, not in the substantial form.

Sanusi begins by establishing what istihala truly means: a progressive transformation of qualities over time. He argues that since forms come into existence instantaneously, any gradual change must occur in the qualities of the matter. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the logical flow.
2
Identify the definition of istihala
Find where Sanusi explicitly defines istihala as change of quality with persistence of form.
3
Understand the first proof
Trace the argument: a new occurrence requires time, so change must be gradual, and cannot be in the instantaneous form, therefore it is in quality.
Note the distinction between form (instantaneous) and quality (gradual).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحدوث أخرى إنما يكون عند تمام استعداد المادة وهو أمر حادث يفتقر إلى زمان، فلا بد من تغير واقع على التدريج، وليس ذلك في نفس الصورة لأن وجودها وعدمها دفعي . فتعين أن يكون في الكيفية بأن تتغير فتضعف الكيفية التي تناسب الصورة التي تفسد وتشتد إلى تناسب الصورة التي تكون ولا نعني بالاستحالة إلا تغير الكيفية مع بقاء الصورة وما ذكر في المتن استدلال على ثبوت الاستحالة بوجه آخر، وهو أنه لما ثبت فيضان الصور والنفوس لزم حدوث كيفية متوسطة متشابهة في جميع أجزاء الممتزج، لتستعد المادة بذلك لقبولها، وهذا نفس الاستحالة. أعني زوال الكيفيات الصرفة في الأجزاء العنصرية وحصول الكيفية المتوسطة وفيه نظر لجواز أن يحصل الاستعداد بمجرد اختلاط الأجزاء المتصغرة بحيث تحصل الكيفية المتوسطة بحسب الحس مع كون كل من الأجزاء البسيطة على صرافة كيفيتها.
And another occurrence is only at the completion of the matter's preparation, and it is a temporal event that requires time, so there must be a change occurring gradually. And that is not in the form itself because its existence and non-existence are instantaneous. So it is determined that it is in the quality, such that the quality that suits the form that corrupts weakens and the quality that suits the form that comes to be intensifies. And we mean by istihāla (transformation) only the change of quality with the persistence of the form. And what is mentioned in the text is a proof for the establishment of istihāla in another way, namely that since the emanation of forms and souls is established, it necessitates the occurrence of an intermediate quality similar in all parts of the mixture, so that the matter becomes prepared thereby to receive them, and this is istihāla itself. I mean the removal of the pure qualities in the elemental parts and the attainment of the intermediate quality. And there is a consideration in it because of the possibility that the preparation occurs merely by the mixing of the minute parts such that the intermediate quality is obtained according to sense perception while each of the simple parts remains in the purity of its quality.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a proof of istihāla (transformation) followed by an objection. The author first argues that a new occurrence (ḥudūth) of a form requires a gradual change because the matter must be prepared over time, and this change cannot be in the substantial form (ṣūra) itself (since forms come to be instantaneously), so it must be in the quality (kayfiyya). Thus, istihāla is defined as the change of quality while the form remains. Then he presents a second proof from the emanation of forms: since forms emanate, there must be an intermediate quality in the mixture to prepare the matter, which is precisely istihāla. However, he raises an objection: it is possible that the preparation occurs simply by the mixing of minute parts, and the intermediate quality is perceived without any real transformation of the elemental qualities, as the simple parts remain pure. The author does not immediately answer this objection here.

Key Terms
استحالة
كيفية
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Istihala involves a change in the substantial form itself.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly defines istihala as change in quality only, with the substantial form persisting unchanged.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can the gradual change not occur in the form itself?
A) Because forms are eternal
B) Because forms are created instantaneously
C) Because forms are made of matter
D) Because forms are not subject to change

Key Takeaway
Istihala is defined as a gradual change in quality while the substantial form remains, and this is proven by the need for temporal preparation before new forms emerge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a fault in your character that you have tried to change for years. In light of this lesson, do you see that fault as a 'quality' (something gradual to improve) or as part of your 'form' (something you think you can never change)? What does that tell you about your true belief in Allah's power to transform you over time?
?
The lesson emphasizes that substantial change (form) happens in an instant, but quality transformation is gradual. Is there an area of your faith—like certainty (yaqīn) or sincerity (ikhlāṣ)—that you have been expecting to perfect overnight? How might this lesson correct that expectation and encourage you to walk the path of gradual growth?
?
If you truly believe that your core self (soul, fitra) is fixed by Allah, but your actions and habits (qualities) are meant to be shaped gradually, what specific quality will you start working on today with patience and trust in the One who brings about the new form in His time?

The Second Proof of Istihala: From the Emanation of Forms
Sanusi presents the second proof of istihala from the emanation of forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof of istihala based on the emanation of forms and the necessity of an intermediate quality in the mixture.

Core Idea
The emanation of forms requires an intermediate quality to prepare the matter.

Sanusi presents an additional argument for istihala, drawing on the metaphysical principle that forms and souls emanate from a higher cause. For matter to receive these forms, it must acquire a mediating quality that is uniform across the mixture. This lesson explores that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second proof carefully.
2
Understand the premise
The emanation of forms and souls is established (from prior discussions).
3
Identify the necessity
The emanation necessitates an intermediate quality in the mixture to make matter receptive.
This intermediate quality is precisely istihala.

?

An Objection to the Second Proof of Istihala
Sanusi raises an objection to the second proof of istihala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a potential objection to the second proof: that preparation could occur simply by mixing minute parts without real qualitative change.

Core Idea
The intermediate quality might be merely apparent, not real.

Sanusi does not present the second proof without critique. He raises an important objection: perhaps the preparation needed for receiving forms is achieved simply by the mechanical mixing of small particles, resulting in a perceived intermediate quality while each simple part retains its pure quality. This lesson examines that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation.
2
Understand the objection
The objection says that the intermediate quality might be only perceptual, arising from mixture, not a real transformation.
3
Reflect on implications
If true, this challenges the second proof, because istihala as real change would not be necessary.
Sanusi does not answer here, but leaves it as a consideration.

Similarity Does Not Imply True Moderation
Refutes claim that similarity implies moderation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of significant change does not automatically indicate nearness to true moderation, and how this logical distinction resolves a dispute about climatic balance.

Core Idea
Lack of change is not the same as being close to the mean.

This lesson examines a concise refutation from Sharh al-Maqasid. The author responds to an objection that might claim regions with stable conditions are closer to ideal moderation. He clarifies that mere similarity (absence of significant change) does not prove true moderation, which is a key distinction in the dispute about the most balanced clime.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice that it is a single sentence that contains both the objection and the refutation.
The phrase 'على ما هو المتنازع' indicates this answer is directed specifically at the point under dispute.
2
Understand the objection being refuted
The objection likely argued that regions with stable, unchanging conditions (similarity) are close to true moderation. Identify why someone might think that.
Stability can be mistaken for balance, but the author rejects this inference.
3
Analyze the logical distinction
The author distinguishes between similarity (no significant change) and true moderation. Realize that absence of extremes does not automatically mean closeness to the perfect mean.
Think of a lukewarm bath: constant temperature doesn't mean it's perfectly comfortable for everyone.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider how this logical point applies beyond geography—in ethics, theology, or everyday reasoning. Not every stable condition is moderate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن التشابه بمعنى عدم طريان تغير معتد به لا يفيد القرب من الاعتدال الحقيقي على ما هو المتنازع .
And the answer: that similarity in the sense of the absence of any significant change does not indicate nearness to true moderation, concerning that which is disputed.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a brief refutation. The author responds to an unnamed objection by drawing a logical distinction. The objection likely claimed that certain regions (perhaps the equator) are similar to the ideal because they lack extremes. The author counters that mere similarity of conditions—specifically, the absence of any notable change—does not actually prove that a region is close to true moderation (al-iʿtidāl al-ḥaqīqī). The phrase 'على ما هو المتنازع' indicates that this response is directed at the specific point under dispute, which in context is the claim about which clime is most balanced. Thus, the author rejects the inference that lack of variation equals closeness to the mean.

Key Terms
تشابه
اعتدال حقيقي
تغير معتد به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Stability or lack of change automatically implies moderation.
Correction: The author points out that true moderation requires more than just absence of variation; similarity in the sense of no significant change does not prove nearness to the true mean.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what does 'similarity' (al-tashābuh) not indicate?
A) The presence of significant change
B) Nearness to true moderation
C) The length of the day
D) The size of the clime

Key Takeaway
Similarity (absence of significant change) is not evidence for true moderation; the two concepts are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you have been consistent for a long time. Have you ever assumed that this consistency makes you balanced—without checking whether you are actually close to the true mean (the ideal)? What significant change might you be ignoring because it would disrupt your sense of stability? Will you examine that today?
?
When you look at your spiritual practice, do you value stability over accuracy? Are you more concerned with not changing than with being truly moderate in the sight of Allah? What would it cost you to pursue true moderation rather than mere similarity?
?
Consider a belief or habit you hold because 'it has always been this way.' Could it be that you are mistaking lack of change for righteousness? How can you test whether your position is actually near the point of true balance, and are you ready to move if it is not?

Why the Human Temperament Is the Most Balanced
Taftazani argues that human temperament is most balanced, giving two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main reasons the human temperament is considered the most balanced mixture of all species according to Taftazani.

Core Idea
Human balance reflects the nobility of the rational soul and the need for precise, opposing actions.

In this passage, Taftazani presents a consensus that the human mixture is closest to true moderation. He defends this claim with two interconnected reasons: one based on the nobility of the rational soul, and another on the practical needs of human actions. This argument weaves together metaphysical and physiological considerations to explain human exceptionalism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the compact style and the logical connectors indicating reasons.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage gives two reasons: (1) because the human mixture is the substrate of the rational soul, and (2) because humans need precise actions requiring balanced qualities. Find the phrases that introduce each reason.
The first reason uses 'لأنه متعلق النفس الناطقة' and the second 'ولأنه أحوج الأنواع'.
3
Understand the first reason
The rational soul is the noblest. The mixture must be noblest, meaning closest to true unity and farthest from opposition and multiplicity. How does that imply balance?
4
Understand the second reason
Human actions need precision, requiring a balance of heat, cold, dryness, and moisture. Each quality aids specific functions: heat for digestion, cold for retention, dryness for preservation, moisture for perception.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how both reasons together support the claim that human temperament is the most balanced. The first reason ties balance to nobility, the second to functionality.
This shows balance is both metaphysical and practical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد اتفقوا على أنه إذا اعتبرت الأنواع كان أعدل الأمزجة أي أقربها إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي، مزاج نوع الإنسان لأنه متعلق النفس الناطقة الأشرف، فلا بد أن يكون أشرف أي أقرب إلى الوحدة الحقيقية وأبعد عن التضاد والكثرة، ولأنه أحوج الأنواع إلى الأفعال المتقنة التي تعين على بعضها الحرارة كالهضم، وعلى بعضها البرودة كالإمساك، وعلى بعضها اليبوسة كالحفظ، وعلى بعضها الرطوبة كالإدراك.
They have agreed that when species are considered, the most balanced of mixtures—i.e., the closest to true moderation—is the mixture of the human species, because it is the substrate of the noblest rational soul. Hence it must be noblest, i.e., closest to true unity and farthest from opposition and multiplicity, and because it is the most in need of precise actions, some of which require heat, like digestion, others cold, like retention, others dryness, like preservation, and others moisture, like perception.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing that the human temperament is the most balanced of all species. The author begins by noting a consensus: when considering different kinds of beings, the most balanced mixture (mizāj) is that of the human species. The reason is that this mixture is the substrate (muta‘alliq) of the rational soul, which is the noblest soul. Consequently, it must itself be noblest, meaning closest to true unity and furthest from opposition and multiplicity. A second reason is that humans are the most in need of precise actions; these actions require a balance of qualities: heat helps digestion, cold helps retention, dryness helps preservation, and moisture helps perception.

Key Terms
مزاج (mizāj)
اعتدال (i‘tidāl)
نفس ناطقة (nafs nāṭiqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Balance here is merely physical or biological, without connection to the soul.
Correction: The passage explicitly ties the balance of the human temperament to the nobility of the rational soul and the functional needs of precise actions, showing it is both metaphysical and practical.

Checkpoint
Why is the human temperament considered the most balanced according to this passage?
A) Because humans live in the most moderate climate.
B) Because it is the substrate of the rational soul and requires precise actions.
C) Because humans are the most numerous species.
D) Because humans have the healthiest diet.

Key Takeaway
The human temperament is the most balanced because it serves the rational soul and requires a harmony of opposing qualities for precise actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your temperament is balanced precisely for actions that please Allah? Which of the four qualities (heat, cold, dryness, moisture) is most out of balance in your daily life, and how does that affect your ability to perform acts of worship with precision?
?
If your body's mixture is the most balanced substrate for the rational soul, how are you using your intellect to align your actions with that nobility? What specific precise action (like remembering Allah, controlling anger, preserving knowledge, perceiving truth) are you neglecting?
?
The passage says human mixture is closest to unity and farthest from opposition. Where in your life do you experience internal opposition (e.g., desires vs. duty)? How does that contradict the intended balance of your nature, and what will you commit to do to restore that balance?
