Ibn Sina's Argument on Equatorial Inhabitants
Ibn Sina presents astronomical reasons for equatorial climatic balance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's astronomical argument that equatorial inhabitants have the most balanced climate.

Core Idea
Balance is found where extremes cancel out.

This passage presents Ibn Sina's distinctive view on which human group is most balanced in constitution, based on celestial geometry. Before reading his argument, consider what 'balanced climate' might mean and why it matters for human temperament. Ibn Sina uses detailed astronomical reasoning to support his claim that equatorial regions enjoy perpetual moderation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing for analysis. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to astronomical terms like 'equinox' and 'declination'.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'اعتدالان' (equinoxes) and 'انقلابان' (solstices).
2
Identify the reasons
Ibn Sina gives several reasons for equatorial balance. List them as you find them: equal day/night, mild summer, mild winter, short seasons.
Each reason is linked to a specific celestial motion.
3
Reflect on the argument
Ask yourself: Why does Ibn Sina think constant spring-like conditions make a group 'most balanced'? How does this connect to broader discussions of human nature?
This argument relates to the idea of 'iʿtidāl' (balance) as a desirable trait.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في أعدل الأصناف بالنظر إلى أوضاع العلويات . فقال ابن سينا: سكان خط الاستواء، أي الموضع الموازي لمعدل النهار، وذلك لتشابه أحوالهم في الحر والبرد، لتساوي ليلهم ونهارهم دائماً ، ولأنه ليس صيفهم شديد الحر، لأن الشمس تزول عن سمت رأسهم بسرعة ، لما تقرر في موضعه من أن حركتها في الميل. أعني البعد عن معدل النهار أسرع عند الاعتدالين، وأبطأ عند الانقلابين ولا شتاؤهم شديد البرد ، لأن الشمس لا تبعد عن سمتهم كثيراً، فلا يعظم التفاوت بين صيفه وشتائه . ومع ذلك فمدة كل منهما قصيرة، وهي شهر ونصف كما مر من كون الفصول هناك ثمانية. فالشمس لا تسامتهم عن بعد كثير، بل عن قرب من المسامتة، فهم دائماً منتقلون من حالة متوسطة إلى ما يشابهها، فكأنهم في الربيع دائماً.
And they differed concerning the most balanced of races with respect to celestial configurations. Ibn Sina said: the inhabitants of the equator, i.e., the location parallel to the equinoctial line, due to the similarity of their conditions in heat and cold, because their day and night are always equal, and because their summer is not intensely hot—for the sun departs from their zenith quickly, because it is established in its place that its motion in declination (i.e., the distance from the equinoctial line) is faster at the equinoxes and slower at the solstices—and their winter is not intensely cold, because the sun does not depart far from their zenith. Hence the difference between summer and winter is not great. Moreover, the duration of each is short, being a month and a half, as has passed regarding the eight seasons there. The sun does not distance itself from them far, but is near the zenith, so they are constantly moving from a moderate state to that which resembles it, as if they are always in spring.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's view on which race or grouping of people is most balanced, a question that follows the previous discussion on species. Considering celestial positions (auḍā‘ al-‘ulwīyāt), Ibn Sina identifies the inhabitants of the equator as the most balanced. He provides several astronomical reasons: their days and nights are always equal; summer is not extremely hot because the sun moves away from the zenith quickly (since the sun's motion in declination is fastest at the equinoxes, and the equator sees the equinoxes often); winter is not extremely cold because the sun never goes far from the zenith; each season lasts only a month and a half, and transition is constant, so they are perpetually in a spring-like state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: That the equator has constant extreme heat.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues the equator is actually moderate due to the sun's rapid movement away from the zenith and short seasons.

First Objection to Ibn Sina and Its Answer
Objection and response regarding equatorial heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against Ibn Sina's claim about moderate equatorial heat and its refutation based on the duration of solar proximity.

Core Idea
Proximity without duration does not guarantee intense heat.

This lesson presents a key debate in Islamic natural philosophy about heat distribution. The objector argues that equatorial regions should be extremely hot due to the sun's regular zenith passage. But the response reveals a subtle factor: the fleeting nature of that proximity compared to the sustained near-zenith days in temperate zones. Understanding this exchange sharpens our grasp of how cause and effect are weighed in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the objection and answer unfold in the author's own order.
2
Identify the objection's core claim
The objector says equatorial regions should be extremely hot because the sun is near zenith twice a year and never goes far away. Notice the additional point about winter cold making air receptive.
3
Analyze the answer's key distinction
The answer introduces duration as a factor: the sun passes zenith quickly at the equator, while in higher latitudes it remains near zenith for many days with longer days. This sustained proximity causes more heating.
The analogy with iron in fire illustrates the principle.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The answer compares a constant weak cause to a strong intermittent one. Iron in a gentle fire for a long time becomes hotter than iron in a strong fire for a moment. This clarifies why sustained proximity matters more than brief intensity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل بعضهم على فساد هذا الرأي بوجهين : أحدهما: أن الشمس تسامت رءوسهم في السنة مرتين، ثم لا تبعد عن المسامتة بأكثر من ثلاثة وعشرين جزءاً ونصفاً، على ما هو غاية الميل الكلي، فهم دائماً في المسامتة أو في القرب منها ، فتكون حرارتهم مفرطة، لأن قرب المسامتة في زمان يسير كما في الصيف عندنا ، مع تقدم برد الشتاء المخرج للهواء عن استعداد التسخن مسخن جداً فهذا أولى. وجوابه : أن مسامتتهم لسرعة زوالها أقل نكاية وتسخيناً للهواء من المسامتة، أو القرب منها في البلاد ذوات العروض، لأن قرب المسامتة يبقى هناك أياماً كثيرة، ويكون النهار أطول من الليل طولاً ظاهراً. والسبب الدائم وإن ضعف، قد يكون أكثر تأثيراً من غير الدائم، وإن قوى كالحديد في نار لينة مدة، وفي نار قوية لحظة .
And some argued against the soundness of this view with two points. The first: that the sun is directly above their heads twice a year, and then does not depart from the zenith by more than twenty-three and a half degrees, which is the maximum total declination. Thus they are always at the zenith or near it, so their heat is excessive, because proximity to the zenith in a short time, as in summer with us, combined with the preceding winter cold which removes the air's readiness to be heated, becomes extremely heating—so this is more plausible. And its answer: that their being at the zenith, due to the speed of its departure, is less harmful and less heating of the air than being at the zenith or near it in lands with latitude, because near the zenith remains there for many days, and the day becomes visibly longer than the night. And the constant cause, even if weak, may be more effective than the non-constant, even if strong, like iron in a gentle fire for a duration versus in a strong fire for a moment.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two objections against Ibn Sina's claim that equatorial inhabitants have moderate heat, along with the response. The objector argues that twice a year the sun is directly overhead, and never goes more than 23.5° away, so equatorial people are always near the zenith, causing extreme heat. Moreover, this proximity lasts only a short time (like the summer we experience), but the preceding winter cold makes the air more receptive to heating. The answer counters that because the sun passes the zenith quickly at the equator, its heating effect is less than in higher latitudes where the sun remains near the zenith for many consecutive days with longer days. A constant but weak cause can be more effective than a strong but intermittent one, just as iron in a gentle fire for a prolonged time becomes hotter than iron in a strong fire for an instant.

Key Terms
مسامتة (musāmata)
ميل كلي (mayl kullī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Proximity to the zenith always causes intense heat.
Correction: Duration matters more than intensity; brief proximity at equator is less heating than sustained near-zenith in higher latitudes.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the first objection?
A) The equator never experiences direct sunlight.
B) The sun's proximity is brief at the equator, while in higher latitudes it lasts longer and thus heats more.
C) The winter at the equator is longer, reducing heat.
D) The sun's rays are weaker at the equator.

Key Takeaway
The heating effect of the sun depends not only on proximity but also on duration, as shown by the counterexample of equatorial zenith passage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your spiritual life, do you rely more on occasional intense acts of worship or on consistent small deeds? Reflect on the analogy of iron in a gentle fire versus a strong fire for a moment. ?
The objector assumed that proximity to the sun's zenith always causes extreme heat, but he overlooked the speed of its passage. Identify one such assumption and decide how you will change your approach.

Second Objection to Ibn Sina and Its Answer
Second objection: equatorial winter and high-latitude summer share equal zenith distance; answer denies heat parity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against Ibn Sina's theory of summer heat based on the comparison between equatorial winter and high-latitude summer, and how the beginning of the answer challenges the assumption of identical causes.

Core Idea
Equal solar distance does not guarantee equal temperature—other factors intervene.

Sanusi presents the second objection: when the sun is equally distant from the zenith of the equator and a high-latitude city at opposite seasons, their temperatures would be equal if solar distance were the only factor. The objector even suggests the equator's winter might be hotter due to prior heat. The answer begins by denying the parity, stating that the cause of heat is not confined to this single factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Examine the astronomical scenario: the sun entering Cancer (summer solstice) causes winter at the equator and summer at latitude 47° (e.g., Sarai). Note the specific distances from the zenith.
The key point is that the zenith distance is equal for both locations at this moment.
2
Understand the logical structure
The objector argues that equal zenith distance implies equal heat, and even suggests more heat for the equator due to prior causes of warmth.
This is a conditional argument: if cause A leads to effect B, then same A gives same B.
3
Grasp the beginning of the answer
The answer denies the similarity of heat between the two seasons and locations. It claims that the objection would only work if the cause of heat were limited to zenith distance alone.
The answer is incomplete here; it likely continues with other causal factors.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does this objection matter for Ibn Sina's broader theory? It challenges a key assumption about the cause of seasons.
This connects to debates about natural causation and rational judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما أن زمان وصول الشمس إلى أول السرطان شتاء لخط الاستواء لكون الشمس على غاية البعد عن سمت رأسهم، وصيف البقعة عرضها سبعة وأربعون ضعف الميل الكلي كبلدة سراي لكونها على غاية القرب عنها مع أن بعدها عن سمت رأس البقعتين على السواء، فيكون حر شتاء خط الاستواء كحرّ صيف هذه البلدة، بل أكثر إذا تأملت لأن ما قبل هذه الحالة لهم من أسباب السخونة، ولأهل البلدة من أسباب البرودة، وإذا كان حر شتائهم هذا، فما ظنك بحر صيفهم . وجوابه : منع تشابه حر الفصلين في البقعتين، وإنما يلزم لو انحصر سبب الحرّ
And the second: that the time of the sun's arrival at the beginning of Cancer is winter for the equator because the sun is at the furthest distance from their zenith, and summer for a locality whose latitude is forty-seven, double the total declination, like the city of Sarai, because it is at the extreme proximity to it, even though the distance from the zenith of both localities is equal. Hence the heat of the equator's winter would be like the heat of that city's summer, indeed more if you reflect, because before this state the equatorial inhabitants have causes of heat, while the people of that city have causes of cold. And if such is the heat of their winter, what do you think of their summer? And its answer: denial of the similarity of the heat of the two seasons in the two localities. This would only follow if the cause of heat were confined.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against Ibn Sina and its beginning of the answer. The objector uses a specific example: when the sun enters Cancer (summer solstice), it is winter for the equator (since the sun is furthest away) but summer for a location at latitude 47°, like the city of Sarai (which is at 2 × 23.5°). The distance from the zenith is the same for both, so the heat at the equator in winter would equal the heat at Sarai in summer; perhaps even more, since the equatorial climate had prior causes of heat, while Sarai had prior cold. The answer begins by denying the assumption that the heat of the two seasons in the two locations is similar. The objection would hold only if the cause of heat were limited to that single factor (distance from zenith), but other factors differ.

Key Terms
سرطان (saraṭān)
بعد عن السمت (bu‘d ‘an al-samt)
سبب الحر (sabab al-ḥarr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that identical solar zenith distance yields identical temperature at different locations and seasons.
Correction: Temperature depends on multiple factors, including prior climatic conditions and the history of heating and cooling, not just instantaneous solar distance.

Checkpoint
What is the core point of the second objection?
A) The equator has never been visited by the sun.
B) At a certain time, the equatorial winter and a high-latitude summer have the same solar distance, so their temperatures should be equal.
C) The equator is always colder than high latitudes.
D) The sun's motion is slower at the equator.

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that if temperature depended solely on solar distance, then equatorial winter and high-latitude summer would have equal heat, but the answer denies this by pointing to additional causal factors beyond zenith distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Common Sensibles Are Perceived by External Senses
al-Taftazani explains that common sensibles are not perceived by a separate sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that common sensibles like magnitude and number are not perceived by a distinct internal sense but by the external senses, sometimes aided by reasoning.

Core Idea
Common sensibles are not sensed by a separate 'common sense'; the external senses do it.

You might think that perceiving the size, shape, or motion of an object requires a special internal sense. But classical scholars like al-Taftazani argue otherwise. In this lesson, we'll see why common sensibles are actually perceived by the same external senses we use for color, sound, and other qualities, though sometimes with a little help from reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its flow and key terms like 'المحسوسات المشتركة' and 'المقادير'. Don't worry about understanding every word yet.
Notice the list of examples – they are all qualities that can be perceived by more than one sense.
2
Understand the misconception
The passage begins by stating that common sensibles are not, as some suppose, perceived by another sense. Identify that misconception and note why it might seem plausible.
The mistaken view is that there is a separate 'common sense' organ for these qualities.
3
Grasp the correct position
Al-Taftazani affirms that perception of common sensibles is only by the external senses. However, he adds that sometimes one sense helps another or analogical reasoning and intellection are involved. Understand this nuance.
He doesn't deny that reasoning plays a role, but the primary perception comes via external senses.
4
Review the examples
Go through the list: magnitudes, numbers, positions, motions, rests, shapes, proximity, distance, contact. Think of concrete examples for each and which external sense(s) might perceive them.
For instance, you can see and touch shape; you can see and hear motion (if sound changes).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المحسوسات المشتركة، مثل المقادير والأعداد والأوضاع والحركات والسكنات، والأشكال، والقرب، والبعد والمماسة، ونحو ذلك . فليست كما يظن أن مدركها حس آخر، بل إدراكها، إنما هو بالحواس الظاهرة، وإن كان بعضها قد يستعين بالبعض، أو بضرب من القياس والتعقل .

Rather their perception is only by the external senses, although some of them may seek assistance from others, or by a kind of analogical reasoning and intellection.

This passage corrects a misconception about common sensibles (al-mahsusat al-mushtaraka). Some might think that qualities like size, number, position, motion, etc., require a separate sense. The author states they are perceived by the external senses, although sometimes one sense helps another or reasoning is involved. He lists examples: magnitudes, numbers, positions, motions, rests, shapes, proximity, distance, contact.

Critique of the Basis for Multiplicity of Touch
Author critiques basis for touch multiplicity and raises a new objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of the argument that diversity of opposition in tangible objects necessitates multiple senses, and his objection that opposition itself is an intellectual concept, not directly perceived.

Core Idea
Opposites are sensed, but opposition is intellectual—so why base multiplicity on types of opposition?

In this passage, the author turns a critical eye on the philosophers' reasoning for claiming that touch has multiple powers due to diverse types of opposition. He dissects their argument, dismisses a weak supporting claim as 'foolish speech,' and then raises a powerful new objection: what the senses perceive are the opposites themselves, not the abstract relation of opposition. This challenges the very foundation of the multiplicity claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping to analyze. Then read the translation to get the full argument.
2
Identify the philosophers' position and the two critiques
Locate the opening statement that the philosophers require a single power for each type of opposition. Then find where the author examines the basis for diversity of opposition in tangible objects.
The two critiques: one related to the objects causing relations, the other to opposition itself.
3
Understand the dismissed argument
Notice what the author labels as 'foolish speech' — that primary qualities have greater disparity than secondary ones, justifying multiplicity only for touch.
Why does the author dismiss this? Think about consistency with other senses.
4
Grasp the new investigation
Read the final part where the author states that the sensed are the opposites (e.g., hot and cold), not the opposition itself (which is intellectual). Reflect on why this undermines the philosophers' basis.
The example of vision perceiving both black and white with a single power is key.
5
Summarize the overall argument
In your own words, explain how the author challenges the multiplicity of touch. What is his strongest point?

والحكماء أوجبوا أن يكون الحاكم على كل نوع من أنواع التضاد قوة واحدة تسمى بالشعور والتمييز. وأنت خبير بأن دعوى تنوع التضاد في الملموسات إن كانت من جهة أن تنوع المعروضات يوجب تنوع الإضافات العارضة، فالكل سواء. وإن كانت بالنظر إلى نفس التضاد العارض ، فلا يتم بدون برهان ،وتفرقة، ومن سخيف الكلام ما قيل أن تباين الكيفيات الأول. أعني الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة، أشد من تباين الكيفيات الثواني الحادثة من تفاعلها كالروايح والألوان والطعوم، فلذلك تعددت قوى اللمس، دون باقي الحواس . : وههنا بحث آخر، وهو أن المدرك بالحس هو المتضادات كالحرارة والبرودة دون التضاد، فإنه من المعاني العقلية، فكيف جعلوا مبنى تعدد اللامسة، تعدد أنواع التضاد، وجوزوا إدراك القوة الواحدة للمدركات المتضادة، كالباصرة للسواد والبياض . ولم يجعلوا ذلك أفعالاً مختلفة من مبدأ واحد بالذات والاعتبار.
The philosophers required that the judge over each type of opposition be a single power called sensation and discrimination. And you are aware that the claim of diversity of opposition in tangible things, if it is due to the diversity of the objects causing diversity of the accidental relations, then all are the same. And if it is with respect to the opposition itself that is accidental, then it is not complete without a proof and a distinction. And it is from foolish speech what was said that the disparity of the primary qualities—namely heat, cold, wetness, and dryness—is stronger than the disparity of the secondary qualities arising from their interaction, such as odors, colors, and tastes, and therefore the powers of touch are multiplied without the rest of the senses. And here is another investigation: namely, that what is perceived by sense is the opposites, like heat and cold, not the opposition, for it is among the intellectual meanings. So how did they make the basis for the multiplicity of the sense of touch the multiplicity of types of opposition, and they allowed a single power to perceive opposite objects, like the eye for black and white? And they did not consider that to be different acts from a single principle in essence and consideration.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the basis for the multiplicity of the sense of touch. The author first states the position of the philosophers (al-Ḥukamāʾ) that there must be a single power over each type of opposition, called sensation and discrimination. Then he examines the claim that tangible objects involve diverse types of opposition. He argues that if the diversity is due to the variety of objects causing accidental relations, then all senses are the same in that regard. But if the diversity is in the opposition itself, then the claim requires proof and distinction. He dismisses as foolish the view that primary qualities (heat, cold, wetness, dryness) have greater disparity than secondary qualities (odors, colors, tastes), and that this justifies the multiplicity of touch alone. Then he raises a new investigation: what is perceived by the senses are the opposites themselves (like heat and cold), not the opposition itself, which is an intellectual concept. So how can they base the multiplicity of touch on types of opposition, when the same power (e.g., vision) can perceive opposite objects (black and white) without requiring multiple powers? Why do they not consider that as different acts of a single principle? This objection challenges the previous argument that multiplicity of types of opposition necessitates multiple senses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That diversity of opposition in tangible objects necessarily implies multiple senses, as the philosophers claim.
Correction: The author shows that a single sense (like vision) can perceive opposites (black and white), so basing multiplicity on types of opposition is invalid. Moreover, opposition itself is an intellectual concept, not sensed.

Checkpoint
What is the 'another investigation' (بحث آخر) raised in this passage?
A) That touch is actually a single sense
B) That the perceived is the opposites, not the opposition, so basing multiplicity on types of opposition is problematic
C) That hearing should also be multiple
D) That taste has multiple oppositions

Key Takeaway
The author argues that since opposition is an intellectual concept and a single sense can perceive opposites, the philosophers' basis for multiplying the sense of touch is unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sense heat and cold, do you ever stop to realize that the classification of 'opposition' is your mind's construction, not a direct sensation? How does this affect your trust in your own sensory judgments? Will you today pause before assuming your perceptions are raw facts?
?
The philosophers exaggerated the disparity between primary and secondary qualities to justify their theory. Think of an opinion you strongly hold—is there any chance you have exaggerated the differences between your view and opposing views to avoid having to prove your position? Will you honestly re-examine that belief this week?
?
The author calls an argument 'foolish speech' when it lacks proof. Is there any belief you currently hold that you have never subjected to rigorous proof? What would it take for you to either find that proof or let go of the belief?
